<a id="paper-2605.28085"></a>
### ⭐ [Environment-Enhanced Single-Photon Absorption in a Nano-Ring of Dipole-Coupled Quantum Emitters](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28085v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Helmut Ritsch  
**Authors:** Eric Sánchez-Llorente, Helmut Ritsch, Maria Moreno-Cardoner  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28085v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Dicke superradiance` **4/5** · 🔥 `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **4/5** · 🔥 `free-space correlated emission` **4/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28085_figures/2605.28085_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Schematic of the system. A nanoring of three-level quantum emitters (|g⟩, |e⟩and |t⟩) with radius R and interparticle spacing d, coupled through dipole–dipole interactions and driven by a classical field. Black arrows indicate the dipole moments; Ωand δ denote the Rabi frequency and detuning of a coherent drive. An emitter can decay from |e⟩into |g⟩by spontaneously emitting photons at rate Γ0, while it irreversibly decays into |t⟩at rate ΓT. (b) Level scheme of the single-excitation collective modes of the nano-ring, in the small-volume (Dicke) limit (R/λ0 ≪1). The bright mode (B) and the ground state (g) are coherently coupled by the external drive. Local and thermal dephasing,...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28085_figures/2605.28085_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Absorption cross-section σabs (in units of σ/4) in the presence of local (top panels) and thermal (bottom panels) dephasing, as a function of ΓT and the dephasing rate (ΓD or Γth B , respectively), normalized to the bright mode spontaneous decay rate NΓ0. (a) N = 1 (single emitter), where σabs decreases monotonically with ΓD/Γ0 and is maximal at ΓT = Γ0. (b) N = 10 and (c) N = 50. (d) βJ = 10−2, (e) βJ = 1 and (f) βJ = 102 for N = 10. All panels correspond to the Dicke (small-volume) limit. The dashed black line indicates the optimal dephasing rate maximizing σabs at fixed ΓT/NΓ0. σabs is always bounded by σ/4.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28085_figures/2605.28085_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Absorption cross section σabs (in units of σ/4) in pres- ence of thermal dephasing and in the Dicke (small-volume) limit, at fixed values of ΓT/NΓ0 = 0.6 and Γth B /NΓ0 = 0.05, plotted versus N and βJ.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28085_figures/2605.28085_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Scaling of the ratio σabs/Nσ1at abs with the number of emitters N, in the presence of thermal dephasing, for βJ = 3. For sufficiently large arrays (N ≳100), the ratio approaches a saturation value that depends on βJ. Inset: Saturation value of σabs/Nσ1at abs(Γ0), extracted for N = 300, as a function of βJ. The gray dashed line marks the asymptotic low temperature limit from Eq. (39), scaling as ∼Γ0/ΓT. The trapping rate and coupling strength to the bath are fixed to ΓT/Γ0 = 10−4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28085_figures/2605.28085_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Ratio of the collective absorption cross-section σabs of a nanoring to that of N independent emitters as a function of trapping (ΓT/NΓ0) and dephasing (ΓD/NΓ0 and Γth B /NΓ0) rates. (a) Pure local dephasing and (b) thermal dephasing with βJ = 1. In the independent emitter case, each emitter has spontaneous emission rate Γ0, trapping rate ΓT, and the dephasing rate is taken to be ΓD or Γth B , respectively. The dashed line marks 1, corresponding to equal absorption in the collective and independent cases. Results are shown for N = 10 in the Dicke (small-volume) limit.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that instead of being purely detrimental, environmental dephasing can enhance light absorption in a ring of quantum emitters by moving population into long-lived subradiant modes. This mechanism is particularly effective with thermal dephasing and can lead to absorption efficiencies exceeding the sum of independent atoms.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a counter-intuitive insight into open quantum systems, showing that environmental noise can be a constructive resource for enhancing light-matter interaction, which is highly relevant to quantum optics and light-harvesting research.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether environmental decoherence, such as dephasing and phonon coupling, can be utilized as a resource to enhance single-photon absorption in subwavelength nano-rings of quantum emitters.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that decoherence can enhance absorption by redistributing population from superradiant (bright) modes into subradiant (dark) modes, with thermal dephasing potentially allowing collective absorption to surpass that of independent emitters.

**Method.** The authors use a Lindblad master equation approach within the single-excitation manifold, employing the Born-Markov approximation and analyzing steady-state populations and absorption cross-sections.

**Model / system.** A nano-ring of N dipole-coupled two-level quantum emitters in the deep subwavelength regime, modeled with a non-Hermitian effective Hamiltonian including dipole-dipole interactions and collective radiative decay.

**Key observables.** Absorption cross-section, scattering cross-section, and excited state population.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of emitters (N), interparticle spacing (d/lambda_0), dephasing rates (pure and thermal), trapping rate (Gamma_T), and temperature (beta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Single-excitation manifold approximation, weak-field/weak-driving limit, Born-Markov approximation, and fixed emitter positions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the nano-ring geometry and the level scheme of collective modes; Figure 2 illustrates the absorption cross-section as a function of dephasing and emitter number, showing the emergence of enhancement.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of decoherence as a resource, defines the nano-ring model and master equation, analyzes single-atom vs. collective behavior under local dephasing, extends the study to thermal dephasing and temperature effects, and concludes with finite-size and incoherent illumination analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Decoherence is mostly detrimental in quantum information and quantum optics applications. However, the interplay between environment-induced incoherent dynamics and unitary evolution can give rise to novel quantum many-body phenomena that can be harnessed as a useful resource. As is well known, in dense subwavelength atomic arrays only a single collective eigenmode in the single-excitation manifold couples strongly to free-space radiation, exhibiting superradiant spontaneous emission. Most of the remaining eigenstates form a manifold of weakly radiative modes, giving rise to long-lived subradiant excitations. Here we demonstrate that populating these subradiant modes via additional decoherence mechanisms, such as dephasing or coupling to phonons, can significantly enhance single-photon absorption in a nanoring of quantum emitters. Such nanoring geometry is particularly appealing due to its unique optical properties and its resemblance to natural light-harvesting complexes, which serve as efficient antennas in photosynthesis. Our findings may shed light on fundamental aspects of energy absorption in nature; despite the much greater complexity of biological systems, they may nonetheless operate according to similar underlying optical principles.

</details>

### ⭐ [Photon correlation microscopy of quantum matter](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28623v1)

**Highlighted author(s):** Nicolò Defenu  
**Authors:** Elie Vandoolaeghe, Iñigo Lasheras, Chirag Vaswani, Sampriti Saha, Purbasha Ray, Takashi Taniguchi, Kenji Watanabe, Prasana Sahoo, Nicolò Defenu, Thibault Chervy, Puneet A. Murthy

**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<sub>FIG. 1. Photon correlation microscopy of many-body systems. (a) Matter. A mesoscopic many-body system of emitters exhibiting three distinct phases: a compressible thermal phase (yellow), a quantum-degenerate phase (purple), and a strongly correlated, incompressible phase (blue). These are mirrored in three complementary observables (left to right): the number distribution p(N) broadens in the thermal phase and sharpens toward the incompressible regime; the matter pair correlator g(2) mat(r) evolves (for bosons) from spatial bunching to a deepening correlation hole; the compressibility κT collapses as repulsive interactions stiffen the system. (b) Light. Emitted photons are sent into a...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28623_figures/2605.28623_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Mesoscopic confinement of dipolar excitons. (a) Schematic of the dual-gated device: a lateral MoSe2–WSe2 semiconductor heterostructure encapsulated in hBN. Nanostructured top-gate fingers of width L = 50, 100, 150 nm, oriented perpendicular to the lateral interface, define confinement regions for interfacial dipolar excitons (inset). (b) Micrograph of the device with top-gate fingers (yellow) and the lateral interface (red) outlined; inset: SEM image of the top-gate fingers. (c) Simulated longitudinal trapping potential for L ≈50 nm shows flat-bottomed 1D confinement. (d) VBG-dependent PL spectra at VTG = 0. The unconfined interface exciton XLI exhibits a characteristic blueshift...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28623_figures/2605.28623_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Interaction-driven crossover. (a, b) Power-dependent PL spectra (normalized by integration time) for the L = 50 nm and L = 100 nm traps, respectively. As the power is increased, emission shifts sequentially from the lowest transverse subband to higher-lying ones. (c) Integrated PL count rate (normalized by the average photon energy) for the L = 50 nm (orange), L = 100 nm (blue) and L = 150 nm (green) traps, displaying a steep rise followed by saturation at higher powers. (d) Mean emission energy for the same traps, exhibiting a similar steep rise and saturation. The integrated counts and the mean energy saturate at the same power Psat for each trap, with Psat depending on the trap...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28623_figures/2605.28623_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Many-body blockade of photon emission. (a, b) Power-dependent g(2) ph (τ) for the L = 50 nm and 100 nm traps.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Photon Correlation Microscopy to study many-body correlations in 1D dipolar excitons. By measuring photon statistics, the authors observe a transition from photon bunching to antibunching, signaling a crossover from a compressible thermal gas to an incompressible, number-stabilized state. This technique provides a new way to probe correlated electronic phases using optical tools.

**Why it may be interesting.** It establishes a powerful new bridge between quantum optics and many-body physics, showing how photon statistics can serve as a non-invasive probe for complex phases like Wigner crystals or Mott insulators in semiconductor systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Bridging the gap between quantum optics and many-body physics by developing a method to probe real-space many-body correlations and compressibility in quantum matter at mesoscopic scales.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate Photon Correlation Microscopy (PCM) to observe a density-driven crossover in a 1D dipolar exciton gas from a compressible thermal phase (photon bunching) to an incompressible, number-stabilized quasi-crystal phase (photon antibunching).

**Method.** The study combines experimental Hanbury Brown-Twiss interferometry and power-dependent spectroscopy with numerical many-body simulations using the Multi-Configuration Time-Dependent Hartree method for bosons (MCTDH-B).

**Model / system.** A 1D ensemble of dipolar excitons confined in mesoscopic, gate-defined traps at a lateral MoSe2-WSe2 heterojunction, characterized by large permanent in-plane electric dipole moments and long radiative lifetimes.

**Key observables.** Second-order photon correlation function g2(0), photon bunching/antibunching, isothermal compressibility, particle number fluctuations, and photon recovery time.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trap widths (50, 100, 150 nm), pump power, dipolar interaction strength, and the ratio of sampling length to coherence length.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is assumed to be in quasi-equilibrium (thermalization time is much shorter than radiative lifetime), and the photon statistics are assumed to track the local density fluctuations.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 provides a schematic of PCM and the transition from thermal to incompressible phases; Figure 4 shows power-dependent photon correlation measurements for different trap sizes; Supplemental Figure S6 shows chemical potential and density distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the PCM technique, describes the experimental platform and confinement method, presents the observation of the phase crossover via photon statistics, provides a theoretical validation of the link between photon and matter correlations, and concludes with scaling and dynamical analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Light and matter share fundamental statistical properties, yet the experimental probes of quantum optics and many-body physics have largely evolved along separate trajectories. While many-body physics explores emergent collective phenomena, quantum optics has refined the measurement of correlations between individual photons. Here, we introduce photon correlation microscopy (PCM) - which bridges the two domains by leveraging correlations of emitted light to probe the correlations in quantum matter at mesoscopic scales. We demonstrate this approach using a one-dimensional (1D) ensemble of dipolar excitons confined at a lateral monolayer MoSe$_2$-WSe$_2$ heterojunction. We use gate-defined potentials to confine the 1D excitons to a mesoscopic lengthscale to enhance the visibility of matter correlations in the emitted photon field. Power-dependent spectroscopy reveals a transition from a compressible to an incompressible phase, signaled by the simultaneous saturation of the emission intensity and energy blueshift, which is supported by numerical simulations. Through this crossover, photon correlation measurements show a striking evolution from bunching at low densities to antibunching at high densities. This constitutes a many-body blockade of photon emission emerging directly from a number-stabilized state, driven by collective dipolar repulsion. Our results establish PCM as a powerful probe of many-body physics through the lens of quantum optics, extensible to a broad class of correlated electronic phases, while pointing toward a route to generating non-classical light through many-body correlations.

</details>

### [Observing conformal Floquet dynamics on a digital quantum processor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27530v1)

<sub>Figure 1. Setup and implementation of Floquet circuit. (A) We consider a periodic driving protocol alternating between the uniform TFIM Hamiltonian H0 and the deformed TFIM Hamiltonian H1, with the initial state prepared in the ground state of H0 . In each cycle, the system evolves under either H0 or H1 for periods T0 and T1, respectively. (B) Depending on driving parameters, the system exhibits either a heating phase where energy grows exponentially, or a non-heating phase where energy remains bounded. (C) Circuit implementation of the ground state preparation and time-evolution protocol. The target critical ground state is generated via the hierarchical UMERA circuit, where each two-qubit...</sub>

The black curve denotes the phase boundary predicted by CFT, which separates the heating phase (decaying L(n)) from the non-heating phase (oscillating L(n)). The red diamond and blue square at T0 = ∓0.3, T1 = 0.3 mark the two representative parameter points used in panel (B). (B) Corresponding energy density profile in the non-heating phase, which is bounded over Floquet cycles without localized energy accumulation. In both panels, solid lines denote exact numerical simulations, while markers represent experimental results obtained from up to 1000 measurement shots. <details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27530_figures/2605.27530_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Phase diagram of the perturbed transverse-field Ising chain (A1). The colour map shows the order parameter |⟨σz⟩| across the (Γ, g) plane; the black curve is the critical boundary gc(Γ) extracted from the ξχ peak as described in the main text. The three highlighted markers are integrable / known limits: the free transverse-field Ising point (Γ, g) = (0, 1) (c = 1/2), the “inter- acting Ising” critical point (Γ, g) = (0.25, 0.607) (c = 1/2), and the XX model (Γ, g) = (1, 0) (c = 1).</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27530_figures/2605.27530_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Comparison between exact diagonalization and CFT predictions for different critical lattice models obtained from (A1). (A) Free transverse-field Ising chain, (J, g, Γ) = (1/2, 1/2, 0). (B) Non-integrable interacting Ising chain, (J, g, Γ) = (1, 0.6066, 0.25). (c) XX model, (J, g, Γ) = (1/2, 0, 1/2). Markers denote exact diagonalisation calculations with size L = 16 and periodic boundary conditions. Solid lines are the analytical CFT predictions from Eq. (6). Here we use deformation ⃗κ1 = (1.0, 1.2, −0.2), q = 2, with T0 = −0.3, T1 = 0.3 for heating phase and T0 = 0.3, T1 = 0.3 for non-heating phase. Across these three microscopically different models, numerical data at L = 16...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports the first experimental observation of non-equilibrium dynamics initiated from a many-body critical state using a trapped-ion quantum processor. By using MERA-based state preparation and a Floquet drive, the authors successfully measured the central charge of the Ising universality class. This establishes a versatile framework for studying complex, scale-invariant quantum dynamics on digital hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it demonstrates a scalable way to explore Conformal Field Theory (CFT) predictions and non-equilibrium phase transitions using a digital quantum processor, moving beyond simple product-state initializations.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Simulating non-equilibrium dynamics starting from many-body critical states is difficult due to bottlenecks in state preparation, hardware performance, and classical verification.

**Main result.** The researchers experimentally observed conformal Floquet dynamics on a trapped-ion processor, successfully extracting a central charge of c = 0.575 +/- 0.036, which is consistent with the Ising universality class prediction of c = 1/2.

**Method.** The study uses a hardware-tailored MERA circuit for critical state preparation and a deep Floquet drive implemented via Trotter-Suzuki decomposition, supplemented by error mitigation techniques like dynamical decoupling and parity post-selection.

**Model / system.** A 1D Transverse-Field Ising Model (TFIM) implemented on Quantinuum's Helios fully-connected trapped-ion quantum processor using Ba+ ions.

**Key observables.** Loschmidt echo, energy density, and central charge (c).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Heating and non-heating phases, driving periods (T0, T1), and the deformation amplitude/wavenumber.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The spatial profile of the deformation is simplified to a single harmonic sector, and the initial state is assumed to be the ground state of a uniform Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the Floquet circuit setup and architecture; Figure 2 presents the phase diagram of the Loschmidt echo; Figure 4 shows the critical boundary in a perturbed Ising chain; Figure 5 compares experimental data to ED and CFT predictions; Figure 6 demonstrates the effectiveness of error mitigation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the challenge of critical state dynamics, describes the experimental platform and MERA-based state preparation, details the Floquet driving protocol and error mitigation, presents the observation of heating/non-heating phases and central charge extraction, and concludes with the scalability and universality of the framework.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum simulations are traditionally confined to exploring dynamics starting from unentangled or low-entanglement states due to severe bottlenecks in protocol design, hardware performance, and classical verification. Here, we report the first experimental observation of non-equilibrium dynamics initiated directly from a many-body critical state. Using a fully-connected trapped-ion processor, we prepare the critical ground state of a transverse-field Ising model via a hardware-tailored, logarithmic-depth quantum circuit based on multi-scale entanglement renormalization. Following this initialization, we apply a deep Floquet drive that maintains emergent conformal symmetry, enabling us to benchmark the lattice dynamics against analytical results from continuum theory. In the resulting conformal heating phase, we extract a central charge consistent with the Ising universality class ($c=1/2$) from the universal decay of the Loschmidt echo and observe spatial energy localization predicted by field theory. Conversely, the non-heating phase exhibits global finite-time revivals. This work establishes a scalable and versatile framework for exploring critical quantum dynamics.

</details>

**Summary.** The researchers successfully created a large-scale array of 30 synchronized squeezed light beams using a single atomic vapor cell. By leveraging the movement of atoms between light beams, they achieved collective effects that improve the stability and purity of the quantum states. This provides a scalable pathway for generating multi-channel quantum light sources for imaging and computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates how dissipative coupling through a shared reservoir (flying atoms) can induce synchronization and collective enhancement in a many-body optical system, relevant to open quantum systems and quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of scalable production of multiple beams of squeezed light for quantum information technologies and exploring the collective synchronization of these beams.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a 3-beam array of polarization-squeezed light with 2.03 dB of squeezing, showing that inter-channel coupling via atomic diffusion leads to synchronization and improved state purity.

**Method.** Experimental generation of squeezed light using polarization self-rotation (PSR) in a Rubidium vapor cell, with detection via balanced homodyne detection and characterization of array uniformity and synchronization.

**Model / system.** A 30-beam spatial array interacting with an 87-Rb atomic vapor in a paraffin-coated glass cell, modeled using a multi-level master equation describing atomic density matrix evolution and inter-channel coupling via a shared reservoir of atoms.

**Key observables.** Squeezing level (dB), squeezing angle, noise power vs. laser power, squeezing bandwidth, and time-resolved recovery dynamics after perturbations.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of channels (1 to 31), laser power per channel, cell temperature (55 to 66.7 C), beam size, and detection frequency.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Neglect of certain experimental imperfections like extreme inhomogeneities and optical losses in the numerical models; assumes atoms in the dark region act as a global reservoir.

**Figures summary.** Figures show atomic energy levels, experimental schematics, noise power vs. laser power for different array sizes, squeezing angle uniformity, and the impact of polarization defects and cell geometry on squeezing performance.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scalability problem, describes the experimental setup and atomic mechanism, presents results on squeezing levels and array scaling, investigates synchronization and robustness to defects, and concludes with optimization strategies via numerical simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum light sources such as squeezed light are essential for quantum information science and technologies, but the scalable production of multiple beams of them remains a challenge. Here,we experimentally demonstrate a novel approach to the generation of a large spatial array of polarization-squeezed light beams via atomic-coherence-enhanced nonlinear optical processes using a single atomic vapor cell. Unlike schemes based on independent squeezing generators, the squeezing dynamics of each channel here are governed by a common collective ground-state atomic coherence, produced by all input beams, homogenized by the thermal motion of the atoms, and protected against wall collisions by a paraffin coating. Consequently, the optical states of all channelsare coupled and regulated by each other via the moving atoms, leading to synchronization behavior.We realized a 30-beam array of polarization squeezed state with 2.03 dB of squeezing, experimentally verified the synchronization, and observed improved purity of the squeezed state as well as the system response to perturbations when the size of the array increases. This work provides a pathway towards scalable high-performance quantum light sources for applications in precision measurement, quantum imaging and quantum information processing.

</details>

### [A multilevel tensor network compression technique for simulating Lindblad dynamics in superconducting circuits](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27515v1)

**Authors:** Adrien Moulinas, Xavier Waintal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27515v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **5/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27515_figures/2605.27515_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Role of the three axes of compression for a cat qubit. The plot shows the size of the compressed density matrix versus which compression stages are activated. The compression ratio depends significantly on the ratio between the strength of the dephasing κϕ and the photon loss rate κ2 of the buffer. Bad cat qubits (large κϕ) have a more complex dynamics which is more expensive to simulate. See section IV for more details.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27515_figures/2605.27515_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Illustration of the QTT framework. Starting with a function ψ of a continuous variable x defined over an interval [a, b], we discretize it with 2R points (R = 4 here) and transform it into an R-dimensional tensor F. We then use the TCI algorithm to write this tensor as a tensor train with R tensors.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27515_figures/2605.27515_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Construction of ˆa in QTT form. Evolution of the error of the MPO representing the annihilation operator ˆa with the bond dimension χq for various R values. We observe that a bond dimension of 10 is enough to reduce both errors under 10−14, for all values of R.</sub>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a multilevel tensor network technique to simulate the dissipative dynamics of superconducting bosonic circuits. By leveraging state purity, low entanglement, and a quantized representation of Fock space, the method achieves massive reductions in memory and time complexity. This allows complex simulations of phenomena like transmon ionization to be performed on a laptop rather than a supercomputer.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a highly efficient numerical framework for simulating open quantum systems with large local Hilbert spaces, specifically bridging the gap between complex many-body dynamics and accessible desktop-level computation.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The exponential growth of the Hilbert space dimension (the curse of dimensionality) makes simulating the Lindblad dynamics of large-scale bosonic systems, such as superconducting circuits, computationally prohibitive.

**Main result.** The authors developed a multilevel tensor network compression technique that achieves orders-of-magnitude speed-ups, reducing the computational resources required for transmon ionization simulations from supercomputing scales to laptop level.

**Method.** A three-axis compression approach combining global purification of the density matrix, Matrix Product State (MPS) representation for low entanglement, and Quantics Tensor Train (QTT) for efficient Fock space discretization using Tensor Cross Interpolation (TCI).

**Model / system.** Superconducting quantum circuits, specifically focusing on bosonic modes in cat qubits and transmon-cavity systems subject to Lindblad master equation dynamics involving photon loss and dephasing.

**Key observables.** Purity, Wigner functions, expectation values of field amplitude, transmon and cavity populations, and error rates (phase-flip and bit-flip).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Bond dimensions (purity chi_mu, entanglement chi_e, and quantics chi_q), photon loss rate (kappa_2), dephasing rate (kappa_phi), and the number of bits (R) in the QTT.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The density matrix is relatively pure or not far from pure, and the entanglement between different modes is moderate (qubit-like).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows memory reduction across compression stages; Figure 2 illustrates the QTT construction process; Figure 3 shows error convergence of the annihilation operator; Figure 4 shows fidelity of basis conversion; Figure 12/13 show population evolution, purity loss, and Hamiltonian eigenstate branches.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational problem, details the three-axis compression ansatz (Purification, MPS, and QTT), describes the numerical integration schemes (Kraus maps, TDVP, Magnus expansion), and validates the method through simulations of cat qubits and transmon ionization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Designing superconducting quantum hardware requires simulation tools that can account for various deviations from ideal scenarios. This, in turn, requires approaches that automatically detect certain structures and leverage them to make the computation affordable. Here, we develop a tensor network based technique to simulate the Lindblad dynamics of a few interacting bosonic modes with a focus on superconducting quantum circuits. The technique detects and takes advantage of two very common situations: (i) the density matrix being pure or not far from pure and (ii) the entanglement between different modes being moderate (typically qubit-like). However, (iii) the occupation of the modes can be arbitrarily high (making naïve truncations inefficient). To leverage these features, we use three different nested levels of tensor network compression: (i) we work with a global purification of the density matrix, (ii) we compress the connection between different modes to account for the moderate entanglement and (iii) we use a quantics representation of the Fock occupation number. We showcase the technique for the simulation of large cat qubits as well as for the ionization of transmon qubits, demonstrating orders-of-magnitude speed-up with respect to brute force approaches. In the latter example, it brings the simulation, previously reported on a large supercomputing infrastructure, to laptop level. The favorable scaling with system size should bring genuine computer assisted design of these systems within scope.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28126"></a>
### [Quantum Spin Squeezing Enhanced by Critical Exceptional Points](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28126v1)

**Authors:** Yuma Nakanishi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28126v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `Tavis-Cummings & cavity-many-emitter` **4/5** · 🔥 `driven-dissipative phase transition` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **4/5** · `correlated cavity matter` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `Dicke superradiance` **2/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5**

<img src="2605.28126_figures/2605.28126_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2 (f) shows this collapse by plotting G(δ;S )S −1/3 eff against δ S 2/3 eff . **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that Critical Exceptional Points in open many-body systems can significantly enhance quantum spin squeezing. It establishes specific power-law scaling for squeezed and anti-squeezed variances near the transition and identifies the conditions under which this effect is robust against decoherence.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a novel mechanism for engineering highly anisotropic quantum correlations and steady-state entanglement by leveraging non-Hermitian singularities (CEPs) in driven-dissipative platforms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether Critical Exceptional Points (CEPs) in open many-body systems can be used to parametrically enhance quantum spin squeezing and how these effects scale near the transition.

**Main result.** Near a CEP, the optimally squeezed variance scales linearly with the order parameter (|Z|), while the anti-squeezed variance diverges (|Z|^{-1}), creating a highly anisotropic fluctuation plane. This enhancement is robust against certain dephasing channels but is destroyed by z-axis dephasing.

**Method.** The study uses a mean-field approach, linearized dynamics around the PT-broken fixed point, and the solution of the steady-state Lyapunov equation within a Gaussian approximation in the thermodynamic limit.

**Model / system.** The model consists of dissipative collective-spin systems governed by the GKSL master equation, specifically focusing on L-PT symmetric models such as a spin-boson system where a collective spin is coupled to a lossy cavity mode.

**Key observables.** Quantum spin squeezing parameter (Kitagawa-Ueda), Wineland parameter, covariance matrix, and the Husimi-Q distribution.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimensionless order parameter (Z), spin size (S), collective decay rate (kappa), transverse field (g), and dephasing rates (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the thermodynamic limit (large S), a Gaussian description of fluctuations, and specific constraints on the diffusion matrix (e.g., Dz = 0 for the primary scaling result).

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of mode coalescence at the CEP and an illustration of the increased anisotropy of the spin distribution on the Bloch sphere. Figure 2 demonstrates the scaling collapse of the inverse squeezing using a logarithmically corrected finite-size scaling form.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of CEPs and their role in fluctuations, develops the mathematical framework for the L-PT symmetric collective spin model, derives the scaling laws for squeezing and anti-squeezing near the CEP, analyzes the robustness against dephasing, and concludes with finite-size scaling and experimental realizations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Critical exceptional points (CEPs) are nonequilibrium critical points in open many-body systems at which multiple collective excitation modes coalesce. CEPs are known to amplify classical fluctuations, but their effect on genuinely \textit{quantum} fluctuations remains unclear. Here, we show that dissipative collective-spin systems hosting CEPs exhibit parametrically enhanced steady-state \textit{quantum} spin squeezing. Close to the CEP, the optimally squeezed variance scales as $|Z|$, whereas the anti-squeezed variance diverges as $|Z|^{-1}$, with $Z$ the dimensionless order parameter. Importantly, the anti-squeezed fluctuation direction asymptotically aligns with the coalescing eigenvector of the stability matrix, reflecting the defective nature of the CEP dynamics. These scalings are robust against dephasing channels generated by spin components orthogonal to the coalesced critical collective mode. Our results identify CEPs as a route to engineering steady-state anisotropic quantum fluctuations and correlations in driven-dissipative platforms.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27635"></a>
### [A method for calculating entropy production without knowing transition rates or dwell times](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27635v1)

**Authors:** John W. Biddle  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27635v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `methods for driven-dissipative` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

(B) Shows conformation Eo, in which the binding sites for L and M face the outside of the cell and binding of L and M take place at chemical potentials µM o and µL o . (C) shows a graphical representation of the possible states of E, also reflecting the limitation that L...</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.27635_figures/2605.27635_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2: Top row: spanning trees of the graph in Fig. 1 Bottom row: the fundamental set of cycles associated with each spanning tree; the chords associated with each cycle are shown as dashed rather than solid lines.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27635_figures/2605.27635_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3: Top: The system described in Fig. 1, together with a decomposition into fundamental cycles and a hypothetical sequence σX associated with a possible trajectory X. Bottom: a coarse graining of the same system corresponding to the binding state being unobservable if the protein complex is in conformation Ei. </details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27635_figures/2605.27635_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Results of Gillespie simulations modeling the system described in the Illustrative Example. (A)</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a method to calculate the entropy production rate of a Markovian system without needing to know transition rates or dwell times. By using only the sequence of states and total elapsed time, the method allows for the estimation of thermodynamic properties from incomplete trajectory data. It also demonstrates that choosing specific cycle bases can optimize the convergence of this estimator.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics, as it provides a way to quantify non-equilibrium properties and dissipation in systems where the full microscopic dynamics are not experimentally resolvable.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Calculating the entropy production rate in non-equilibrium systems is difficult when underlying transition rates, dwell times, or transition times are unknown or unobservable.

**Main result.** The author presents a new method to infer the entropy production rate using only the sequence of visited states and the total trajectory time, bypassing the need for transition rates or dwell times.

**Method.** The method uses a fundamental-cycle decomposition (spanning tree approach) to express entropy production in terms of the log-ratio of forward to reverse cycle occurrences.

**Model / system.** The model consists of Markovian systems with discrete microstates and continuous-time dynamics, represented by a strongly connected, reversible directed graph governed by a master equation.

**Key observables.** Entropy production rate, net flux, cycle affinity, and the number of times a cycle occurs in a trajectory.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Total trajectory time, sequence of states, and the choice of cycle basis for convergence optimization.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The underlying dynamics must be Markovian, the system must have a finite number of discrete states, and the graph must be strongly connected and reversible.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates a biological active transport system; Figure 2 shows spanning trees and cycle decomposition via chords; Figure 3 compares full vs. coarse-grained systems; Figure 4 demonstrates the convergence of the estimator using different cycle bases.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the problem of calculating entropy production, introduces the mathematical framework of Markov graphs and master equations, presents the new inference method based on cycle affinities, validates the method via Gillespie simulations, and discusses the impact of cycle basis selection and coarse-graining.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The entropy production rate is central to the study of non-equilibrium systems. This parameter is closely connected to violation of time-reversal symmetry, energy consumption, efficiency, and other properties of interest; in short, it quantifies how far a system is from thermodynamic equilibrium. Standard formulas for the entropy production require knowledge of the system's underlying dynamics, but this knowledge may be hard to acquire in practice. Here, I present a method for inferring the entropy production rate of a Markovian system from the sequence of states that the system occupies on a single long trajectory or many shorter trajectories, and the total time of the trajectory or trajectories. The method does not require knowledge of the dwell times in the various states, the times at which transitions occur, or of the transition rates characterizing the Markov process.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Security Metrics for Nonlinear Optical Light Sources from Interferometric Field Reconstruction](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28695v1)

**Summary.** This paper investigates how the intrinsic spin and coherence dynamics of 2D perovskites can be used to generate secure quantum communication signals. By analyzing the Holevo bound and secret-bit rates, the authors show that controlling material parameters like population time can optimize information throughput. The work establishes a framework for using nonlinear spectroscopy to design new materials for quantum key distribution.

**Why it may be interesting.** It bridges nonlinear spectroscopy with quantum information theory, demonstrating how many-body dynamics (exciton-biexciton interactions) and spin relaxation can be engineered to optimize the security and throughput of photonic quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Evaluating the security metrics (Holevo bound and secret-bit rates) of quantum communication using nonlinear optical light sources generated from material-intrinsic dynamics.

**Main result.** Incorporating coherence time into the parameter space reduces the Holevo bound by 2.6-5.8%, and population-time encoding is found to be significantly more efficient for high-throughput communication than coherence-time encoding.

**Method.** The researchers use polarization-resolved interferometric measurements and field reconstruction to extract polarization density matrices, combined with a microscopic nonlinear response model for the Bloch vector.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a 2D organic-inorganic hybrid perovskite ((PEA)2PbI4) utilizing four-wave-mixing (FWM) signal fields, modeled via a reduced-order response-function framework involving single-exciton and biexciton resonances.

**Key observables.** Polarization contrast (C_pol), relative phase (delta_phi), Holevo bound (chi), effective secret-bit yield (Y_eff), and Bloch vector components.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coherence time (tau), population/spin-relaxation time (T), detection wavelength (lambda), and spectral overlap (Xi_X,XXB).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The calculation assumes reconstructed fields are pure by construction, the system operates in the slow-modulation limit, and the exciton fine-structure splittings are smaller than the spectroscopic linewidths.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the FWM communication approach and spin dynamics; Fig 2 details the experimental setup; Fig 3 displays energy levels and transient absorption; Fig 4 shows polarization components and contrast; Fig 5 shows polarization modulation; Fig 6 presents Bloch-sphere representations; Fig 7 compares encoding efficiencies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the quantum communication problem, describes the experimental FWM setup and material properties, presents the field reconstruction and security metric derivation, analyzes the results across different spectral regimes and encoding strategies, and concludes with implications for material-intrinsic quantum engineering.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Nonlinear optical light sources enable the generation of photons with quantum states that are intrinsically linked to underlying material dynamics, rather than imposed through external modulation. Here we investigate fundamental quantum communication metrics of four-wave-mixing signal fields generated by the two-dimensional perovskite (PEA)2PbI4. Using polarization-resolved interferometric measurements together with a microscopic nonlinear response model for the Bloch vector, we reconstruct effective single-photon polarization density matrices inferred from the experimental signal fields and evaluate the corresponding Holevo bound and effective secret-bit rates as a function of the coherence time, population time, and detection wavelength. We find that incorporating the coherence-time degree of freedom systematically lowers the Holevo bound by approximately 2.6-5.8% across the various excitonic resonances, indicating reduced distinguishability of the polarization states when the full multidimensional parameter space is sampled. To connect the polarization-state indistinguishability with experimentally achievable throughput, we further introduce an effective secret-bits-per-pulse metric that enables rapid evaluation of secure information throughput for candidate materials without requiring photon-number-resolved detection. For the present system, control of the population time via spin-dependent evolution yields substantially higher secret-bit rates than manipulation of the coherence time, while spectral regions associated with single-exciton and biexciton resonances define complementary operating regimes for secure communication. More broadly, this work positions nonlinear spectroscopy as a framework for exploring how emergent optical materials can generate and structure quantum states in ways that are advantageous for established quantum communication schemes.

</details>

<sub>FIG. 4. Schematic of the laser and cavity stabilization scheme. **Summary.** The paper describes the engineering of a new cryogenic apparatus designed to couple 2D materials to a confocal optical cavity. By using a confocal geometry, the system creates localized supermodes that enhance light-matter coupling while allowing for simultaneous electrical transport measurements. This platform enables the study of steady-state driven phenomena in correlated 2D systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to cavity QED and quantum optics as it demonstrates a method to achieve high-cooperativity regimes using multimode supermodes, providing a platform for studying driven-dissipative phase transitions and light-matter interactions in 2D materials.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for a stable, cryogenic, and ultrahigh-vacuum apparatus capable of coupling 2D van der Waals materials to a confocal multimode optical cavity for simultaneous optical and electronic measurements.

**Main result.** The development of a specialized platform that achieves high-finesse light-matter coupling using a confocal supermode while maintaining minimal mechanical vibrations and enabling electrical transport measurements.

**Method.** The researchers constructed a cryogenic UHV system featuring a length-tunable Fabry-Perot cavity, utilizing the Pound-Drever-Hall technique for frequency locking and a 4-axis nanopositioner for sample alignment.

**Model / system.** A cryogenic, ultrahigh-vacuum apparatus housing a length-tunable confocal multimode Fabry-Perot cavity containing hBN-encapsulated transition metal dichalcogenides (MoSe2 and WSe2) on a sapphire substrate.

**Key observables.** Cavity finesse, cavity length noise, sample displacement, transmission spectra, and electrical transport measurements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Base temperature of 10.4 K, vacuum pressure of 10^-10 Torr, cavity finesse of ~208, and a supermode waist of ~1.7-9.5 micrometers.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The effective response of the cavity length to dispersive shifts is assumed to be subject to the finite bandwidth of the PDH servo.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the vacuum assembly and cavity/sample stack; Figure 2 details the 4-axis nanopositioner; Figure 3 displays cavity transmission spectra; Figure 4 shows the laser stabilization chain; Figure 5 presents in-situ sample images; Figure 7 shows the power spectral density of cavity length noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific motivation, describes the construction and components of the cryogenic/UHV apparatus, details the optical and electrical measurement methods, presents performance metrics for stability and positioning, and concludes with the demonstration of the integrated platform.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Two-dimensional van der Waals materials exhibit a variety of correlated electron phases, and optical driving offers a promising route toward manipulating them. For example, cavity-enhanced, continuous-wave (CW) Raman excitation has been suggested as a way to coherently and superradiantly populate phonons or charge density waves via material excitons. A steady-state phonon population may be sustained with sufficiently strong electron-phonon coupling to drive novel collective response. We describe an apparatus built to meet the requirements of such an experimental program: Namely, an ultrahigh-vacuum system housing a length-tunable confocal Fabry-Pérot cavity with an intracavity sample, both cryogenically cooled and stabilized against vibrations. A four-axis nanopositioner aligns the sample and supports electrical leads for sample carrier density modulation and transport measurements. Transmission through the multimode cavity enables in situ sample imaging for alignment; the sample is a transition metal dichalcogenide in this work. Operating near the confocal geometry concentrates the optical field into a localized supermode that substantially enhances light-matter coupling. This enhancement is preserved despite the millimeter-scale cavity length, which provides room for sample alignment and exchange.

</details>

### [Coherent Dark State Formation of a Lead-Vacancy Spin Qubit in Diamond](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27841v1)

**Authors:** Yiyang Chen, Koyo Hirai, Tzyy Zheng Neo, Eiki Ota, Takashi Taniguchi, Masashi Miyakawa, Shinobu Onoda, Toshiharu Makino, Mutsuko Hatano, Takayuki Iwasaki

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that lead-vacancy centers in diamond can maintain long spin lifetimes and optical coherence at temperatures above 4 K. By achieving successful single-shot readout and observing coherent dark state formation, the study positions PbV centers as highly promising candidates for scalable, thermally robust quantum networks.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates the use of coherent population trapping to create dark states in a solid-state system and provides experimental evidence of suppressed phonon-mediated decoherence due to large zero-field splitting, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The need for quantum emitters that can operate at higher temperatures (above 4 K) for scalable quantum networks while maintaining high optical coherence and suppressed spectral diffusion.

**Main result.** The PbV center in diamond demonstrates exceptional thermal robustness with a spin lifetime of 12 ms at 7.5 K and successful formation of a coherent dark state via coherent population trapping.

**Method.** The study uses photoluminescence excitation (PLE) spectroscopy, all-optical spin initialization and single-shot readout (SSR), and coherent population trapping (CPT) to characterize spin properties.

**Model / system.** A lead-vacancy (PbV) center in diamond, which is a spin-1/2 system with a large ground-state zero-field splitting of approximately 3900 GHz, implemented in a solid immersion lens (SIL) platform.

**Key observables.** Spin lifetime (T1), spin dephasing time (T2*), spin initialization fidelity, branching ratio, and saturation power.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperatures of 6.5 K to 13 K, magnetic fields up to 200 mT, spin lifetime of 12 ms at 7.5 K, and spin dephasing time of 354 ns at 6.5 K.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The decoherence at 6.5 K is assumed to be limited by the spin bath rather than phonon-mediated processes.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the PbV energy level structure, PL spectrum, and PLE spectra under magnetic fields; Fig 2 details the spin initialization process, pulse sequences for SSR, and photon counting statistics.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation for high-temperature emitters, describes the PbV system and experimental setup, presents spectroscopic characterization, details spin initialization and readout results, and concludes with the implications for quantum networking.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A lead-vacancy (PbV) center in diamond exhibits coherent emission above the liquid helium temperature, making it highly attractive for quantum network applications. Here, we report the magneto-optical and spin properties of PbV centers in diamond. We record a spin lifetime of 12 ms at 7.5 K under large off-axis magnetic field. Furthermore, we observe formation of the coherent dark state by coherent population trapping and estimate a spin dephasing time of 354 ns at 6.5 K. This work demonstrates the outstanding thermal robustness of the PbV spin compared to other group-IV centers above 4 K.

</details>

### [Fractional short-time dynamics in driven quantum gases](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28606v1)

**Authors:** Uri Sharell, Tilman Enss  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28606v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium universality` **4/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

The blue and red lines show the derived simple approximate forms for early (26) and...</sub>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the short-time evolution of pairing in driven quantum gases is governed by a fractional differential equation, leading to universal non-thermal scaling. It further shows that as many-body effects take over, the dynamics transition into a hydrodynamic regime described by a Müller-Israel-Stewart type equation. The theoretical predictions show excellent agreement with experimental observations of momentum distributions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a unified mathematical framework (fractional calculus) to bridge the gap between non-equilibrium, non-thermal fixed points and classical hydrodynamics in many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Connecting the early-time, non-universal dynamics of driven quantum gases to their late-time hydrodynamic equilibrium regimes.

**Main result.** The authors discover that short-time pairing dynamics satisfy a fractional differential equation (FDE) characterized by a conformal fixed point and non-thermal scaling, which eventually relaxes into a Müller-Israel-Stewart type hydrodynamic attractor.

**Method.** The study employs Riemann-Liouville fractional calculus, Laplace transforms, and the Lippmann-Schwinger equation to derive analytical solutions for sudden quenches and power-law drives.

**Model / system.** The system consists of dilute, ultracold atomic gases with short-range attractive interactions, modeled by a time-dependent Schrödinger equation with a delta-function potential and Bethe-Peierls boundary conditions.

**Key observables.** Pairing amplitude, contact density, momentum distribution, and particle population growth.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Scattering length a(t), diffusion quantum D0, relaxation time tau, and non-thermal scaling exponents alpha and beta.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes the pairing amplitude is differentiable and that the initial integral of the fractional derivative is zero; it also assumes a specific form for the power-law drive.

**Figures summary.** The notes refer to a figure showing the theoretical prediction for particle population growth matching experimental data.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of early-to-late time dynamics, derives the fractional differential equation for short-time pairing, provides analytical solutions for various driving protocols (quenches and power-laws), and concludes by describing the transition to the hydrodynamic regime and comparison with experiment.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum gases with short-range attractive interaction have a tendency to form pairs. For time-dependent interaction we find that the pairing amplitude at small separation satisfies a fractional differential equation (FDE). We derive analytic solutions of the pairing evolution for sudden interaction quenches and power-law drives toward resonant scattering. We observe universal short-time dynamics governed by a conformal fixed point at which the momentum distribution exhibits nonthermal, self-similar scaling in time, in quantitative agreement with experiment. At longer times, many-body effects induce relaxation toward an equilibrium state. In this limit, the FDE turns into a Müller-Israel-Stewart type equation that describes a hydrodynamic attractor approaching equilibrium.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27512"></a>
### [Many-Body Quantum Chaos At All Time Scales](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27512v1)

**Authors:** Antonio M. García-García, Lucas Sá, Jacobus J. M. Verbaarschot, Jie-Ping Zheng

**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>Figure 2. Left: The Wightman function GW (t) at β = 0 for an out-of-time-order source with strength s = 10−6, obtained from the SD equations on the Keldysh contour up to t = 30 with spacing ∆t = 0.04 following the methods outlined in Appendix C of Ref. [55] (black), compared to the Ansatz Eq. (6) with one fitting parameter (dark red). For large t, GW (t) ∼a exp[−2Γt] + Re(b exp[−2(Γ + iΩ)t]) shows weaker oscillations than the squared Green’s function G2(t) ∼Re(eb exp[−2(Γ + iΩ)t]) obtained by solving the SD equations. Middle: The normalized derivative exp(−λLt)dGW (t, s)/sdt, compared to the derivative of the Ansatz. The disagreement for t &lt; 2 is an artifact due to the position of the...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27512_figures/2605.27512_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Energy dependence of the average matrix elements |⟨k|γ0|l⟩|2 as a function of El for fixed Ek. Note the enhance- ment by a factor 2 for k = l. The diagonal matrix elements |⟨k|γ0|k⟩|2 (inset) only show a weak dependence on the energy in the bulk of the spectrum. The matrix elements for k ≈l, which give the main contribution to the Green’s function, are much larger than the SYKGUE values of (1 + δkl)/D.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27512_figures/2605.27512_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Time dependence of the Green’s function (upper) and OTOC (lower) on a log-log scale. Numerical results ob- tained from exact diagonalization for the N = 26, q = 4, β = 0 (black disks) SYK model are compared to the analytical results and numerical fits derived in the main text, given by solid curves as indicated in the legend of the lower figure. The inset in the upper figure shows the same curves for t &gt; 100 on a linear plot. The correction to the SYKGUE result is large, but because of the factorization assumption there is still a 10 percent discrepancy between analytical and numerical results.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a complete analytical description of the dynamics of the SYK model across all time scales. It demonstrates that the evolution of quantum chaos, from initial exponential growth to late-time plateaus, is interconnected and deviates from standard random matrix theory due to energy-dependent correlations.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a unified analytical framework for understanding how information scrambling evolves into late-time ergodic behavior and identifies specific deviations from universal RMT predictions.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving a comprehensive understanding of many-body quantum chaos across all time scales, specifically bridging the gap from the early perturbative/scrambling regime to the late-time RMT/ergodic regime.

**Main result.** The authors obtained analytical expressions for Green's and OTOC functions at all time scales, discovering that late-time behavior deviates from standard RMT predictions due to local-in-energy correlations.

**Method.** A combination of the scramblon formalism, Random Matrix Theory (RMT) techniques, and numerical exact diagonalization/Krylov subspace methods.

**Model / system.** The q=4 Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev (SYK) model, specifically the N-Majorana version, in the infinite temperature limit.

**Key observables.** Retarded Green's function G(t), Out-of-Time-Order Correlator (OTOC) F(t), Wightman function G_W(t), and Spectral Form Factor (SFF) K(t).

**Important parameters / regimes.** System size N, Lyapunov exponent lambda_L, temperature beta=0, and the Ruelle-Pollicott resonances.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumptions include locality in energy for late times, energy-independent matrix elements within specific ranges, and the use of the SYKGUE model as a comparison for RMT behavior.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares numerical ED data with various analytical fits (scramblon, Ruelle-Pollicott, power-law, RMT) for G(t) and F(t); Figure 2 shows the Wightman function and its derivative; Figure 5 shows time dependence for N=26.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of multi-scale chaos, describes the SYK model and the scramblon/RMT methods, analyzes specific temporal domains (perturbative, scrambling, power-law, RMT), and concludes with a discussion on the interconnectedness of these regimes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We describe the dynamics of many-body quantum chaotic systems at all time scales by studying the Green's and out-of-time order correlation (OTOC) functions of the four-body, $N$-Majorana Sachdev-Ye-Kitaev model. By combining the scramblon formalism and random-matrix-theory techniques, we obtain analytical expressions for these functions at all times. The early exponential growth of the OTOC is followed by an exponential decay at a rate governed by that of the Green's function (the real part of the leading complex Ruelle-Pollicott resonances). For late times that scale exponentially with $N$, both functions have a dip-ramp-plateau pattern for $N \mathrm{mod}8 = 2, 6$ that deviates substantially from the ergodic prediction due to local-in-energy correlations of matrix elements and eigenvalues, even after the Heisenberg time.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28038"></a>
### [Squeezed-slit Bohr-Einstein Interferometer](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28038v1)

**Authors:** Hao-Wen Cheng, Xu-Zhao-Qiu Zeng, Yu-Chen Zhang, Yu-Hao Deng, Zhan Wu, Rui Lin, Yu-Cheng Duan, Zi-Han Chen, Jun Rui, Ming-Cheng Chen, Chao-Yang Lu, Jian-Wei Pan  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28038v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `quantum optics experiment` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `QC/QI experiment` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<sub>Figure 3. Observed squeezing dynamics and enhancement beyond the thermal visi-</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<sub>Figure 4. Kerr-induced phase-space dynamics and intrinsic visibility beyond the SQL.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28038_figures/2605.28038_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 of the main text and Sec. III B), assuming an initial thermal occupation ¯n = 0.5. This state</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The study demonstrates a method to surpass the Standard Quantum Limit in a single-atom interferometer by using a squeezed-state protocol. By engineering the motion of a trapped Rubidium atom, the researchers achieved interference visibility significantly higher than the vacuum limit. The work also shows that this setup can be used for high-fidelity Wigner function reconstruction.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates active quantum state engineering to beat fundamental limits and provides a new interferometric method for continuous-variable Wigner tomography.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The Einstein-Bohr recoiling-slit experiment is fundamentally limited by the Standard Quantum Limit (SQL) due to zero-point fluctuations of the atomic slit. The goal is to transcend this limit by actively engineering the quantum state of the atomic slit.

**Main result.** The researchers achieved an intrinsic interference visibility of 0.938, violating the SQL of 0.819 by over 10 standard deviations, corresponding to 7.6 dB of effective squeezing.

**Method.** A non-adiabatic quench-evolve-quench (QEQ) protocol is used to prepare the atom in a squeezed state, combined with global Bayesian analysis and Wigner tomography to reconstruct the quantum state.

**Model / system.** A single 87Rb atom trapped in an 852-nm optical tweezer, subject to an effective Kerr Hamiltonian due to the intrinsic anharmonicity of the trap.

**Key observables.** Interference visibility (V), quantum characteristic function (chi), Wigner function (W), and photon count asymmetry.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lamb-Dicke parameter (eta = 0.316), squeezing parameter (S1 = 0.50), Kerr coefficient (K/omega1 = -0.011), and trap frequencies (37.8 kHz to 12.8 kHz).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The sudden quench approximation is used for trap modulation; the Truncated Wigner Approximation (TWA) is used to model non-Gaussian dynamics; and the system is assumed to be in the weak nonlinearity regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 3 shows visibility as a function of trap durations and comparison to thermal limits; Figure 4 illustrates the evolution of the Wigner function from Gaussian to S-shaped non-Gaussian distributions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the Bohr-Einstein problem, describes the experimental setup and QEQ protocol, presents the results of surpassing the SQL, analyzes the impact of Kerr-induced non-Gaussianity, and demonstrates the utility of the setup for Wigner tomography.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Einstein-Bohr recoiling-slit gedankenexperiment, a cornerstone of quantum complementarity, has long been constrained by the zero-point fluctuations of the atomic slit -- the spatial Standard Quantum Limit (SQL). Here we transcend this fundamental boundary through active quantum state engineering of a single-atom slit. By implementing a non-adiabatic quench-evolve-quench protocol, we prepare the atomic motion in a squeezed state, dynamically redistributing phase-space uncertainty to suppress which-path information and restore high-visibility interference beyond the static vacuum limit. We report an intrinsic visibility of $0.938_{-0.008}^{+0.004}$, violating the SQL ($0.819$) by over 10 standard deviations, corresponding to $7.6(2)$ dB of effective squeezing. Our work reveals Kerr-induced non-Gaussian dynamics and reinterprets the traditional interferometer as a powerful tool for continuous-variable Wigner tomography, bridging the gap between quantum foundations and advanced metrology.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28808"></a>
### [Device-Agnostic Microwave Noise Metrology for Nonlinear Cryogenic Quantum Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28808v1)

**Authors:** Andrea Celotto, Alessandro Alocco, Bernardo Galvano, Luca Fasolo, Emanuele Palumbo, Luca Callegaro, Luca Oberto, Patrizia Livreri, Emanuele Enrico  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28808v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `quantum optics experiment` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28808_figures/2605.28808_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. Flow diagram (not at scale) of the scattering of fluctuations impacting the output signal mode, tracked across relevant experimental stages. Photons are picked up from thermal baths due to dissipative processes. The gain G can be identified as the ratio of the final and initial widths of the uppermost flow of photons inside the device.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28808_figures/2605.28808_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. Two placements of the absolute noise reference, labeled REF. (a) In a serial configuration the DUT is included in the path between the noise source and the detector. (b) In a substitution configuration the noise source and the DUT are connected to the readout chain through cryogenic switches. The reference planes Rj indicate where the relevant microwave quantities are defined. The female-female thru connection can symbolize a more complete S-parameter calibration calkit consisting also of impedance standards.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28808_figures/2605.28808_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. (a,b) Gain G and added noise Nadd of the JTWPA as functions of pump power Pp and signal frequency. The frequency-dependent fine features in the gain, commonly referred to as ripple, are deterministic features attributed to standing waves in the device packaging rather than measurement noise, as their consistency for various pump powers may suggest. (c) Gain and added noise, averaged over the 3.6–5.5 GHz band, as functions of pump power. The colored band around the noise curve represents its associated uncertainty, propagated from the fit on Planck-spectroscopy data.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28808_figures/2605.28808_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Illustration of the bias described in Appendix A based on synthetic but realistical data. The solid curve is the nonlinear model of Eq. (12a); the dots are sampled calibration points; the dashed line is the simplified affine regression. The clear structure of residuals to the fit shows the consequence of neglecting energy-dependent contributions δN(E).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28808_figures/2605.28808_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. Mechanical rendering of the custom VTS assembly. The internal cavity houses a matched attenuator, a 100 Ωheater, and a ruthenium-oxide thermometer. **Summary.** The paper presents a new method for measuring the noise and gain of cryogenic microwave devices by substituting the device with a known thermal noise source. This approach avoids the errors caused by the device's own nonlinearities during the calibration process. It was successfully tested on a nonlinear traveling-wave parametric amplifier.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum optics and open quantum systems researchers as it provides a rigorous way to characterize the noise and signal integrity of nonlinear microwave components essential for quantum readout and signal processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Accurately characterizing the gain and added noise of nonlinear cryogenic microwave devices, such as JTWPAs, is difficult because traditional serial measurement configurations introduce model-dependent biases and cannot easily decouple the device's internal dynamics from the readout chain.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate a device-agnostic substitution-based metrology protocol that successfully extracts the gain and input-referred added noise of a JTWPA even under complex, pump-dependent nonlinear regimes.

**Method.** The protocol uses a substitution method where a controllable noise source (from a Variable Temperature Stage) is switched into the measurement path to replace the DUT, combined with Planck spectroscopy and SOLR S-parameter calibration.

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of a dilution refrigerator setup with a Variable Temperature Stage (VTS) and a Josephson Traveling Wave Parametric Amplifier (JTWPA) operating in the C and X bands at millikelvin temperatures.

**Key observables.** Power Spectral Density (PSD), S-parameters (gain, transmission, reflection), and input-referred added noise.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Pump power, signal frequency, system gain, system noise temperature, and the quantum-limited noise floor.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the noise source can be treated as a two-port component and that the electron temperature in the VTS follows the temperature of the copper body.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a flow diagram of photon detection; Figure 2 compares serial vs. substitution configurations; Figure 3 displays JTWPA gain and noise as functions of pump power; Figure 4 shows statistical bias in regression models.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of calibration bias, proposes a substitution-based architecture, details the integration of SOLR and Planck spectroscopy, validates the method using a nonlinear JTWPA, and provides a mathematical analysis of potential errors.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Microwave devices capable of near-quantum-limited signal processing are essential components in the toolbox of solid-state quantum technologies. The manipulation and readout of single-photon microwave signals through amplifiers, mixers, isolators, etc. must fulfill strict requirements in terms of signal integrity to ensure reliable operation.   These active microwave quantum devices operate in complex cryo-electronic setups. This poses challenges to their characterization, since all relevant figures of merit must be expressed at the reference planes of their ports. Even though cryogenic S-parameter calibration is non-trivial, metrological approaches are converging toward rigorous methods. Furthermore, preserving signal integrity must be quantified via absolute noise levels at the ports of the Device Under Test (DUT), requiring an absolute power reference.   In this work, we present an in situ noise metrology protocol based on substituting a controllable noise source for the DUT. We motivate this choice by showing that placing the noise source at the DUT input impacts the separability of the calibration from the DUT characteristics.   Our proposed architecture combines Planck spectroscopy using a Variable Temperature Stage with Short-Open-Load-Reciprocal scattering-parameter calibration, so that noise and scattering quantities are referred to the same cryogenic reference planes. In this configuration, the readout-chain calibration is separated from the internal dynamics of the DUT. As a demanding use case, we apply the protocol to a Josephson Traveling Wave Parametric Amplifier and extract its gain and input-referred added noise under pump conditions activating multimode nonlinear behavior. This illustrates how our device-agnostic protocol supports portable noise characterization of nonlinear cryogenic microwave devices.

</details>

The residual fundamental beam is attenuated and mode-matched to provide a weak coherent seed.
