**Summary.** This paper experimentally demonstrates the generation of correlated photon triplets by using a very weak coherent seed to stimulate parametric down-conversion. By measuring third-order correlations, the authors prove that the seed photon directly participates in the nonlinear process to create a three-photon state. This provides a new method for engineering multi-photon states for quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates a way to engineer higher-order multipartite photon states (like triplets) using weak seeds, which is highly relevant for quantum imaging, sensing, and generating complex states for quantum information processing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the transition from spontaneous to stimulated parametric down-conversion in the ultra-weak seed regime, specifically looking for genuine three-photon correlations.

**Main result.** The observation of a clear enhancement in third-order temporal correlations (g3n = 12.5) that confirms the seed photon stimulates the emission of a photon pair to form a correlated triplet.

**Method.** The researchers used third-order temporal correlation measurements and a background-normalized quantity (g3n) to distinguish stimulated three-photon events from accidental coincidences and spontaneous multi-pair emission.

**Model / system.** A continuous-wave, low-gain, type-I parametric down-conversion setup using a BBO crystal pumped by a 400 nm laser, with a weak coherent field (average photon number << 1) used as a seed.

**Key observables.** Third-order temporal correlation functions G(3)(0,0) and the normalized function g(3)n.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Low-gain regime (|eta t| << 1), ultra-weak seed regime (average photon number per coherence time << 1), and a seed detection probability of approximately 10^-6.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Undepleted pump approximation, low-gain approximation, and the assumption that the seed field does not significantly modify the underlying SPDC statistics.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the conceptual schematic of photon-triplet generation; Figure 2 details the experimental setup; Figure 3 displays the raw three-fold coincidence histogram and the flat normalized simulation results.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of stimulated PDC, describes the experimental setup and theoretical Hamiltonian, details the normalization protocol and Monte Carlo validation, presents the experimental observation of the three-photon peak, and discusses implications for quantum technologies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Parametric down-conversion is a widely used source of nonclassical light in quantum optics and photonic quantum technologies. While stimulated parametric down-conversion with strong classical seeds is well studied, the regime in which stimulation occurs at the single-photon level has hitherto remained largely unexplored experimentally. Here, we study continuous-wave, low-gain down-conversion seeded by a weak coherent field with an average photon number well below one per coherence time. By measuring third-order temporal correlations, we observe a clear enhancement that cannot be accounted for by spontaneous processes or accidental coincidences alone, and is consistent with stimulation involving the seed and the generated photon pair. These results provide time-domain evidence of seed-induced three-photon correlations and suggest new ways to engineer and probe multi-photon states for quantum imaging, sensing, and information processing.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28681"></a>
### [Krylov complexity has it all](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28681v1)

**Authors:** Wolfgang Mück  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28681v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `scars & prethermalization` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28681_figures/2605.28681_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28681_figures/2605.28681_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28681_figures/2605.28681_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28681_figures/2605.28681_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28681_figures/2605.28681_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that Krylov complexity encodes all information about the dynamics of a quantum operator. It provides a specific mathematical procedure to reconstruct the underlying Lanczos coefficients from the time-evolution of the complexity. This establishes Krylov complexity as a complete diagnostic tool for operator growth.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for many-body dynamics as it provides a fundamental 'proof of principle' for using a single complexity measure to fully characterize the evolution of quantum operators.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether Krylov complexity contains the complete information required to reconstruct the full dynamics of a quantum operator.

**Main result.** The author proves that Krylov complexity is a complete characterization of operator evolution and provides a recursive algorithm to extract Lanczos coefficients from its Taylor expansion.

**Method.** The study employs Taylor expansion analysis of the Krylov complexity and its moments, combined with the Lanczos algorithm and properties of orthogonal polynomials.

**Model / system.** The dynamics are modeled using a quantum operator evolving under a Liouvillian, which is mapped onto a one-dimensional semi-infinite chain (Krylov basis) described by a hopping model.

**Key observables.** Krylov complexity K(t), second spread complexity K2(t), return amplitude, and spectral density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lanczos coefficients (b_n, Delta_n) and diagonal terms (a_n) representing the Liouvillian action.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reconstruction algorithm assumes that the input Taylor coefficients represent a valid, physically consistent Krylov complexity.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing the equivalence of Krylov complexity to other dynamical quantities, presents a recursive algorithm for coefficient extraction, and concludes by comparing Krylov complexity to spread complexity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

This paper establishes that Krylov complexity contains the entire information about the dynamics of a quantum operator, extending the list of equivalent quantities that can serve this purpose, such as the Lanczos coefficients, the return amplitude, and the spectral density. To demonstrate this equivalence, an explicit recursive algorithm is constructed to calculate Lanczos coefficients from the Taylor expansion of the Krylov complexity around $t=0$. Furthermore, the paper discusses the distinction between Krylov and spread complexity, clarifying why a similar recursive algorithm cannot exist for the latter without additional dynamical input. These results provide a ``proof of principle'' for using Krylov complexity as a complete characterization of operator evolution in quantum systems.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27577"></a>
### [Sympathetic Cooling in Trapped Ions with Spectral Selectivity via the Zeeman Shift](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27577v1)

**Authors:** Kavyashree Ranawat, Jiyong Yu, Andrew Van Horn, Jacob Whitlow, Kenneth R Brown, Jungsang Kim

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27577_figures/2605.27577_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: Heating rate suppression of the COM and tilt modes in a 5-ion 171Yb+ chain. The</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27577_figures/2605.27577_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5: Steady state average phonon number versus the number of cooling pulses and duty</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The researchers developed a way to cool specific ions in a single-isotope chain without disturbing the quantum information stored in other ions. By using the Zeeman shift to create spectral isolation, they successfully suppressed motional heating while maintaining high qubit coherence. This approach reduces the hardware complexity and overhead typically required for sympathetic cooling.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for quantum optics and open quantum systems as it provides a practical method for managing dissipation (cooling) in a many-body system (ion chain) while minimizing the impact of the environment on the system's internal quantum states.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving high-fidelity quantum logic in trapped ions requires cooling motional modes to the ground state without introducing decoherence to data qubits, which is challenging with traditional multi-species or multi-isotope cooling methods.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate a same-isotope sympathetic cooling scheme using spectral selectivity via the Zeeman shift, achieving ground-state cooling of radial modes with minimal impact on data qubit coherence.

**Method.** The protocol uses a sequence of microwave pi-pulses, 4-35 nm shelving, and Raman transitions to selectively manipulate Zeeman states of coolant ions, combined with periodic cooling pulses to suppress heating.

**Model / system.** A 5-ion chain of 171Yb+ ions trapped in a cryogenic surface trap (8 K), operating in the Lamb-Dicke regime.

**Key observables.** Average motional phonon number (n-bar), heating rates, qubit coherence time (T2), and resonance frequencies of the quadrupole transition.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Natural linewidth of 3 Hz for the quadrupole transition, Zeeman shift separation of 11 MHz, and a duty cycle of approximately 70%.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the Lamb-Dicke approximation holds for radial modes but not strictly for axial modes; assumes an upper bound for photon scattering based on ideal polarization matching.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Detection scheme and spectroscopy; Fig 2: Cooling sequence schematic; Fig 3: Demonstration of targeted cooling across all radial modes; Fig 5: Steady-state phonon number vs. pulse number; Fig 6: Spin-echo data showing coherence decay; Fig 7: Decay rate dependence on laser intensity and detuning; Fig 8: Laser amplitude stability (Allan deviation).

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of motional heating and existing cooling limitations, describes the experimental setup and the new spectral-selective protocol, demonstrates successful mode-specific cooling, analyzes the resulting decoherence in data ions, and provides a theoretical analysis of secondary error sources like light shifts and photon scattering.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-fidelity quantum logic operations in trapped ions often require the ions' collective motion to be cooled to near the ground state. Since cooling the ions' motion typically involves dissipative processes such as spontaneous photon scattering, sympathetic cooling is used on select coolant ions between gate sequences to cool the ion chain without affecting the data qubits. Common implementations for coolant ions include different atomic species, different isotopes of the same species or individually addressable ions. Each of these approaches have challenges associated with them, which include increased hardware complexity, reduced efficiency of radial mode cooling and re-ordering events which add additional experimental overhead. We demonstrate a sympathetic cooling scheme leveraging internal metastable atomic levels accessible via a narrow quadrupole transition, utilizing the natural Zeeman shift and individually addressed Raman transitions, to achieve isolation of the non-coolant or ``data ions" from coolant ions. We demonstrate modest decoherence of the data ions due to cooling, while preserving the coherence requirements for high-fidelity gate operations.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28040"></a>
### [Filter-assisted quantum subspace diagonalization via wavefunction sparsity engineering](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28040v1)

**Authors:** Han Xu, Tomonori Shirakawa, Seiji Yunoki  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28040v1>

<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic comparison between the standard SQD workflow and the proposed filter-assisted SQD (FSQD) protocol. In FSQD, a designed filter circuit C† Q (unitary ˆU † Q) transforms the original Hamiltonian ˆH into the filtered Hamiltonian ˆH′ := ˆU † Q ˆH ˆUQ, whose ground state is more strongly concentrated on the compu- tational basis state |0⟩= |0⟩⊗n. To avoid excessive sampling of the dominant |0⟩component, samplers for subspace expansion are constructed from the projected state—either by directly encoding the...</sub>

</details>

<img src="2605.28040_figures/2605.28040_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. (a) Matrix representations of the original Hamiltonian ˆH [Eq. <sub>FIG. 5. (a) Deviation of the Gini coefficient from unity, 1 −G, as a measure of wavefunction sparsity, plotted as a function of system size n. A fit to the ground state |ψg⟩of the original Hamiltonian ˆH gives (1 −G)N ∼20.7n, shown by the blue dashed line, whereas the filtered states generated by ˆUMPS,I and ˆUMPS,II approach the sparsest-case scaling, shown by the black dashed line. </details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new protocol called Filter-assisted SQD (FSQD) to solve the efficiency problems in quantum subspace diagonalization. By using a quantum circuit to transform the Hamiltonian, the method makes the ground-state wavefunction sparser, which drastically reduces the number of measurements needed to estimate energies accurately. The approach is validated through both large-scale numerical simulations and experiments on real quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a scalable framework for many-body calculations by using a quantum filter to reshape the measurement distribution, directly addressing the sampling bottleneck in quantum-centric algorithms for strongly correlated systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The intrinsic trade-off in sample-based quantum subspace diagonalization (SQD) between sampling efficiency and wavefunction sparsity, which causes exponential resource growth in strongly correlated systems.

**Main result.** The proposed Filter-assisted SQD (FSQD) protocol significantly enhances wavefunction sparsity and reduces ground-state energy estimation errors by orders of magnitude while substantially lowering sampling overhead.

**Method.** A protocol that uses a quantum filter (a unitary transformation of the Hamiltonian) implemented via a tensor-network-based circuit-encoding algorithm to concentrate ground-state weight onto fewer basis states.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on many-body systems in the strongly correlated regime, specifically using the quantum Ising model with transverse and longitudinal fields as a benchmark, with demonstrations on systems up to 100 qubits and hardware experiments on IBM's ibm_kobe processor.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy estimation error, Gini coefficient (sparsity measure), Lorenz curve, sampling overhead (number of shots), and subspace dimension.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Gini coefficient (G), subspace dimension (NR), number of measurement shots (NS), and the scaling exponent (g) of the sampling overhead.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The quantum state is prepared by applying a unitary operator to the zero state, and the accuracy depends on the fidelity of the tensor-network-based circuit encoding.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 compares standard SQD with FSQD variants; Figure 2 shows the piecewise-linear approximation of the Lorenz curve; Figure 3 illustrates the MPS circuit-encoding algorithm; Figure 9 shows optimization convergence; Figure 13 shows qubit layouts on the IBM hardware.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the SQD bottleneck, proposes the FSQD protocol, establishes theoretical resource bounds using the Gini coefficient, details the MPS-based circuit-encoding implementation, and validates the approach through numerical simulations and quantum hardware experiments.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Subspace diagonalization techniques based on quantum sampling, such as quantum selected configuration interaction (QSCI) and sample-based quantum diagonalization (SQD), have recently emerged as promising quantum-centric approaches for approximating ground-state energies of many-body systems. However, their performance is fundamentally limited by an intrinsic trade-off between sampling efficiency and the sparsity of the ground-state wavefunction, which becomes particularly severe in strongly correlated systems. Here, we introduce a filter-assisted SQD protocol that engineers wavefunction sparsity via a quantum filter, i.e., a unitary transformation of the Hamiltonian designed to concentrate the ground-state weight onto a small number of computational basis states. Using the Gini coefficient as a robust sparsity measure, we establish a quantitative relationship between wavefunction sparsity and the resource requirements of SQD, providing theoretical bounds on the required subspace dimension and sampling cost. To realize the quantum filter, we employ a tensor-network-based circuit-encoding algorithm that maps target states to quantum circuits with controllable fidelity. We benchmark our approach on the quantum Ising model with transverse and longitudinal fields using both numerical simulations and quantum hardware experiments. Our results demonstrate that, compared with standard SQD, the proposed protocol significantly enhances wavefunction sparsity, reduces ground-state energy estimation errors by orders of magnitude, and substantially lowers sampling overhead. These findings establish filter-assisted subspace diagonalization as a powerful and scalable framework for quantum many-body calculations in the strongly correlated regime.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28055"></a>
### [Cavity-Induced Suppression of Entanglement and Enhancement of Quantum Discord](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28055v1)

**Authors:** Shagun Kaushal, Harkirat Singh Sahota  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28055v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28055_figures/2605.28055_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Partial sums of the nonlocal correlation term |M (n) AB| as functions of the mode cutoff n for different values of the detector size parameter σ/R. The upper panels in each block show the oscillatory convergence behavior of the partial sums together with the oscillation midpoint estimate (dashed line), while the lower panels display the corresponding relative change, on a logarithmic scale. The top two rows correspond to Ωσ = 0.05 and ρ0/σ = 0.1, whereas the bottom two rows correspond to Ωσ = 3.0 and ρ0/σ = 2.0. The plots illustrate the oscillatory yet convergent nature of the nonlocal correlation series, with convergence becoming faster for larger values of σ/R.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28055_figures/2605.28055_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Behavior of the correlation terms XAA, XBB, |XAB|, and |MAB| as functions of ρ0/σ for different values of σ/R. The correlation term MAB decays rapidly and is strongly suppressed near the boundary, while XAB remains comparatively robust. The local term XBB is also reduced, leading to an asymmetry that explains the loss of entanglement and the persistence of discord and total correlations.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28055_figures/2605.28055_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: Density plots of N, I(A:B), and D(B|A) (left to right) as functions of the dimensionless parameters ρ0/σ and Ωσ in log scale, for different values of σ/R = 0.005, 0.025, 0.1 (top to bottom). In the left column, the black solid curve denotes the cavity entanglement boundary Ncavity = 0, while the red dashed curve represents the corresponding free-space boundary Nfree = 0; regions above these curves correspond to nonzero entanglement. For larger σ/R, the cavity significantly modifies the entanglement region due to the discrete mode structure, leading to deviations from the free-space result. As σ/R decreases, the cavity boundary approaches the free-space limit, although small residual...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28055_figures/2605.28055_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Harvested correlations in one-dimensional slices of the parameter space. The first row shows N, I(A:B), and D(B|A) as functions of ρ0/σ for fixed Ωσ(= 1) and varying σ/R, while the second row shows their dependence on Ωσ for fixed σ/R(= 0.005) and varying ρ0/σ. Entanglement exhibits sudden death, mutual information decays smoothly, and quantum discord remains robust, becoming non-monotonic with enhancement near the cavity boundary for larger σ/R.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that a cylindrical cavity can selectively suppress entanglement while enhancing quantum discord. It shows that boundary conditions break translational invariance and create a regime where more general non-classical correlations persist even after entanglement has vanished.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides fundamental insights into how spacetime boundaries and geometric confinement can be used to control the types of quantum correlations available, which is crucial for understanding entanglement harvesting and the robustness of non-classicality in open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how geometric confinement in a cylindrical cavity reshapes the hierarchy of quantum correlations, specifically whether the suppression of entanglement is accompanied by the preservation or enhancement of quantum discord.

**Main result.** The cavity suppresses distillable entanglement (negativity) and induces 'sudable death' at smaller separations, while simultaneously enhancing quantum discord near the cavity boundary.

**Method.** Perturbative expansion of the Unruh-DeWitt model up to second order in coupling strength, using the Wightman function for a massless scalar field with Dirichlet boundary conditions.

**Model / system.** Two stationary Unruh-DeWitt detectors (two-level systems) coupled to a massless scalar field confined within a perfectly reflecting cylindrical cavity of radius R and large length L.

**Key observables.** Entanglement negativity, quantum discord, and mutual information.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Cavity radius (R), detector separation (rho_0), detector energy gap (Omega), switching time (sigma), and the dimensionless ratio sigma/R.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The field is in the vacuum state, detectors are pointlike and stationary, the interaction is perturbative (O(lambda^2)), and the cavity is infinitely long.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the convergence of the nonlocal correlation term series; Figure 2 plots the behavior of various correlation terms (local noise, cross-correlations, and non-local correlations) as a function of detector separation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of correlation hierarchy, derives the reduced density matrix using the Unruh-DeWitt framework in a cylindrical geometry, analyzes the convergence of the mode expansion, presents numerical results for different correlation measures, and discusses the implications of boundary-induced asymmetry.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study correlations between two Unruh-DeWitt detectors coupled to a scalar field in a cylindrical cavity. Boundary conditions strongly modify the detector-correlation dynamics relative to free space. The entanglement negativity is suppressed in the cavity and vanishes for smaller separation as compared to the free space. Increasing the cavity radius does not recover the free-space behavior of the negativity. In contrast, mutual information and quantum discord remain nonzero over much larger separations. While the mutual information decays monotonically with separation, the quantum discord is enhanced near the cavity boundary. Our results demonstrate that geometric confinement can selectively suppress distillable entanglement while preserving and even enhancing more general non-classical correlations, providing a controlled setting to probe the hierarchy of correlations in quantum field theory.

</details>

### [Thermodynamic-limit dispersion relations on trapped-ion quantum hardware](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28599v1)

**Authors:** Lucas Marti, Sumeet, Stefan Wolf, K. P. Schmidt, Michael J. Hartmann  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28599v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `QC/QI experiment` **4/5** · `analog quantum simulation` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

**Summary.** This paper explores using a 20-qubit trapped-ion processor to compute many-body dispersion relations in the thermodynamic limit via a hybrid quantum-classical expansion technique. It demonstrates that while current hardware can successfully implement the necessary non-linear transformations, noise significantly biases the results and necessitates much higher gate fidelities for more complex algorithms like ASP.

**Why it may be interesting.** It addresses the critical challenge of how error propagation through non-linear classical post-processing affects the extraction of many-body properties from NISQ-era quantum devices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining if current noisy quantum hardware can accurately compute thermodynamic-limit dispersion relations using non-linear classical post-processing of quantum data.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that the NLCE+VQE framework is viable on current trapped-ion hardware, producing recognizable dispersion relations, though noise significantly biases results and ASP requires much lower error rates to be competitive.

**Method.** The authors combine Numerical Linked-Cluster Expansion (NLCE) with quantum algorithms (VQE and ASP) and use a novel CX-test for efficient measurement of matrix elements.

**Model / system.** The study uses a 20-qubit AQT Marmot trapped-ion quantum processing unit to simulate the Transverse-Field Ising Model (TFIM) in 1D chains, 1D chains with a longitudinal field, and 2D ladders.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energies and one-quasi-particle (1QP) dispersion relations omega(k).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Coupling strength J/h, longitudinal field strength hl, cluster size Nmax, and gate depolarization rates.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The perturbation is small enough to avoid quantum phase transitions or quasi-particle decays, and the hardware error is primarily modeled as depolarizing noise.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate the HVA ansatz, the CX-test circuit, the impact of depolarization noise on dispersion curves, and comparisons between QPU, simulation, and analytical results for various TFIM regimes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the NLCE+QA framework, details the PCAT post-processing and CX-test measurement technique, compares VQE and ASP algorithms, analyzes the impact of hardware noise and stability, and concludes with the requirements for reaching the classical limit.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We run a numerical linked-cluster expansion with a quantum algorithm (NLCE+QA), computing ground-state energies and one quasi-particle dispersions in the thermodynamic limit using a 20-qubit trapped-ion quantum processing unit (QPU). The NLCE+QA framework extracts thermodynamic-limit properties from small-cluster calculations, making it naturally suited for near-term quantum devices. Projector-based block-diagonalization schemes such as projective cluster-additive transformation (PCAT) are essential to NLCE+QA, and they involve matrix inversion and square root operations that amplify measurement noise. A central question is therefore whether current hardware can provide expectation values that are accurate enough to withstand non-linear classical post-processing. We explore this challenge for the transverse-field Ising model (TFIM) in one dimension, on a ladder geometry, as well as in a longitudinal field in one dimension. For the quantum algorithm, we consider adiabatic state preparation (ASP), as well as a variational quantum eigensolver (VQE) trained on a classical device. The final expectation values are obtained from the QPU, using a novel alternative to the Hadamard test that we name the CX-test. We explore the regimes currently attainable on quantum devices and comment on the improvements needed for quantum computers to achieve results beyond classical reach.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28404"></a>
### [On the existence of fully inseparable biseparable Gaussian states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28404v1)

**Authors:** Olga Leskovjanová, Klára Baksová, Jan Provazník, Ladislav Mišta,, Nicolai Friis  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28404v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `entanglement & information structure` **4/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28404_figures/2605.28404_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Graphical representation of sets of tripartite states with different separability properties. The convex hull of partition-separable states (II) forms the set of biseparable states, and its complement to the whole set of states is com- posed of states with GME (IV). The complement to the set of partition-separable states within the set of biseparable states is the set of FIB states (III). The union of the sets (III) and (IV) forms the set of fully inseparable states.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28404_figures/2605.28404_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Comparison of ranges of the squeezing parameter r for which the Gaussian state with CM γ (1) ABC is detected as fully inseparable (blue, bottom), and as genuinely multipartite en- tangled using different detection methods (top to bottom): the witness inequality (10), or the fully decomposable witness in the form of the SDP in (8) after local projection to three qubits (green, second from the top), to three qutrits (red, third from the top), and to three ququarts (yellow, fourth from the top).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28404_figures/2605.28404_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Comparison of ranges of the threshold squeezing pa- rameter r for which the Gaussian state with CM γ (0) ABC is de- tected as fully inseparable (blue, bottom), and as genuinely multipartite entangled using different detection methods (top to bottom): the witness inequality (10), or the fully decom- posable witness in the form of the SDP in (8) after local pro- jection to three qubits (green, second from the top) [31], to three qutrits (red, third from the top), and to three ququarts (yellow, fourth from the top).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28404_figures/2605.28404_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Comparison of regions where the Gaussian state with CM γ (3) ABC, Eq. (19), is detected as GME by the witness in- equality (10) (I), and by the fully decomposable witness W on qubits (II), qutrits (III), and ququarts (IV), inside the fully- inseparable region (V).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28404_figures/2605.28404_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Comparison of regions where the Gaussian state with CM γ (2) ABC is detected as GME by the witness inequality (10) (I), and by the fully decomposable witness W on qubits (II), qutrits (III), and ququarts (IV), inside the fully-inseparable region (V).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper explores whether Gaussian states can be fully inseparable without being genuinely multipartite entangled. By analyzing various three-mode Gaussian families using local projections to finite-dimensional subspaces, the authors find no evidence for such states and conjecture that all fully inseparable Gaussian states are genuinely multipartite entangled.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to quantum optics and continuous-variable quantum information as it addresses a fundamental question about the structure of entanglement in Gaussian states and suggests that standard covariance matrix-based tests might be sufficient for GME detection.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper investigates whether 'fully inseparable biseparable' (FIB) Gaussian states exist, specifically whether there are Gaussian states that are entangled across every bipartition but are still expressible as a convex mixture of states separable with respect to different partitions.

**Main result.** The authors find that for all tested families of three-mode Gaussian states, states appearing to be FIB are actually genuinely multipartite entangled (GME), leading to the conjecture that all fully inseparable Gaussian states are GME.

**Method.** The study uses the PPT criterion for entanglement detection, projections of Gaussian density matrices onto finite-dimensional Fock subspaces, and semidefinite programming (SDP) to construct fully decomposable witnesses.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on three-mode continuous-variable Gaussian states, including families constructed from mixtures of two-mode squeezed vacuum states with single-mode squeezed, thermal, or coherent states, as well as noisy GHZ-like Gaussian states.

**Key observables.** Squeezing parameter (r), mean photon number (n), displacement (alpha), and noise/mixing parameter (eta).

**Important parameters / regimes.** The dimension of the local projection subspaces (d=2, 3, 4) and the squeezing parameter (r).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is restricted to three-mode Gaussian states and relies on the observation that the gap between full inseparability and GME shrinks with increasing projection dimension to form a conjecture.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the hierarchy of multipartite entanglement; Figures 2-6 show detection ranges for various Gaussian state families (SMSV, thermal, coherent, and noisy GHZ) across different detection methods and subspace dimensions.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the distinction between full inseparability and GME, defines the Gaussian state models, describes the numerical methods for Fock-basis projection, presents results for various state families, and concludes with a conjecture and discussion of implications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Genuine multipartite entanglement and full inseparability are two inequivalent quantum resources. Even though all genuinely multipartite entangled states are also fully inseparable, not all fully inseparable states are genuinely multipartite entangled. There exist fully inseparable states that can be prepared as convex mixtures of states separable with respect to different bipartite splits. Here, we are interested in examples of Gaussian states that possess this type of entanglement, so-called fully inseparable biseparable states. We show for several archetypical families of multimode Gaussian states that fully inseparable biseparable candidate states are actually genuinely multipartite entangled. Using projections to finite-dimensional subspaces and fully decomposable witnesses, we observe a shrinking of the regions of potentially fully inseparable biseparable Gaussian states with growing dimension of the projection subspaces. We therefore conjecture that all fully inseparable Gaussian states are genuinely multipartite entangled.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Simulation of additive binding energies in asphalt using quantum-selected configuration interaction (QSCI)](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27640v1)

**Authors:** Karim Elgammal, Marc Maußner

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates a hybrid quantum-classical workflow called QuantumPave that uses a quantum processor to sample electronic configurations for calculating binding energies. By simulating a pyridine-phenol complex, the authors show that current NISQ-era hardware can achieve chemically relevant results for industrial materials science problems. The approach highlights the potential of quantum-centric supercomputing to solve complex molecular recognition tasks.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to demonstrate the feasibility of using a quantum-centric supercomputing workflow to predict additive binding energies in asphalt, a critical factor in road infrastructure durability.

**Main result.** The QSCI/SQD algorithm on the 54-qubit IQM Emerald processor exactly reproduced the active-space CASCI reference binding energy of -3.52 kcal/mol, proving that chemically meaningful results are attainable on current NISQ hardware.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical workflow (QuantumPave) using Quantum-Selected Configuration Interaction (QSCI) where a quantum processor samples electronic configurations and classical HPC performs diagonalization, integrated with machine-learning interatomic potentials (ORB v3) for geometry optimization.

**Model / system.** A 24-atom pyridine-phenol hydrogen-bonded complex (C11H11NO) serving as a representative model for asphalt binder interactions, mapped onto a 54-qubit IQM Emerald superconducting processor using a (10e, 10o) active space.

**Key observables.** Additive binding energy (in kcal/mol and eV).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Active space of 10 electrons in 10 orbitals; 5000 measurement shots; 10^-6 Ha energy tolerance; NISQ regime.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The discrepancy between calculated and experimental values is attributed to the use of a compact/limited active space; the study assumes the pyridine-phenol complex is a sufficient proxy for asphalt binder interactions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the molecular structure of the pyridine-phenol complex; Figure 2 displays the 3D optimized structure from ORB v3; Figure 3 illustrates the QSCI workflow loop.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the industrial problem of asphalt aging, presents the QuantumPave hybrid workflow, details the QSCI algorithm and hardware implementation, compares results against classical (CASCI, DFT) and experimental benchmarks, and concludes with future scaling directions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum-centric supercomputing (in which a quantum processor samples the dominant electronic configurations and classical high-performance computing resources perform the diagonalisation) is emerging as a practical route to correlated electronic-structure calculations. We present QuantumPave, a hybrid quantum-classical workflow for computing additive binding energies in asphalt binder, a quantity central to the oxidative ageing of road infrastructure. Using a 24-atom pyridine-phenol hydrogen-bonded complex as a representative model, we couple machine-learning interatomic potentials (ORB v3) for geometry optimisation with quantum-selected configuration interaction (QSCI), also referred to as sample based quantum diagonalisation (SQD), in a (10e, 10o) active space run on the 54-qubit IQM Emerald processor. On hardware, SQD reproduces the active-space CASCI reference exactly, giving a binding energy of -3.52 kcal/mol (-0.153 eV); the device noise broadens the sampling to span the active space, so no zero-noise extrapolation is required. This active-space value describes a physical hydrogen bond that underbinds the calorimetric enthalpy of about -6.25 kcal/mol, the difference reflecting the compact active space. We show that chemically meaningful binding energies for an industrially relevant materials problem are attainable on current quantum hardware within a quantum-centric supercomputing workflow.

</details>

### [Dissipative Spectral Form Factor of the Complex Elliptic Ginibre Ensemble across Various Non-Hermiticity Regimes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28319v1)

**Authors:** Gernot Akemann, Sung-Soo Byun, Seungjoon Oh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28319v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `driven-dissipative phase transition` **3/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `non-equilibrium universality` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28319_figures/2605.28319_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Phase diagrams of the DSFF in the (α, γ)–plane, where the non-Hermiticity and time scalings are given by 1 −τ = N −α and T = N γT, with T &gt; 0 fixed. The Thouless time TTh and the generalised Heisenberg time TH are indicated on the figure. (A) Dip–ramp– plateau structure of the DSFF across different time scales. (B) Universality crossover of the DSFF, showing GUE-type behaviour for γ ≤α and GinUE-type behaviour for α &lt; γ &lt; 1+α</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28319_figures/2605.28319_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. DSFF in the regime of strong non-Hermiticity (α = 0) with τ = 0.3 and θ = π/6. Here, all axes are on a logarithmic scale. Top: DSFF for N = 64 (green, dashed), together with the analytic limits for γ = 0 (red, solid) and γ = 1</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28319_figures/2605.28319_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. The plots illustrate the DSFF in the mesoscopic non-Hermiticity regime with pa- rameters N = 214, θ = π/6, and γ = 0.6, in all 3 figures. Accordingly, in case (A) with α &lt; γ, one observes the quadratic ramp κT2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28319_figures/2605.28319_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Decomposition of the positive real axis into the four asymptotic regimes for the Laguerre polynomials.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28319_figures/2605.28319_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Each plot compares the asymptotic behaviour of fN in the corresponding regime. In the regimes I, II, and III, the dotted, dot-dashed, and dashed curves represent the remainder obtained after subtracting the expansion up to the second term, for N = 8, 16, and 32 respec- tively, while the black solid curve shows the third-order term. In the regime II, the oscillatory component of the third-order term is further subtracted due to its explicit dependence on N. **Summary.** This paper provides a complete asymptotic characterization of the Dissipative Spectral Form Factor for the complex elliptic Ginibre ensemble. It maps out a phase diagram showing how the spectral statistics transition between Hermitian and non-Hermitian behaviors depending on the scaling of time and non-Hermiticity. The results rigorously define the scaling of key features like the Thouless and Heisenberg times.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers of open quantum systems as it provides a rigorous description of how dissipation and non-Hermiticity affect spectral correlations and the emergence of universal features like the ramp and plateau in quantum chaotic dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to derive the mathematically rigorous large-N asymptotic behavior of the Dissipative Spectral Form Factor (DSFF) for the complex elliptic Ginibre ensemble across various non-Hermiticity regimes.

**Main result.** The authors identify a complete phase diagram for the DSFF, characterizing the dip-ramp-plateau structure and the transition between GUE-type (linear ramp) and GinUE-type (quadratic ramp) statistics, including the scaling of Thouless and Heisenberg times.

**Method.** The study employs the framework of determinantal point processes, using orthogonal polynomial techniques, Christoffel-Darboux formulas, and uniform asymptotic expansions (Bessel, Airy, and exponential regimes) to analyze the large-N limit.

**Model / system.** The model is the Complex Elliptic Ginibre Ensemble (eGinUE), a non-Hermitian random matrix model that interpolates between the Hermitian GUE limit and the strongly non-Hermitian Ginibre limit, representing open, dissipative quantum chaotic systems.

**Key observables.** Dissipative Spectral Form Factor (DSFF), split into connected and disconnected components; Thouless time (T_Th); Heisenberg time (T_H).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Non-Hermiticity parameter (tau); scaling parameters (alpha and gamma) for the non-Hermiticity and time variables; matrix dimension (N).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is restricted to the large-N (asymptotic) limit and focuses on the simplest ensemble without additional symmetries.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1(a) shows the dip-ramp-plateau structure of the DSFF with characteristic time scales, and Figure 1(b) illustrates the universality crossover in the (alpha, gamma) phase plane; Figure 2 provides numerical illustration in the strong non-Hermiticity regime.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the definition of the eGinUE and the DSFF, followed by the decomposition into connected and disconnected components. It then proceeds through a detailed asymptotic analysis across four regimes (Bessel, Oscillatory, Airy, and Exponential) to identify the phase diagram and characteristic time scales, concluding with mathematical appendices on expansion coefficients.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study the dissipative spectral form factor (DSFF) at complex time $T e^{iθ}$ for the complex elliptic Ginibre ensemble with non-Hermiticity parameter $τ\in [0,1)$. As the matrix dimension $N \to \infty$, we consider the natural scalings in both the time variable and the non-Hermiticity parameter, namely $T = O(N^γ)$ and $1 - τ= O(N^{-α})$. For all regimes $γ\ge 0$ and $α\ge 0$, we derive the precise asymptotic behaviour of both the disconnected and connected components of the DSFF. In particular, we explicitly characterise the dip--ramp--plateau structure, including the dip time and the Heisenberg time. In addition, we identify the mesoscopic regime $α\in (0,1)$, which interpolates between the behaviour of the DSFF of non-Hermitian random matrices and the spectral form factor (SFF) of Hermitian ensembles. We further provide an explicit description of the phase diagram, in which the ramp exhibits quadratic, linear, or intermediate behaviour depending on the scaling parameters.

</details>

### [Superradiant LIDAR](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28378v1)

**Authors:** T. Kullick, M. Bojer, J. von Zanthier, G. S. Agarwal  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28378v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `Dicke superradiance` **2/5** · `free-space correlated emission` **2/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5** · `superradiant laser` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28378_figures/2605.28378_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Sketch of a practical implementation of Superradiant LI- DAR. N equidistant TLS emit light that is separated by a beam split- ter. The transmitted light is detected by m −1 detectors at a known distance. The reflected light propagates towards a remote object. After being reflected by the remote object, the light travels towards the mth detector. Correlating the photons of all m detectors yields a measurement of the mth-order correlation function G(m) N (δ1, δ2), from which the unknown distance to the remote object can be extracted. Note that the measurement is insensitive to atmospheric turbulence.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28378_figures/2605.28378_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. (a) Conceptual scheme of Superradiant LIDAR. N equidistantly spaced, identical, statistically independent TLS are placed at positions Rl, l ∈{1, . . . , N}, along the x-axis. The radiated light is detected by m detectors at positions r j, j ∈{1, . . . , m}, in the far field. The mth detector is placed at an unknown distance z2 under a variable angle ϑ2 ≈x2/z2, whereas the remaining m −1 detectors are placed at a distance z1 under the angle ϑ1 ≈x1/z1. (b) The mth-order correlation function of N TLS of the Superradiant LIDAR setup ˜G(m) N (δ1, δ2) is shown for the cases N = m = 2 (purple) and N = m = 10 (blue), highlighting that higher-order correlation functions of N &gt; 2 light...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28378_figures/2605.28378_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. Fisher information F (sup) z2 of Superradiant LIDAR as a func- tion of the correlation order m ∈{2, 3, . . . , 20} and the number of TLS N ∈{2, 3, . . . , 20}. The special case N = m = 2 (green rectangle), equivalent to two-photon LIDAR, marks the minimum of F (sup) z2 .</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28378_figures/2605.28378_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Detailed proposal for a Superradiant LIDAR setup. For details, see text. GGD: rotating ground-glass disk, BS: beam splitter, CCD: charge-coupled device camera.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28378_figures/2605.28378_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. The relative difference ∆Frel between the numerically evaluated Fisher information of Superradiant LIDAR (Eq. (C7)) and the approximated Fisher information in Eq. (D5) displayed for N ∈{4, 5, . . . , 20} and m ∈{2, 3, . . . , 20}. The cases N = 2, 3 are not shown, as analytical solutions are presented in Eqs. (6) and (7) in the main text, respectively. All deviations are below 9 %, and the approximate form always underestimates the numerical results.</sub>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new LIDAR technique called Superradiant LIDAR that uses multiple independent thermal light sources and higher-order intensity correlations. This method significantly improves distance measurement sensitivity, outperforming traditional LIDAR by a factor proportional to the number of light sources used.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates how exploiting collective effects (superradiance) and higher-order correlations can significantly enhance parameter estimation precision, which is a core theme in quantum metrology and quantum optics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Enhancing the sensitivity of Light Detection and Ranging (LIDAR) for measuring the distance of remote objects beyond the limits of traditional and coherent LIDAR.

**Main result.** The proposed Superradiant LIDAR achieves a Cramér-Rao bound that is undercut by a factor of N compared to traditional LIDAR, with sensitivity further improved by increasing the correlation order m.

**Method.** The authors use analytical derivations and numerical evaluations of the m-th order photon correlation function and Fisher information, developing a high-precision fit function to describe the scaling behavior.

**Model / system.** A system of N statistically independent thermal light sources (TLS) placed equidistantly along an axis, where light is reflected by a remote object and detected using m-th order intensity correlations.

**Key observables.** m-th order correlation function (G(m)_N) and Fisher information (F_{z2}^{(sup)})

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of light sources (N), correlation order (m), unknown distance (z2), and phase differences (delta_i)

**Assumptions / limitations.** Measurements follow Poisson statistics, light sources are identical and uncorrelated, and pixel width is small enough for the phase approximation to hold.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental schematic; Figure 2 compares correlation functions and shows the steeper slopes for higher N; Figure 3 highlights the significant gains in Fisher information for larger N and m; Figure 4 compares the accuracy of the analytical lower bound versus the proposed fit function.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the LIDAR problem, proposes the Superradiant LIDAR scheme, provides the mathematical framework for correlation functions, derives analytical bounds and fits for Fisher information, and validates these with numerical simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In recent years, light detection and ranging (LIDAR) has seen a steep rise in the sensitivity of measuring the distances of remote objects. Here, we propose to enhance the sensitivity of LIDAR even further by exploiting Dicke's concept of superradiance, i.e., the collective light emission of statistically independent light sources. By using $N$ thermal light sources (TLS) and measuring intensity correlations of order $m \geq 2$ instead of $m=1$, i.e., the intensity, we show that the Cramér-Rao bound on the measurement of the distance of a remote object undercuts that of traditional LIDAR by a factor of $N$, and can be reduced further with increasing correlation order $m$. Our numerical calculations are supported by analytical expressions for the special cases of two and three TLS and a general approximate expression for any number of TLS.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper examines how steady bias and random magnetic fields influence the rate of transitions between states in a kinetic Ising model. It finds that while moderate noise helps overcome energy barriers, excessive noise leads to a loss of directional control. The results highlight how stochastic driving can effectively minimize finite-size effects in the system's dynamics.

**Why it may be interesting.** The study of noise-induced transitions and the modulation of energy barriers by stochastic and bias fields is highly relevant to understanding decoherence and switching dynamics in open quantum systems and driven many-body systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how a combination of a weak steady bias field and time-dependent Gaussian random fields affects barrier crossing and switching dynamics in a two-state system.

**Main result.** Moderate stochastic fields lower the effective energy barrier and facilitate transitions, while strong noise induces backflow and reduces directional selectivity; additionally, stochastic driving suppresses finite-size effects.

**Method.** Monte Carlo simulations using single-spin heat-bath dynamics on a 2D square lattice.

**Model / system.** A kinetic Ising model on a 2D square lattice with periodic boundary conditions, subject to a Hamiltonian with ferromagnetic coupling, a constant bias field H, and a time-dependent Gaussian random field h_r(t).

**Key observables.** Probability distribution of magnetization P(m), time-averaged magnetization Q, and average first-passage time <tau>.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), noise intensity (D), bias field (H), and system size (L).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The stochastic magnetic field is assumed to be spatially homogeneous (uniform across the lattice).

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows a phase diagram in the (D, T) plane; Fig 2 displays P(m) for various parameters; Fig 3 compares P(m) for different system sizes; Fig 4 shows <tau> vs D for various H and T; Fig 5 demonstrates suppression of finite-size effects; Fig 6 shows <tau> vs H.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the model and its phase diagram, explores the effect of noise and bias on the magnetization distribution, analyzes first-passage times and their dependence on noise and bias, and concludes with a discussion on system-size dependence and optimal transition conditions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study barrier crossing in a two-state system, namely the kinetic Ising model, in the presence of a weak bias field and spatially homogeneous, but time-dependent, Gaussian random fields. We find that the bias field determines the location of the dominant maxima of the probability distribution function of the magnetization, whereas the noise intensity controls their sharpness and stability of the distribution. A moderate stochastic field lowers the effective energy barrier and facilitates transitions between ordered states, while strong noise induces broad distributions and significant backflow, which reduces directional selectivity. Our results suggest that efficient barrier crossing requires a balanced combination of moderate stochastic driving and controlled bias.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28445"></a>
### [The Resetting Heat Engine: A Thermodynamic Cycle of Thermal Expansion and Compression](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28445v1)

**Authors:** Oded Farago  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28445v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5** · `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28445_figures/2605.28445_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Entropy production for stochastic resetting in the anharmonic potential U(x) = x4/4, obtained from Langevin simulations with T = 1, γ = 1, and tc = 0.01. The black solid curve shows the entropy production per cycle, ∆SKL, as a function of the resetting time t, while the red dashed curve shows the corresponding entropy production rate for periodic resetting, r∆SKL (r = 1/t).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper treats stochastic resetting as a thermodynamic cycle of thermal expansion and compression. It demonstrates that in harmonic potentials, the entropy production of this cycle can be simplified to a state-function-like expression, and shows that the resulting thermodynamic features are robust even in anharmonic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant for researchers in open quantum systems and non-equilibrium dynamics, as it provides a thermodynamic framework for understanding the irreversibility and entropy production in systems subject to non-unitary, resetting-like dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to develop a thermodynamic characterization of stochastic resetting processes, specifically addressing whether entropy production can be reduced from complex trajectory-based measures to simpler state-function-like expressions.

**Main result.** The author proves that for a harmonic potential, entropy production reduces exactly to an expression determined by endpoint distributions, and shows that the observed thermodynamic features are qualitatively generic for anharmonic potentials.

**Method.** The study uses the Kullback-Leibler divergence between forward and reversed trajectory ensembles and employs Langevin simulations for anharmonic cases.

**Model / system.** A Brownian particle subject to overdamped Langevin dynamics in a one-dimensional potential (primarily harmonic, but also tested with a quartic potential) that undergoes stochastic resetting to the origin at periodic or Poissonian intervals.

**Key observables.** Entropy production (total, rate, and cycle-based), variance, probability current, and effective temperature.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Resetting rate (r), relaxation time (tau), damping coefficient (gamma), and the distinction between periodic and Poissonian resetting regimes.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes the reset does not produce a perfect delta-function (finite width) and that the endpoint-based description serves as a lower bound for the full trajectory-level entropy production.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Langevin simulation results for an anharmonic potential, plotting entropy production per cycle and the entropy production rate as functions of cycle time.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the thermodynamic cycle analogy, develops the analytical framework for harmonic potentials using KL divergence, extends the analysis to entropy production rates for different resetting protocols, and validates the findings via simulations of anharmonic potentials.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We consider a Brownian particle confined by an external potential and subject to stochastic resetting to the origin. Motivated by the repetitive nature of the dynamics, we describe the process as a thermodynamic cycle of thermal expansion and collapse, analyzed via a framework based on the Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence between forward and reversed trajectory ensembles. While the entropy production generally depends on the full trajectory ensemble and cannot be reduced to thermodynamic state variables alone, we show that the harmonic potential constitute a special case, where the entropy production reduces exactly to a state-function-like expression determined solely by the distributions before and after resetting. Explicit analytical results are derived for periodic and Poissonian resetting. At low resetting rates $r$, the entropy production rate grows linearly with $r$ and is proportional to the symmetric KL divergence between the reset and equilibrium distributions. At very high rates, the resetting process becomes effectively perpetual and the entropy production vanishes. Langevin simulations for an anharmonic quartic potential display the same generic behavior, indicating that these features are not restricted to harmonic confinement. Our results establish a direct connection between stochastic resetting, thermodynamic cycles, and information-theoretic measures of irreversibility.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Exceptional points in diamond optomechanics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27536v1)

**Summary.** This paper reports the first experimental access to an exceptional point in a diamond optomechanical crystal. By using engineered structural asymmetry, the authors demonstrate how to tune mechanical mode hybridization and redistribute optomechanical damping. This provides a new platform for non-Hermitian physics and topological dynamics in diamond-based quantum technologies.

**Why it may be interesting.** It demonstrates experimental control over non-Hermitian singularities and eigenvector rotation, which is highly relevant for developing direction-dependent quantum state transfer and topological protection in hybrid quantum networks.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The experimental achievement and utilization of exceptional points (EPs) in diamond optomechanical systems to leverage non-Hermitian phenomena like chiral dynamics and topological state transfer.

**Main result.** The researchers successfully reached an exceptional point in a diamond optomechanical crystal by using structural symmetry breaking to couple two mechanical modes, observing asymmetric redistribution of damping and branch-switching topology.

**Method.** The study uses structural asymmetry (sidewall angles) to mix mechanical modes and employs a fiber taper for evanescent coupling, with data analysis involving coupled-mode model fitting and COMSOL simulations.

**Model / system.** A diamond optomechanical crystal (OMC) nanobeam where a single optical cavity mode is coupled to two high-frequency mechanical resonances (breathing and flexural modes) via radiation pressure and structural symmetry breaking.

**Key observables.** Mechanical power spectral density (PSD), mechanical eigenfrequencies, damping rates, and optical transmission spectra.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Optical wavelength ~1565 nm, mechanical frequencies ~10.1 GHz, optomechanical coupling rates ~290 kHz, and the transition from weak coupling to the exceptional point and phonon-lasing threshold.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes a strong coherent intracarray field, neglects small radiation-pressure-induced cavity resonance shifts, and approximates mechanical frequency shifts as linear with photon number.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental setup and conceptual EP evolution; Figure 2 displays the SEM image and simulated mode profiles; Figure 3 illustrates Bloch-sphere trajectories and stability maps; Figure 4 shows the 2D map of eigenfrequencies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of EPs in optomechanics, describes the diamond platform and fabrication-induced symmetry breaking, presents the experimental observation of the EP and its topological signatures, and discusses the implications for tunable spin-mechanical coupling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Multimode cavity optomechanical systems allow light to couple otherwise non-interacting mechanical resonators, enabling non-Hermitian phenomena such as exceptional points, where eigenfrequencies and eigenvectors of coupled modes coalesce. Accessing an exceptional point and its nearby parameter space is a first step towards chiral mode dynamics and topological state transfer. Diamond optomechanical devices support strong coherent optomechanical coupling required to tune resonances to an exceptional point, as well as strain-coupling to spin-defects for hybrid quantum technologies, but have not yet been used for multimode non-Hermitian physics. Here we tune to an exceptional point in a diamond optomechanical crystal, which uses structural symmetry breaking to produce two high-frequency mechanical resonances coupled to an optical cavity. The exceptional point is reached within a stable operating window below the phonon-lasing threshold, and we observe asymmetric redistribution of optomechanical damping and anti-damping between hybridized modes. These results establish diamond optomechanical crystals as a platform for non-Hermitian optomechanics, opening routes to topological mechanical dynamics in hybrid spin-phonon interfaces.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28090"></a>
### [Hall effect in multi-leg bosonic ladders](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28090v1)

**Authors:** Edmond Orignac, Roberta Citro, Thierry Giamarchi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum gases · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28090v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `analog quantum simulation` **3/5** · `Keldysh / 2PI / non-Gaussian methods` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28090_figures/2605.28090_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Temperature dependence of the Hall imbalance in the Meissner phase (Red) in the Vortex phase (Blue) and at the commensurate-incommensurate transition (Green).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a theoretical generalization of the Hall effect in multi-leg bosonic ladders using bosonization. It establishes a universal connection between Hall resistance and the density derivative of charge stiffness and characterizes how temperature affects the Hall response in Meissner and Vortex phases.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is highly relevant for many-body dynamics and AMO physics as it provides analytical predictions for transport properties (Hall effect) in synthetic gauge fields, which are a key component of ultracold atom experiments in optical lattices.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to generalize the Hall effect and Hall resistance from two-leg to N-leg bosonic ladders and to understand the temperature dependence of the Hall response in different magnetic flux regimes.

**Main result.** The Hall resistance is found to be proportional to the derivative of the logarithm of the charge stiffness with respect to density, a result that is robust for N-leg ladders. Additionally, Hall imbalance corrections are exponentially small in the Meissner phase at low temperatures but follow a power law in the Vortex phase.

**Method.** The authors use bosonization and perturbation theory in the band curvature, employing a basis transformation with Hahn/Chebyshev polynomials to decouple modes and Green's function techniques for temperature-dependent analysis.

**Model / system.** The system consists of interacting bosonic N-leg ladders threaded by a magnetic flux (artificial gauge fields), modeled using a bosonized Hamiltonian including Tomonaga-Luttinger Liquid terms, transverse interchain hopping, and band curvature.

**Key observables.** Hall resistance (Rh), Hall voltage (Vh), Hall imbalance (Ph), and charge stiffness (uK).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic flux (B), temperature (T), particle density (rho0), transverse hopping (t_perp), and Tomonaga-Luttinger parameter (K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a perturbative expansion in band curvature and focuses on the low-field limit and the validity of the bosonization approach for the ladder geometries.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the temperature dependence of the Hall imbalance in the Meissner phase, Vortex phase, and at the commensurate-incommensurate transition for various magnetic flux values.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of N-leg ladders, applies bosonization and basis transformations to derive Hall resistance formulas, analyzes the temperature dependence of the Hall imbalance in different flux regimes, and discusses the robustness of the results.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We use bosonization to analyze the ground state Hall response of interacting bosonic N-leg ladders threaded by a flux. We derive an explicit expression of the Hall imbalance in a perturbative expansion in the band curvature, retaining fully the interactions. For small magnetic field the Hall resistance is proportional to the derivative of the logarithm of the charge stiffness with respect to density, generalizing the result obtained in the two leg case. We also consider the effect of temperature, and establish that at low temperature, corrections to the Hall resistance are exponentially small in the Meissner phase.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28289"></a>
### [Mechanical Squeezed-Fock Gravimeter](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28289v1)

**Authors:** Rozhin Yousefjani, Saif Al-Kuwari  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28289v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28289_figures/2605.28289_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Coherent performance of the MSFQ gravimeter. (a) Time- normalized sensitivity √</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28289_figures/2605.28289_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Decoherent performance of the MSFQ gravimeter, for m=10−9 kg, ω/2π=1 kHz, and δ/ω=0.05 throughout. (a) Compe- tition ratio Ξ≡Γeff/ωb as a function of r at fixed D/Dcrit=0.2, for var- ious γ0’s. The dashed line Ξ=1 marks the crossover between the coherent (Ξ ≪1) and decoherent (Ξ ≫1) regimes. (b) QFI FQ(g, t) as a function of time at fixed D/Dcrit=0.2 and r=1, for various γ0’s and the coherent reference (γ0=0, dashed). (c) Optimized sensitiv- ity √</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a new type of quantum gravimeter using squeezed-Fock states in a levitated mechanical system. By using a two-phonon pump to create squeezing, the gravitational signal is exponentially amplified. The study highlights a fundamental trade-off where squeezing enhances the signal but also converts mechanical damping into anisotropic noise.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a novel application of squeezed-state engineering and open quantum system dynamics (anisotropic noise) to enhance the precision of a quantum sensor.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing high-sensitivity, compact quantum gravimeters that overcome the mass-independent sensitivity bottleneck and the weak intrinsic nonlinearity of nanomechanical resonators.

**Main result.** The authors propose a mechanical squeezed-Fock qubit gravimeter that provides exponential signal amplification via the anti-squeezed quadrature while preserving mass scaling, though it introduces a trade-off with anisotropic decoherence.

**Method.** The study uses Bogoliubov transformations, the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA), and Quantum Estimation Theory (calculating QFI and CFI) to analyze sensitivity and optimal readout strategies.

**Model / system.** A levitated mesoscopic mechanical particle modeled as a Duffing oscillator driven by a detuned two-phonon pump, described by an effective Hamiltonian in a squeezed-Fock basis and a master equation for zero-temperature damping.

**Key observables.** Gravity-induced transition rate, excited squeezed-Fock population, Bloch vector components, and Quantum/Classical Fisher Information.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameter (r), Duffing nonlinearity (D), mechanical damping rate (gamma_0), detuning (delta), and pump amplitude (Ap).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of the Rotating-Wave Approximation (RWA) and the assumption of a weak-force regime where the gravity-induced coupling is much smaller than the qubit gap.

**Figures summary.** Figures include a map of the RWA validity in the (r, D/D_crit) plane and a demonstration of the locally optimal projective readout for the decoherent gravimeter.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific problem of gravimetry, defines the mechanical squeezed-Fock model and Hamiltonian, analyzes the signal enhancement via squeezing, evaluates the impact of decoherence on sensitivity, and determines the optimal measurement readout strategy.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Levitated mechanical systems are promising candidates for quantum gravimetry, as gravity couples directly to their center-of-mass motion, enabling the large mass of a mesoscopic particle to serve as a sensing resource. In this paper, we propose a mechanical squeezed-Fock qubit gravimeter using a Duffing oscillator that is driven by a detuned two-phonon pump. In the squeezed-Fock basis, the gravitational force couples to the anti-squeezed quadrature, which enhances the gravity-induced transition rate while preserving the direct mass scaling of the mechanical force coupling. We show that sensitivity improves with reduced effective qubit splitting that is controlled by the squeezing parameter and the Duffing nonlinearity. We further analyze mechanical damping and show that squeezing converts ordinary dissipation into anisotropic qubit noise, setting a practical trade-off between signal amplification and decoherence rate. These results identify the mechanical squeezed-Fock qubit as a new platform for quantum-enhanced gravimetry.

</details>

### [Chirped-pulse engineering for robust control of single-molecule orientation in a cavity](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28511v1)
