**Summary.** This paper investigates how chirped laser pulses can be used to control the orientation of a single molecule inside an optical cavity. It shows that by engineering the chirp rates, one can achieve high degrees of orientation and robust control that is resistant to frequency detuning. This work offers a new strategy for manipulating molecular polaritons in cavity-based quantum systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides new insights into using spectral-phase modulation to control molecular polaritons and demonstrates how chirped pulses can activate multiphoton processes that deviate from standard analytical approximations in cavity QED.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Achieving robust and precise coherent control over the orientation of a single molecule within an optical cavity using chirped-pulse engineering.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that chirped pulses can precisely control molecular orientation, reaching a maximum degree of 0.5773, and that equal-chirp configurations provide superior robustness against frequency detuning.

**Method.** The authors use numerical integration of the time-dependent wave function and compare results with the first-order Magnus expansion to analyze population redistribution and multiphoton processes.

**Model / system.** A single carbonyl sulfide (OCS) molecule strongly coupled to a single-mode optical cavity, modeled using an extended Jaynes-Cummings model incorporating rotational states and a three-level polariton system.

**Key observables.** The degree of molecular orientation <cos theta> and the final populations of the polariton states.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Chirp rates (beta plus and beta minus), pulse amplitude (A), pulse detuning (delta), and the strong coupling regime (g0/omega_c <= 0.1).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the ability to achieve photon blockade of higher dressed states to isolate a three-state system and neglects quadratic terms like cavity-field self-energy.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows energy level diagrams in bare and dressed bases; Fig 2 shows orientation dependence on pulse amplitude and chirp; Fig 4 illustrates the orientation landscape and symmetry; Fig 5 compares numerical results to Magnus expansion; Fig 6 maps orientation robustness against detuning.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical system and model, presents the pulse engineering method, analyzes the results in weak and strong field regimes, evaluates the breakdown of analytical approximations, and concludes with a robustness analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a theoretical investigation of coherent control over the orientation of an individual molecule strongly coupled with a cavity using chirped-pulse driving. Specifically, we explore the dynamics of carbonyl sulfide (OCS) molecules under the influence of two chirped pulses with different spectral phases. We compare two pulse configurations: one with equal chirp rates ($β_{+} = β_{-}$) and another with unequal chirp rates ($β_{+} \neq β_{-}$). Numerical simulations reveal that chirped pulses enable precise control of the molecular orientation, achieving a maximum orientation degree of 0.5773. By analyzing the distribution of molecular polariton states, we show that chirped pulses can activate multiphoton processes, leading to deviations from the predictions of first-order Magnus expansion methods. Additionally, we demonstrate the robustness of the maximum orientation with respect to chirp amplitude and detuning, providing insights into the role of pulse parameters in optimizing control. This work introduces a new strategy for controlling molecular orientation in cavity-based systems and offers valuable perspectives for future experimental applications.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28206"></a>
### [Digital Quantum Simulation of the quantum $β$-FPUT Lattice: Formulation and Resource Estimation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28206v1)

**Authors:** Kiratholly Nandakumar Madhav Sharma, Juan Manuel Aguiar Hualde, Julian van Velzen, Phalgun Lolur

The controlled phase gates are Φ2 = exp[−i(κ/2)∆2 0∆t/ℏ] and Φ4 = exp[−i(β/4)∆4 0∆t/ℏ], acting on the reference qubit r0 and controlled by the ancilla.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new method for simulating the quantum beta-FPUT lattice on future fault-tolerant quantum computers. By using a first-quantized approach and a clever decomposition of displacement operators, the authors reduce the circuit complexity and provide a detailed estimate of the necessary qubits and gates. This work establishes a blueprint for studying complex heat transport and phonon dynamics in nonlinear low-dimensional systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a scalable algorithmic approach to simulating nonlinear many-body dynamics and anomalous transport, specifically addressing how to bypass the high overheads of bosonic encoding in quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Developing an efficient digital quantum simulation framework for the quantum beta-FPUT lattice and estimating the resources required for fault-tolerant implementation.

**Main result.** The authors provide a concrete algorithmic blueprint and resource roadmap, demonstrating that a first-quantized approach using quadrature decomposition enables shallow circuits for simulating nonlinear transport dynamics.

**Method.** A first-quantized digital quantum simulation using Trotterized circuit blocks, a Hermitian quadrature decomposition of Fourier-mode displacement operators, and a Hadamard-test-based measurement workflow.

**Model / system.** A one-dimensional quantum beta-Fermi-Pasta-Ulam-Tsingou (beta-FPUT) lattice, characterized by a Hamiltonian with kinetic energy and a potential containing both quadratic and quartic anharmonic terms.

**Key observables.** Mode-resolved displacement correlators and quantum dynamical observables relevant to phonon lifetimes.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice size (N), grid resolution (bits per site), mass (m), harmonic coupling (kappa), anharmonicity (beta), and Trotter step size (delta t).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes fault-tolerant quantum hardware rather than NISQ-era devices and assumes periodic boundary conditions for the lattice.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows motivation regarding heat dissipation in microelectronics and bio-devices; Figure 2 depicts the 1D FPUT chain; Figure 4 illustrates a two-site potential-layer circuit; Figure 5 shows circuits for implementing cosine and sine quadrature unitaries.

**Paper structure.** The paper progresses from physical motivation and the mathematical formulation of the beta-FPUT model to the construction of the quantum simulation framework, followed by error analysis, resource estimation, and a comparison with hardware roadmaps.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Heat conduction in low-dimensional systems exhibits strong deviations from Fourier behavior due to anharmonicity and long-lived vibrational correlations, challenging conventional computational approaches. The $β$-Fermi--Pasta--Ulam--Tsingou ($β$-FPUT) chain provides a minimal nonlinear lattice model for studying anomalous transport, yet its quantum real-time dynamics remain difficult to access with classical methods. We develop a first-quantized digital quantum-simulation framework for the quantum $β$-FPUT lattice, targeting fault-tolerant quantum computers. By working directly with discretized lattice displacements rather than truncated phonon occupation spaces, the approach captures anharmonic interactions while avoiding bosonic encoding overheads. We construct Trotterized circuit blocks for real-time evolution and introduce a Hermitian quadrature decomposition of Fourier-mode displacement operators that enables shallow quantum circuits for mode-resolved displacement correlators. We analyze the quantum resources required for the full simulation and measurement workflow, providing qubit counts, gate complexity, circuit-depth and resource estimates as functions of system size and resolution within a fault-tolerant workflow. These results establish a concrete algorithmic blueprint for simulating quantum transport dynamics in nonlinear low-dimensional lattice models on fault-tolerant quantum hardware.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28702"></a>
### [Global Kochen-Specker Contextuality Without Local Contextuality and Generalized Bell Nonlocality](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28702v1)

**Authors:** Ming Yang  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28702v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28702_figures/2605.28702_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28702_figures/2605.28702_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28702_figures/2605.28702_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28702_figures/2605.28702_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28702_figures/2605.28702_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces the concept of global contextuality, demonstrating that multipartite systems can exhibit nonclassical behavior that cannot be explained by a single global hidden-variable model, even when all local parts appear classical. Through three different bipartite constructions, the author shows that nonclassicality can emerge as a compositional obstruction when local classical descriptions cannot be 'glued' together.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a new fundamental classification of nonclassicality (global contextuality) that is distinct from standard Bell nonlocality and local Kochen-Specker contextuality, which is relevant for understanding the limits of classical simulations of multipartite quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the existence of 'global contextuality,' where a multipartite system fails to admit a single global noncontextual hidden-variable model even though all local subsystems are noncontextual and all multipartite contexts admit a generalized local hidden-variable description.

**Main result.** The paper proves that the absence of local contextuality and generalized Bell nonlocality does not imply the existence of a global noncontextual hidden-variable model, establishing global contextuality as a compositional obstruction to classicality.

**Method.** The author uses an operational approach involving three bipartite constructions (polarization-path, qubit-qutrit, and flagged qutrit Werner-local) and employs linear programming, quantifier elimination, and postselection to identify violations of global hidden-variable models.

**Model / system.** The study considers various bipartite measurement scenarios, including a 2x4 polarization-path system, a 2x3 qubit-qutrit system with KCBS observables, and a qutrit Werner-local state with a product flag.

**Key observables.** KCBS operator, polarization observables, and a Hardy-type linear witness.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Schmidt coefficients (h0, h1), weighting parameter (a), and the KCBS noncontextuality threshold (c0 <= 0.850651).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The claim regarding the absence of generalized nonlocality is restricted to specific measurement scenarios without cross-party joint measurements.

**Figures summary.** The paper includes a summary table (Table I) classifying the three different separation constructions based on their hidden resources and qualifications.

**Paper structure.** The paper defines the hierarchy of classicality, establishes a gluing criterion for global contextuality, and then presents three distinct constructions (incompatibility-based, algebraic/postselected, and state-level) to demonstrate the separation of scales.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

A set of quantum data can look classical in every local test and still fail to admit a single classical explanation of the whole composite system. We formulate this failure as global contextuality. Here global means global in the physical sense of the whole multipartite system, not the local/global terminology of sheaf theory. Each party's local statistics are noncontextual and each measured multipartite context admits a generalized local hidden-variable description, but the GLHV block descriptions cannot be promoted to a single noncontextual hidden-variable model for the whole system. Three bipartite constructions exhibit this separation. A polarization-path construction gives a direct global obstruction. A qubit-qutrit KCBS construction gives an algebraic scenario-level example, with explicit formulas for the unconditional KCBS operator, the correlation-polytope constraints, and the postselected violation. A flagged qutrit Werner-local state gives a state-level example: the state is entangled and local for all projective measurements, its local qutrit marginals do not violate KCBS, yet postselection rules out a single GNCHV model. We also spell out the classical composition lemma: classical conditional hidden variables can be absorbed into a larger hidden variable, whereas quantum contextual data need not allow such a factorization. Within the general Bell-type framework considered here, with arbitrary parties and arbitrary local compatible contexts, but no cross-party joint measurements, the absence of local contextuality and GLHV-type generalized Bell nonlocality does not imply the existence of a global noncontextual hidden-variable model. Global contextuality is thus a compositional obstruction to classical explanation.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27775"></a>
### [Towards entanglement-enhanced probing of atomic parity violation](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27775v1)

**Authors:** Maxim Sirotin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27775v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **3/5** · `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27775_figures/2605.27775_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. (a) Photon- and Z0-mediated electron–nucleus interactions. The weak neutral-current interaction violates parity and mixes opposite-parity atomic states. (b) Standard Model tests versus momentum transfer, adapted from Refs. [1, 6]. APV provides the low-energy limit and probes the nuclear weak charge. (c) Neutral-Yb APV level scheme. The 408 nm 6s2 1S0 →5d6s 3D1 transition is driven in external fields, producing Stark–PV interference (with 3D1 and 1P1 mixing). Atoms excited to 3D1 partially decay to 6s6p 3P0. The 3P0 population is read out by 649 nm excitation to 6s7s 3S1 and detection of cascade fluorescence, including 649 nm, 680 nm, and 556 nm photons. (d) Atomic-beam APV...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27775_figures/2605.27775_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Projected metrological sensitivity to an APV isotope-chain deviation. (a) Parameter uncertainty δθ versus total atom number for 1 h of averaging. SQL measurements scale as N −1/2, while the ideal cross-isotope cat protocol reaches the Heisenberg limit δθ ∝N −1. Squeezed and same-isotope cat protocols give intermediate performance set by their assumed contrast and preparation errors. (b) Uncertainty δθ versus total averaging time for N = 1000. Statistical gains reduce the short-time uncertainty, but the total uncertainty saturates at the systematic floor.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how quantum entanglement can be used to enhance the precision of atomic parity violation measurements in isotope chains. It demonstrates that while cross-isotope entangled states are theoretically optimal for detecting new physics, practical limitations like systematic errors and decoherence must be carefully managed.

**Why it may be interesting.** It bridges quantum information theory (entanglement-enhanced metrology, cat states, DFS) with precision atomic spectroscopy, offering new ways to use many-body entanglement to probe fundamental physics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Determining the optimal quantum metrological strategy to detect deviations from Standard Model weak-charge scaling in atomic parity violation (APV) measurements across an isotope chain.

**Main result.** The paper identifies that a cross-isotope cat state is the information-optimal protocol for measuring deviations, though its utility is limited by systematic error floors and susceptibility to noise compared to more robust strategies.

**Method.** The author compares five quantum strategies (SQL, squeezed-array, same-isotope cat, cross-isotope cat, and DFS cat) using a parameter-estimation framework and simulations involving decoherence and gate infidelity.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on heavy atoms and ions (Cs, Yb, Ba+) and molecular arrays, modeling the nuclear-spin-independent weak Hamiltonian and the isotope-chain scaling of the weak charge.

**Key observables.** The deviation parameter (theta), transition rates, Ramsey phases, and the isotope-chain slope generator.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nuclear weak charge (Qw), number of probes (N), Heisenberg limit (1/N scaling), and the systematic error floor.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a specific form for the deviation pattern (h_A) and evaluates the impact of finite contrast and decoherence on the proposed quantum protocols.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the mechanism of parity violation and Standard Model tests; Figure 2 illustrates various APV platforms like neutral Yb and trapped ions; Figure 3 compares parameter uncertainty across different protocols.

**Paper structure.** The paper reviews the APV mechanism and current experimental state-of-the-art, introduces the metrological problem of isotope-chain scaling, presents a comparative analysis of various quantum entanglement strategies, and discusses the impact of noise and systematic limits.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Atomic parity violation (APV) provides a low-energy probe of the weak interaction between electrons and nuclei, complementary to collider tests of the Standard Model. Isotope-chain measurements are especially attractive because they test weak-charge scaling while reducing dependence on absolute atomic-structure theory. We review the APV mechanism, the state of the art in Cs and Yb, and recent trapped-ion, optical-lattice, and molecular proposals. Motivated by progress in coherent control of atoms, ions, and molecules, we ask a metrological question: given N probes distributed over an isotope chain, what quantum strategy optimally measures a deviation from Standard Model weak-charge scaling? The optimum is a particular form of a cross-isotope cat state protocol. We compare this protocol with the standard quantum limit, squeezed-array, same-isotope cat, and discuss extension to recently suggested decoherence-free subspace protocols. We show that entanglement can strongly accelerate statistical averaging, but the ultimate precision is set by APV-specific systematic floors which require careful studies.

</details>

### [Memory-assisted squeezed light velocimetry under realistic loss and incoherent noise](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27723v1)

**Authors:** Mustafa Gündoğan, Arash Ahmadi, Markus Krutzik  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27723v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27723_figures/2605.27723_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Two-memory Mach-Zehnder model. A coherent probe enters mode ˆa0 and phase-squeezed vacuum enters mode ˆb0. Both arms are stored and retrieved in iden- tical memories with write-store-read eﬃciency ηmem(τ) = η0 exp[−(τ/τmem)2] and the same unconditional noise ﬂoor pn(τ). Motion of the upper memory during storage generates the signal phase ϕv = −kpvτ. Balanced homodyne detection measures the dark output.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27723_figures/2605.27723_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Optimized equal-resource gain versus unconditional memory-noise ﬂoor pn for 10 dB injected squeezing. Each curve is obtained after numerical optimization of Eq. (22) over storage time at ﬁxed shot number for a ﬁxed zero-storage transmission η(0). The vertical lines mark representative detector-referred noise ﬂoors reported or implied by Refs. [42– 44]. Over the experimentally relevant range pn ∼10−4–10−2, and even up to pn ∼10−1, the gain changes only slowly; in the reference working point the advantage is therefore limited more by transmission than by pn.</sub>

<img src="2605.27723_figures/2605.27723_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Storage time dependence of the shot-normalized sen- sitivity ∆v √</sub>

(22) over storage time at ﬁxed shot number, shown as a function of the zero-storage total transmission η(0) and the injected squeezing level. <sub>Figure 3 shows how the signal phase and the retrieval eﬃciency compete. At short storage times the accumu- lated phase is too small. At longer times the Gaussian decay of ηmem(τ) suppresses the retrieved ﬁeld strongly enough that the sensitivity rises again. **Summary.** This paper analyzes a velocity sensor that uses quantum memories and squeezed light to improve measurement precision. It demonstrates that while realistic losses and noise limit the quantum advantage to a few percent, the squeezed-light scheme remains robust and superior to classical methods under near-term experimental conditions.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous metrological analysis of how non-classical states (squeezed light) perform in practical quantum memory-based sensing, specifically addressing the competition between signal accumulation and decoherence/loss.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether a memory-assisted velocimetry platform using squeezed light can achieve quantum enhancement despite realistic experimental imperfections like loss and incoherent noise.

**Main result.** The study finds that squeezed light provides a modest (few to ten percent) improvement in velocity sensitivity, and that this advantage is primarily limited by total transmission and phase stability rather than unconditional memory noise.

**Method.** The authors use Classical Fisher Information (CFI) to derive the Cramér-Rao bound for velocity estimation and employ a Gaussian model for the variance budget.

**Model / system.** A two-memory Mach-Zehnder interferometer where one memory is stationary and the other moves, mapping velocity to an interferometric phase. The system uses a coherent probe and squeezed vacuum with a Gaussian write-store-read lifetime model.

**Key observables.** Velocity sensitivity (Delta v), quadrature variance of the dark port, and the relative figure of merit (quantum gain).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Storage time (tau), memory lifetime (tau_mem), squeezing parameter (r), transmission efficiency (eta), and unconditional memory noise floor (pn).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The memory-generated noise is assumed to be phase-insensitive and diagonal in the number basis; the system operates in a small-signal/linear-response regime.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the experimental schematic; Figure 2 plots optimized gain vs. memory noise; Figure 3 compares shot-normalized sensitivity for different detection schemes; Figure 4 maps gain as a function of transmission and squeezing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the interferometric setup, defines a Gaussian noise and loss model, derives the sensitivity limits using Fisher Information, performs numerical optimization of storage time, and concludes with a comparison of different detection regimes and noise impacts.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a velocity sensor based on a two-memory Mach--Zehnder interferometer fed by a coherent probe and squeezed vacuum, read out by balanced homodyne detection. One memory is taken as a stationary reference, while the second memory moves during storage, so that its velocity is mapped onto a differential interferometric phase at readout. The two memories are otherwise assumed identical and are described by a Gaussian write--store--read lifetime together with the associated unconditional noise floor. Using the classical Fisher information, we derive the velocity sensitivity, the transmission threshold required for a target quantum gain, and the optimum storage time. The squeezed scheme improves on equal-resource coherent homodyne within an operating window set mainly by total transmission and phase stability. For representative near-term parameters, unconditional memory noise floors up to about $10^{-1}$ photons per trial do not by themselves remove the advantage; after optimization the improvement remains at the few-percent level and is limited chiefly by loss.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28286"></a>
### [Photon-energy-programmable subnanometric electron birth-site control](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28286v1)

**Authors:** Hirofumi Yanagisawa, Abhisek Sinha, Ravi Kumar, Neill Lambert, Hirotaka Kitoh-Nishioka  
**Type:** both · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28286v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum optics experiment` **3/5** · `interference shaping light` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28286_figures/2605.28286_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 | Concept of photon-energy-dependent subnanometric control of electron-emission</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28286_figures/2605.28286_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2 | Reversible photon-energy-dependent switching of molecular emission patterns.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28286_figures/2605.28286_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 | Emission-pattern invariance under polarisation and power variations.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28286_figures/2605.28286_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 | Symmetry-resolved emission channels from the molecular scale to the far field.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that the site of electron emission from a single molecule can be controlled at a subnanometer scale simply by changing the energy of the incoming photons. By tuning the photon energy, researchers can switch between different molecular orbitals, effectively moving the electron 'birth site' without changing the optical field distribution. This provides a new mechanism for high-resolution nanoscale imaging and control.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and molecular physics as it demonstrates a new way to manipulate quantum-scale electron pathways using the internal electronic structure (symmetry) of a molecule rather than external field shaping.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Conventional optical control of electron-generation sites is limited by the need to physically shape local-field hotspots, making it impossible to independently select different substructures within the same field distribution.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated that electron birth-site control can be achieved at a subnanometric scale by tuning photon energy to selectively access molecular orbitals of different spatial symmetries, independent of the local-field profile.

**Method.** The study combines experimental electron emission measurements from single-molecule emitters with theoretical modeling using DFT (GAMESS/VASP) and a three-step emission model involving orbital-resolved weighting and field propagation.

**Model / system.** The experimental platform consists of single-molecule electron emitters (C60 multilayers) at the apex of a metallic tungsten nanotip under ultrahigh vacuum and a strong DC field.

**Key observables.** Far-field electron emission patterns (e.g., ring-like vs. two-lobed structures) and normalized lobe separation (d/d0).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Photon energies of 2.99 eV and 3.18 eV; subnanometric spatial resolution (~0.3 nm); DC field of 4.5 V/nm; electron yield < 0.05 per pulse.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes an incoherent sum of orbital contributions and utilizes a normal-incidence approximation for the decay constant of the post-barrier wavefunction.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 compares field-based vs. energy-dependent control; Fig 2 shows experimental reversible switching of emission patterns and simulation overlays; Fig 3 demonstrates the invariance of patterns to polarization and power; Fig 4 shows the asymmetric energy-supply function.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the limitation of local-field control, presents the experimental demonstration of energy-dependent switching, provides a theoretical model for symmetry-selective excitation, and validates the mechanism through polarization and power-dependence tests.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Optical control of electron-generation sites has broadly enabled ultrafast nanoscale imaging, spectroscopy, and functional control. Existing approaches achieve nanoscale site selectivity by shaping localised optical fields around nanostructures, thereby limiting independent site selectivity within the same local-field hotspot. Here, using a single-molecule electron emitter, we show that site selectivity can instead be encoded in the electronic excitation pathway, enabling subnanometric control of electron birth sites within the same local-field hotspot. By tuning the photon energy, we selectively access molecular states of different spatial symmetry and reversibly switch the electron birth site between distinct locations in the same emitter, with the change read out directly in the far-field emission pattern. The switching depends on photon energy alone and is absent under variations in intensity or polarisation. Our results establish optical birth-site selectivity that is not dictated by the local-field distribution, opening a route to electron birth-site control through the electronic excitation pathway.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28540"></a>
### [Anisotropic magnetism and Kondo-lattice behavior in the frustrated antiferromagnet Ce3MgBi5](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28540v1)

**Summary.** Researchers synthesized and characterized Ce3MgBi5, a new intermetallic compound that exhibits both geometric frustration and Kondo-lattice physics. The material shows a complex magnetic phase diagram and strong coupling between charge transport and magnetic order. This work provides a new platform for studying the competition between RKKY interactions and the Kondo effect in frustrated lattices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the interplay between geometric frustration, magnetic anisotropy, and Kondo-lattice physics in the newly synthesized Ce3MgBi5 compound.

**Main result.** The study identifies a Ce-based Kondo-lattice system with antiferromagnetic order below 4.2 K, featuring a complex H-T phase diagram with multiple metamagnetic transitions and strong magnetic anisotropy.

**Method.** Single crystals were synthesized via the Mg-Bi self-flux method and characterized using SQUID magnetometry, PPMS for high-field transport and heat capacity, and X-ray diffraction.

**Model / system.** The physical system is Ce3MgBi5, a member of the Ce3MPn5 family with a hexagonal P63/mcm structure featuring a distorted kagome-like Ce sublattice and zig-zag chains along the c-axis.

**Key observables.** Néel temperature (TN), magnetization (M), electrical resistivity (rho), magnetoresistance (MR), Hall effect, specific heat (Cp), and magnetic entropy (Smag).

**Important parameters / regimes.** TN approx 4.2 K, Weiss temperature (highly anisotropic), Sommerfeld coefficient (gamma approx 199 mJ mol-1 CeK-2), and spin-wave gap (Delta approx 4.96 K).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The use of La3MgBi5 as a nonmagnetic isostructural reference to subtract phonon background from specific heat measurements.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the crystal structure and Ce sublattice details; Fig 3 shows temperature-dependent resistivity and its derivative; Fig 4 shows temperature-dependent specific heat.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with crystal synthesis and structural characterization, followed by magnetic properties and anisotropy, then moves to electrical transport and Hall effect measurements, and concludes with thermodynamic analysis of the Kondo-lattice behavior.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We report the synthesis and physical characterization of single-crystalline Ce3MgBi5, a previously unexplored member of the Ce3MPn5 family. This compound crystallizes in the hexagonal P63/mcm structure, featuring an anisotropic Ce sublattice composed of zig-zag chains along the c axis and a distorted kagome-like network in the basal plane. Magnetization measurements reveal antiferromagnetic order below TN approximately 4.2 K, accompanied by strong magnetic anisotropy and multiple field-induced metamagnetic transitions for fields applied perpendicular to [001], leading to a dome-shaped H-T phase diagram. Electrical transport exhibits characteristic signatures of a Ce-based Kondo lattice, including broad resistivity maxima and pronounced field-dependent anomalies in the magnetoresistance and Hall response that track the magnetic phase boundaries. Specific-heat measurements confirm the magnetic transition and show that the full R ln 2 entropy expected for a Ce3+ Kramers doublet is recovered by 20 K, indicating an extended temperature range of magnetic fluctuations consistent with Kondo correlations. Our results establish Ce3MgBi5 as a platform within the Ce3MPn5 family for exploring the interplay of geometric frustration, magnetic anisotropy, and Kondo-lattice physics under applied magnetic fields.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28374"></a>
### [Global Bounds beyond Local Quantum Metrology](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28374v1)

**Authors:** Hai-Long Shi, Augusto Smerzi  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28374v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `quantum measurements` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28374_figures/2605.28374_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Classical global-score hierarchy for a noisy binary model p(x|θ) = Qm ℓ=1(1 + xℓd sin θ)/2, with d = 1/2, wi = 1/n, and θi ∈[0, π]. (a) For fixed n = 8, BFG C /BGCR C →1 as m increases, recovering local Cram´er–Rao behavior. (b) The global Barankin level remains below the Cram´er–Rao scale. (c) For fixed m = 10, refining the sampled domain drives the variance of the maximum-likelihood estimator (MLE) toward BFG C , whereas the global Cram´er–Rao and Barankin levels do not track the attainable variance.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28374_figures/2605.28374_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Bayesian global-score bound for a noisy qubit with Gaussian prior width σp = 5 and decoherence d = 0.5. The local limit ϵ →0 recovers the Van Trees bound. Finite-width kernels retain correlations between distinct parameter values and yield stronger BMSE lower bounds. The black dot marks the optimal kernel width yielding the strongest BMSE lower bound.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper develops a new hierarchy of precision bounds for quantum metrology that applies to global parameter estimation rather than just local precision. It demonstrates that when a parameter is known only within a broad range, a single fixed measurement strategy can achieve a fundamental 'fully global' limit. This framework also provides a unified approach to strengthening Bayesian estimation bounds.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant for quantum metrology and quantum optics, as it provides a new fundamental limit for sensing when the parameter is not known a priori, moving beyond the standard local-limit paradigm.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper addresses the limitation of local quantum metrology, which only bounds precision near a specific parameter value, by investigating the fundamental precision limits for global parameter estimation over a broad domain.

**Main result.** The authors establish a hierarchy of precision bounds (Global Cramér-Rao, Global Barankin, and Fully Global) and show that a single, parameter-independent measurement can saturate the fully global bound in certain quantum models.

**Method.** The authors introduce global score functions tied to a weighted variance and use a hierarchy matrix to couple score directions across the parameter domain, applying the Cauchy-Schwarz inequality and the Rayleigh quotient maximization theorem.

**Model / system.** The framework is applied to general quantum statistical models, including a noisy qubit model with a density operator parameterized by an angle, and a classical binary measurement model.

**Key observables.** Weighted variance of the estimator, Bayesian mean-square error (BMSE), and the quantum information matrix.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of measurement repetitions (m), parameter domain density (n), parameter domain width, and the bandwidth (epsilon) of the Bayesian hierarchy kernel.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes a discrete set of parameter values for the frequentist derivation and assumes the probability distribution is strictly positive for all outcomes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 2 shows the generalized Bayesian bound as a function of the error parameter epsilon.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the global estimation problem, introduces the global score function and the hierarchy of bounds, provides the mathematical derivation for classical and quantum settings, demonstrates saturation conditions and optimal measurement design, and extends the framework to Bayesian estimation.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum Cramér--Rao theory is intrinsically local: it bounds precision near a specified parameter value, and its saturating measurement generally depends on that value. Barankin-type bounds use finite parameter displacements, but remain anchored to a chosen reference value. This leaves open a basic global-estimation problem: when the parameter is known only within a broad domain, what precision can be guaranteed by a single estimator and a single measurement strategy fixed before the true value is localized? We answer this question by introducing global score functions tied to a weighted variance over the whole parameter domain. Their correlations generate a hierarchy of precision bounds: global Cramér--Rao and Barankin-type bounds arise as restricted levels, whereas unrestricted score correlations yield a fully global bound for the prescribed weighted variance. The hierarchy recovers local Cramér--Rao theory in the many-repetition limit and reveals genuinely global precision limits for finite data over broad domains. In the quantum setting, the construction identifies when this fully global bound can be realized by a single parameter-independent measurement. The same framework extends to Bayesian estimation, recovering the Van Trees bound in the local limit while yielding stronger finite-width lower bounds on the Bayesian mean-square error beyond this limit.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27560"></a>
### [MATBG Josephson diode as an universal thermal machine](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27560v1)

**Authors:** Hadi Mohammed Soufy, Colin Benjamin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27560v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27560_figures/2605.27560_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: (a) Schematic of a MATBG-JDTM loop with a weak-link valley-polarized region of length d, coupled to thermal baths at temperatures Th and Tc via controllable valves vh and vc. (b) SCL and SCR are the left and right superconducting leads which are characterized by the superconducting order parameter ∆s, and VP is weakly-linked valley-polarized region characterized by potential ∆v which is tunable by external gating. The phase difference across the junction is set to zero, since we are dealing with a Josephson diode (JD).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27560_figures/2605.27560_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Entropy as a function of valley-polarization parameter ∆v for (a) QSC, consisting of two quantum isothermal and two quantum isochoric strokes operating across temperatures Tc and Th, and (b) QOC, consisting of two quantum adiabatic and two quantum isochoric strokes operating across temperatures Tc,Th,Tα and Tβ . Red arrows indicate heat exchange with the hot reservoir, while blue arrows indicate heat exchange with the cold reservoir.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27560_figures/2605.27560_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: (a,b) Work and performance metrics of MATBG-JDTM exhibiting a QSC. (c,d) Work and performance metrics of MATBG-JJTM (e,f) for AABLG-JJTM for a QSC, in heat engine (HE), refrigerator (RE), cold pump (CP), and Joule pump (JP) regimes. The bath temperatures are Th = 0.1K and Tc = 0.05K.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27560_figures/2605.27560_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: (a,b) Work and performance metrics of MATBG-JDTM exhibiting a QOC. (c,d) Work and performance metrics of MATBG-JJTM (e,f) for AABLG-JJTM for a QOC, in the heat engine (HE), refrigerator (RE), and cold pump (CP) regimes. The bath temperatures are Th = 0.1K and Tc = 0.05K.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27560_figures/2605.27560_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5: (a) Isothermal contour plots depicting QSC in MATB-JDTM, (b),(c) are isoentropic contours for MATBG-JDTM depicting QOC and QCC, respectively.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes using the intrinsic Josephson diode effect in magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene to create highly efficient, electrically tunable quantum heat engines and refrigerators. By using valley polarization as a control parameter instead of magnetic flux, these devices offer a more stable and efficient alternative to conventional superconducting thermal machines.

**Why it may be interesting.** This paper is interesting for researchers in open quantum systems and many-body dynamics because it demonstrates how broken symmetries and topological features in a correlated electron system can be harnessed to drive non-equilibrium thermodynamic cycles and control heat/work exchange.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the potential of Magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene (MATBG) Josephson diodes to function as universal quantum thermal machines (heat engines and refrigerators) and comparing them to conventional phase-driven architectures.

**Main result.** MATBG-based thermal machines exhibit significantly enhanced work output and efficiency compared to AA-stacked bilayer graphene due to flat-band-enhanced density of states and trigonal warping. Additionally, the diode-based approach allows for a flux-free, electrically tunable control via valley polarization, mitigating magnetic flux noise.

**Method.** The study uses the Blonder-Tinkham-Klapwijk (BTK) formalism to calculate Andreev bound state (ABS) energies and applies the laws of thermodynamics to analyze Quantum Stirling, Otto, and Carnot cycles.

**Model / system.** The system consists of a Magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene (MATBBG) Josephson junction with a gate-tunable, valley-polarized weak link between two superconducting leads, modeled using a 1D effective Hamiltonian incorporating trigonal warping and anisotropic Fermi velocity.

**Key observables.** Andreev bound state (ABS) spectra, current-phase relations (CPR), work output (W), heat exchange (Q), efficiency (eta), and Coefficient of Performance (COP).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Valley-polarization potential (delta_v), superconducting phase difference (phi), Thouless energy (E_T), trigonal warping scale (E_A), and temperature (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a ballistic limit and neglects inter-valley back-scattering; temperatures are assumed to remain below the superconducting critical temperature.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the thermal machine loop and junction; ABS spectra and current-phase relations for MATBG and AABLG; plots of work, efficiency, and anomalous current as functions of valley polarization.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the MATBG Josephson diode platform, defines the microscopic Hamiltonian and ABS derivation, constructs various quantum thermodynamic cycles, performs a comparative performance analysis against AABLG systems, and discusses experimental advantages regarding flux-free control.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Magic-angle twisted bilayer graphene Josephson junctions (MATBG-JJ) with a gate-tunable valley-polarized weak link exhibit an intrinsic Josephson diode effect originating from broken symmetries associated with valley polarization and band-structure anisotropy. Exploiting this nonreciprocal superconducting platform, we construct quantum Stirling (QSC), Otto (QOC), and Carnot (QCC) thermodynamic cycles, where the valley-polarization potential $Δ_v$ acts as the principal control parameter, in contrast to conventional Josephson thermal machines driven by superconducting phase bias. We systematically compare the performance of MATBG-based Josephson diode thermal machines (MATBG-JDTM) with MATBG-based Josephson junction thermal machines (MATBG-JJTM) and AA-stacked bilayer graphene Josephson junction thermal machines (AABLG-JJTM). Owing to the flat-band-enhanced density of states and electrically tunable nonreciprocal transport in MATBG, both MATBG-JDTM and MATBG-JJTM exhibit significantly enhanced work output and efficiency over a broad operating regime compared to AABLG-JJTM. In particular, the gate-controlled MATBG-JDTM provides a flux-free alternative to conventional phase-driven architectures, mitigating limitations associated with magnetic-flux control and flux-noise effects. Our results establish MATBG Josephson diode platforms as a promising route toward electrically tunable quantum thermal machines and nonreciprocal superconducting caloritronics.

</details>

### [Picometer control of a levitating milligram gravity sensor](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28479v1)

**Authors:** Dennis G. Uitenbroek, Jurriaan Langendorff, Tjerk H. Oosterkamp

We feedback cool the magnets motion by applying a phase shifted tone shaking the trap housing through a piezo and lock-in amplifier. (b) A close-up of the permanent magnet used in the experiment. **Summary.** Researchers have achieved picometer-scale control and millikelvin-scale cooling of a milligram-sized magnet levitated in a superconducting trap. By using active feedback cooling, they successfully reduced the thermal motion of two simultaneous mechanical modes. This setup provides a high-precision platform for future experiments testing the limits of quantum mechanics in the macroscopic regime.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work demonstrates extreme precision control and cooling of a macroscopic object, which is a fundamental precursor to studying the interface between quantum mechanics and gravity and exploring decoherence in large-scale quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of cooling a macroscopic, milligram-scale mechanical resonator to its quantum ground state to enable future gravity experiments involving quantum superpositions.

**Main result.** Demonstrated simultaneous linear feedback cooling of two translational modes of a levitated magnet to below 2 picometer amplitude and below 10 millikelvin mode temperature.

**Method.** Active damping via linear feedback cooling using a SQUID-based detection system, a lock-in amplifier, and a piezoelectric actuator to apply a phase-shifted force.

**Model / system.** A 0.35 mg permanent magnet levitated in a type I superconducting tantalum trap via the Meissner effect, housed in a dry dilution refrigerator with multi-stage vibration isolation.

**Key observables.** Power Spectral Density (PSD), Amplitude Spectral Density (ASD), RMS amplitude, mode temperature, and phonon number.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass scale of ~0.35 mg, resonant frequencies of 50.6 Hz and 68.0 Hz, Q factors up to 5.5 x 10^6, and vibration attenuation of 110-130 dB.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes small displacements where magnetic flux change is linear; assumes the resonator behaves as a driven harmonic oscillator.

**Figures summary.** Schematics of the experimental setup and magnet composition; images of the superconducting trap and vibration isolation; plots of resonant modes (ASD) and the effect of feedback gain on mode temperature and amplitude.

**Paper structure.** Introduction to the scientific problem; description of the experimental platform and levitation setup; details on detection and calibration methods; presentation of cooling results for specific modes; discussion of noise upconversion and future paths to ground-state cooling.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Due to their exceptional isolation from the environment, magnetically levitated particles are explored as extremely sensitive mechanical sensors. For future gravity experiments on quantum superpositions, such systems need to be cooled close to their ground state. To demonstrate the combination of state of the art vibration isolation, milligram levitated high Q mechanical resonators and position detection with low noise, we present linear feedback cooling of a magnetically levitated gravity sensor to below 2 picometer amplitude and below 10 millikelvin mode temperature for two translational modes (the x- and y-mode) simultaneously. The sensor is a levitating permanent magnet in a type I superconducting trap, where its six resonance frequencies are measured with a superconducting coil coupled to a DC SQUID. This signal is measured with a lock-in amplifier and a feedback signal is sent to a piezoelectric actuator, allowing the cooling of resonant modes at 50.6 and 68.0 Hz simultaneously. These two translational modes have Q factors of $3.8 \cdot 10^6$ and $5.5 \cdot 10^6$ respectively. The experiment is mounted inside a dry dilution refrigerator where it is vibrationally attenuated with 110-130 dB at these frequencies. In this work, we discuss future improvements on the setup which may enable quantum ground state cooling on a magnetically levitated particle, that has previously been shown to be a gravitational sensor.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.27907"></a>
### [Geometry near rank-changing points on the mixed-state manifold: Bures metric, conical singularities, and Lindblad dynamics](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27907v1)

**Authors:** Yu-Huan Huang, Xu-Yang Hou, Guo Hao, Chih-Chun Chien  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27907v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `methods for driven-dissipative` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27907_figures/2605.27907_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. (a) The Bloch vector norm r and the Bures metric components (b) grr, (C)gθθ, and(d)gφφ during the asymptotic puriﬁcation evolution for p = 0.7 and γ = 2.0.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27907_figures/2605.27907_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Behaviors of (a) the Bloch vector norm and (b)-(d) the Bures metric components during the ﬁnite-time puriﬁca- tion evolution for p = 0.7, γ = 2.0 and α = 1.0, 2.0, 3.0. Since gφφ = 0 in this case, we show gtt in panel (d).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27907_figures/2605.27907_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. (a) Evolution path from pure to mixed state in the Bloch ball. (b)-(d) Behavior of the Bloch vector norm and the Bures metric components during the evolution from pure to mixed state for γ1 = 1.0 and γ2 = 0.5.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27907_figures/2605.27907_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Illustration of the geometry of N = 3 states. The full state space is an 8-dimensional convex body, represented schematically as a ball. The equilateral triangle ∆2 is the eigenvalue simplex of diagonal density matrices (Eq. (28)). Its corners are pure states (rank 1), its edges are rank-2 states, and its interior is full rank. Any density matrix is obtained by a unitary transformation of a point in ∆2. The simplex lies strictly inside the convex body; its edges do not lie on the boundary of the state space. The corners are points where the rank jumps from 3 to 1. Near these corners, the geometry develops a conical singularity.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27907_figures/2605.27907_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Illustration of the metric cone structure near a pure state for N ≥3. The yellow dot at the tip is a pure state and also a rank-changing point. The blue dot is a mixed state in the interior. The red curve shows a radial Bures geodesic approaching or shooting from the cone tip, illustrating the “geodesic approaching/shooting” behavior of Lindblad evolu- tion. Any evolution that approaches or leaves the tip must become asymptotically radial to avoid divergent angular ve- locity.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper reveals that while the Bures metric appears singular near pure states in qubits, higher-dimensional quantum systems possess intrinsic conical singularities. It classifies how different Lindblad-driven processes, such as purification and decoherence, interact with this complex geometry.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides fundamental insights into the geometric landscape of open quantum systems, specifically how the dimensionality of the Hilbert space fundamentally changes the nature of quantum state space singularities.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the geometric structure and curvature singularities of the Bures metric on the mixed-state manifold near rank-changing points.

**Main result.** The paper proves that for N=2 systems, the Bures metric divergence is a coordinate artifact, whereas for N>=3 systems, the geometry forms a metric cone with genuine curvature singularities (Dirac delta or power-law divergence).

**Method.** Geometric analysis using Bloch vector parameterization, coordinate transformations, and the calculation of the Riemann curvature tensor and scalar curvature for conical metrics.

**Model / system.** N-level quantum systems (specifically N=2 and N=3) undergoing Lindblad master equation dynamics, including scenarios of asymptotic purification, finite-time purification, and decoherence.

**Key observables.** Bures metric components, scalar curvature, and the Quantum Geometric Tensor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rank of the density matrix, dimensionality of the system (N), spectral ratio (zeta), and the radial distance to the pure-state boundary.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the transition between full-rank and rank-1 states; the Hamiltonian is assumed to be zero for simplicity in the Lindblad constructions.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows time evolution of the Bloch vector and metric components; Figure 5 illustrates the metric cone structure and radial geodesics; Figure 6 shows 2D and 3D conical singularities; Figure 8 shows the angular deficit in an unfolded cone.

**Paper structure.** The paper first compares N=2 and N>=3 geometries, then analyzes Lindblad evolution trajectories (geodesic vs non-geodesic), followed by a detailed construction of the metric cone and its curvature singularities, and concludes with experimental implications.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We elucidate the Bures metric in quantum state space near a rank-changing point of the density matrix and show contrasting behavior for two-level ($N=2$) systems versus higher-level systems. Due to the smooth pure-state boundary for $N=2$, we prove the apparent metric divergences to be merely coordinate artifacts and present three Lindblad processes exhibiting qualitatively different evolution near rank-changing points, showing geodesic approach, power-law scaling, and pure-state escape law. For higher-dimensional ($N\ge 3$) systems, the geometry near a rank-changing point differs fundamentally. Under suitable restrictions of the density matrix and its approach towards a pure state, the Bures metric reduces to a conical metric with the pure state at the cone tip. Such a conic geometry leads to genuine curvature singularities: A two-dimensional cone exhibits a Dirac delta-function curvature near the tip while a higher-dimensional cone shows a power-law divergence of the curvature towards the cone tip. A construction of Lindblad evolution for $N=3$ systems with conic singularities is presented, along with possible implications for future experimental and theoretical research.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** The paper introduces a framework for noise-adaptive two-way quantum key distribution where encoding and decoding are dynamically optimized to match channel noise. It demonstrates that this approach can increase secret key rates and noise tolerance for specific channel types like bit-phase flip and amplitude damping.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to researchers in open quantum systems and quantum optics as it provides a framework for optimizing quantum communication protocols by leveraging the specific dynamics of the environment-induced noise.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing two-way quantum key distribution (QKD) protocols use fixed encoding and decoding strategies that do not account for specific channel noise characteristics. The authors aim to develop and analyze noise-adaptive QKD (NAQKD) protocols that optimize these operations based on the noise model.

**Main result.** The study identifies specific noise channels, such as bit-phase flip and amplitude damping, where adaptive encoding and decoding significantly enhance secret key rates and noise thresholds. Conversely, it proves that for certain unital channels like depolarizing and bit-flip channels, adaptive strategies provide no advantage.

**Method.** The authors use entropic uncertainty relations and the Devetak-Winter bound to derive closed-form expressions for secret key rates. They optimize the parameters of SU(2) unitary operators for encoding and measurement bases to maximize these rates under collective attacks.

**Model / system.** A two-way quantum communication framework involving two honest parties (Alice and Bob) and an eavesdropper (Eve). The system involves a qubit traveling through forward and backward quantum channels subject to various noise models, including Pauli, amplitude damping, and non-Markovian environments.

**Key observables.** Secret key rate, critical noise threshold, and measurement probabilities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Encoding unitaries (SU(2) parameters theta, chi, phi), noise strength (p), and the overlap parameter (gamma).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes the eavesdropper performs collective attacks and that the honest parties can accurately estimate the noise model of the channel.

**Figures summary.** Plots of secret key rate versus noise strength for different noise models (Bit Flip, Phase Flip, Bit-Phase Flip, and Amplitude Damping), demonstrating where adaptive strategies outperform non-adaptive ones.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the NAQKD framework, defines the mathematical optimization of encoding/decoding unitaries, provides security proofs using entropic uncertainty relations, compares performance across various noise models (Pauli, amplitude damping), and includes detailed mathematical appendices for protocol verification.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We introduce noise-adaptive quantum key distribution (QKD) protocols, in which the honest parties optimize the encoding (state preparation) and decoding (measurement basis) operations according to the noise models affecting the honest subsystems induced by an eavesdropper. This extends conventional QKD schemes that employ fixed encoding and decoding strategies independent of the noise characteristics of the communication channel. We investigate three representative protocols: entanglement-based secure dense coding (SDC), the entanglement-free Lucamarini and Mancini (LM05), and a two-way prepare-and-measure Bennett Brassard (BB84) protocols. Using entropic uncertainty relations, we derive the corresponding secret key rates for both adaptive and conventional non-adaptive scenarios under collective attacks. For independent but identical noise acting on the forward and backward transmission channels, as well as for correlated and non-Markovian environments, we identify classes of channels for which adaptive schemes yield enhanced secret key rates for the considered protocols. In contrast, we also determine Pauli channels, including depolarizing and bit flip channels, for which adaptive strategies provide no benefit. We further show that these optimal sets are generally non-unique and can differ substantially from the unitaries that maximize dense-coding capacity in the absence of security constraints. Our results establish noise-adaptive encoding and decoding as a powerful framework for improving secure communication over realistic noisy quantum channels.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28784"></a>
### [Complex abelian varieties and quantum error correction: a mathematical framework for GKP codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28784v1)

**Authors:** Maxence Mayrand, Baptiste Royer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28784v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `entanglement & information structure` **2/5** · `quantum measurements` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28784_figures/2605.28784_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28784_figures/2605.28784_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28784_figures/2605.28784_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a formal mathematical framework that maps GKP quantum error-correcting codes to the theory of polarized complex abelian varieties. It establishes that the robustness of these codes against Gaussian noise is determined by the geometric systolic invariants of the associated varieties. This connection allows for the translation of quantum error correction optimization problems into problems within the moduli space of abelian varieties.

**Why it may be interesting.** This provides a powerful new geometric toolkit for optimizing GKP codes, specifically relating error-correction performance to well-studied invariants in algebraic geometry, which is highly relevant for continuous-variable quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Establishing a rigorous mathematical foundation for Gottesman-Kitaev-Preskill (GKP) quantum error-correcting codes by linking them to the geometry of complex abelian varieties.

**Main result.** The authors prove a dictionary between GKP code structures and abelian varieties, showing that logical gates correspond to automorphisms and error robustness is governed by the systolic invariant of the underlying polarization.

**Method.** The paper utilizes the Bargmann transform to relate Schrödinger and Fock representations, applying the theory of Heisenberg groups, theta functions, and polarized complex abelian varieties.

**Model / system.** The system consists of continuous-variable GKP codes modeled via symplectically integral lattices in a multi-mode harmonic oscillator (represented by the space of tempered distributions or L2 functions).

**Key observables.** Logical Pauli gates, logical Clifford gates, decoding success probability (robustness), and the syndrome of displacement errors.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Small Gaussian displacement noise variance (sigma), the type of the lattice (d1, ..., dn), and the systolic invariant (shortest nontrivial displacement).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis focuses on the small-noise limit (sigma to 0) and assumes Gaussian displacement noise and a symplectically integral lattice.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by establishing a geometric dictionary between GKP codes and abelian varieties, moves to the representation theory of the Heisenberg group and the Bargmann transform, then analyzes the decoding problem and error dynamics in the small-noise limit, and concludes with optimization problems on the moduli space.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study a class of quantum error-correcting codes through the geometry of complex abelian varieties. These codes, introduced by Gottesman--Kitaev--Preskill, are built from symplectically integral lattices and therefore naturally define polarized complex abelian varieties. We give a precise mathematical formulation of this relationship and extend it to a dictionary between the main structures of GKP code theory and classical objects in the theory of abelian varieties. For instance, under this dictionary, the finite-dimensional code space becomes the space of theta functions $H^0(X, L)$, logical Pauli gates arise from the theta group, passive logical Clifford gates correspond to automorphisms of the polarized abelian variety, and concatenation with stabilizer codes corresponds to isogeny. We also prove several key results that give precise mathematical formulations of statements about these codes that often appear in heuristic form in the physics literature. In particular, we prove that the encoding is asymptotically isometric, that every logical Clifford gate is realized by a Gaussian unitary, and that, for noise of small variance, the failure probability is governed to first order by the shortest nontrivial displacement in the kernel of the polarization isogeny, a systolic invariant of the underlying polarization. This leads naturally to optimization problems on the moduli space of polarized abelian varieties.

</details>

### [Latent-Conditioned Parameterized Quantum Circuits as Universal Approximators for Distributions over Quantum States](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28690v1)

**Authors:** Quoc Hoan Tran, Koki Chinzei, Yasuhiro Endo, Hirotaka Oshima  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28690v1>

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28690_figures/2605.28690_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic of the LPQC framework for approximating the target distribution Q on the density matrices space D(HS). Latent variable z ∼r(z) is mapped via neural networks fℓ(z; wℓ) to gate parameters θℓ(z), which define the composite unitary U(θ(z)) = UL(θL(z)) · · · U1(θ1(z)) on HS ⊗HA. In our setting, the same z is used for all fℓ. Applying U(θ(z)) to |0⟩and tracing over the ancilla HA yields ensemble (r, ρw), which can approximate any Q arbitrarily well in the 1-Wasserstein distance W1.</sub>

Results are averaged across 10 experimental trials per configuration, with solid lines representing the mean and shaded regions indicating the standard deviation.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper introduces Latent-Conditioned Parameterized Quantum Circuits (LPQCs), a framework that uses classical neural networks to map latent variables to quantum circuit parameters. It proves that this approach can universally approximate any distribution of quantum states and shows that it effectively mitigates the vanishing gradient problem (barren plateaus). The method is successfully applied to generate complex molecular structures from the QM9 dataset.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous theoretical foundation for quantum generative modeling and introduces a practical method to bypass the barren plateau problem, which is a central bottleneck in variational quantum algorithms and quantum machine learning.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of efficiently generating complex, multimodal distributions of quantum states (ensembles) and mitigating the barren plateau problem in parameterized quantum circuits (PQCs).

**Main result.** The authors prove that Latent-Conditioned Parameterized Quantum Circuits (LPQCs) are universal approximators for distributions over density operators and demonstrate that they significantly alleviate barren plateaus while outperforming quantum generative baselines on molecular datasets.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical framework where a classical neural network maps a low-dimensional latent variable to the parameters of a PQC, utilizing a Mixture-of-Experts architecture and a 1-Wasserstein distance loss function.

**Model / system.** A hybrid generative model consisting of a classical neural network (the decoder) and a parameterized quantum circuit (the generator) acting on a system of n data qubits and m ancilla qubits.

**Key observables.** 1-Wasserstein distance, gradient norm (to measure barren plateaus), trace distance, and molecular metrics (validity, uniqueness, novelty).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Latent dimension (d), number of qubits (n), circuit depth (L), number of experts (E), and number of prior modes (M).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The latent space is compact, the prior density is positive and continuous, and the primitive gate set is dense in the unitary group.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the LPQC framework schematic; Figure 2 compares performance across different expert/mode configurations; Figure 3 uses PCA/t-SNE to visualize density matrix ensembles; Figure 4 shows test loss scaling; Figure 5 and 6 illustrate gradient norm decay/stability; Figure 7 compares Wasserstein loss; Figure 8 compares LPQC to IMPE; Figure 9 showcases generated novel molecules.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the LPQC framework, provides a mathematical proof of its universal approximation property, details architectural enhancements like Mixture-of-Experts, presents numerical experiments on synthetic ensembles, and demonstrates application to molecular generation using the QM9 dataset.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Many applications in quantum simulation, quantum chemistry, and quantum machine learning require not a single quantum state but an ensemble of states characterizing the heterogeneity of a target system. Preparing such ensembles state-by-state is prohibitive in both variational and fault-tolerant settings, motivating a generative-modeling approach. We introduce latent-conditioned parameterized quantum circuits (LPQCs), a hybrid quantum-classical framework in which classical neural networks map a latent variable sampled from a prior distribution to the parameters of a parameterized quantum circuit. We prove that LPQCs are universal approximators for probability measures over density operators in the $1$-Wasserstein distance, extending classical universal approximation theorems to the quantum-distribution setting. We additionally introduce a multimodal latent prior and a mixture-of-experts circuit architecture, and show that it empirically alleviates the barren plateau problem during optimization. Numerical experiments validate the framework on a synthetic multi-cluster ensemble of mixed quantum states and on a QM9-derived ensemble of 3-D molecular structures. In these tasks, LPQC outperforms recent quantum generative baselines while remaining competitive with typical classical baselines at substantially reduced output dimensionality. By leveraging classical expressivity in the latent space, LPQCs offer a tractable route to quantum generative modeling.

</details>

**Summary.** The paper compares different non-Gaussian quantum states to see how they perform under displacement noise. It finds that while cat states are better for overall non-Gaussianity, photon-conditioned squeezed states are better for protecting against noise in specific, known directions.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and continuous-variable quantum information, as it provides a task-oriented framework for selecting non-Gaussian resources for noise-resilient quantum communication and sensing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to establish a common, energy-matched resource map to compare non-Gaussian state families (squeezed Fock, photon-subtracted, and cat states) based on their utility for displacement-noise mitigation and directional sensing.

**Main result.** While cat states provide superior scalar Wigner negativity, photon-conditioned squeezed states offer a tunable, anisotropic displacement-fidelity response, making them superior for tasks where the noise geometry is known.

**Method.** The authors use numerical simulations with a Fock-space cutoff of 80 and a uniform phase-space grid to benchmark states at a matched mean photon number.

**Model / system.** The study is set in the framework of continuous-variable (CV) quantum optics, utilizing single-mode bosonic operators and operations including squeezing, photon subtraction, and displacement.

**Key observables.** Integrated Wigner negativity, energy-normalized negativity, displacement-fidelity radii (Rx, Rp), and the anisotropy ratio.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Matched mean photon number (n), squeezing level (up to 12.5 dB), and fidelity threshold (Fth = 0.90).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes a weak-tap limit for photon subtraction and does not account for experimental imperfections like optical loss or finite detector efficiency.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows Wigner functions of various states; Figure 3 displays matched-energy displacement-fidelity radii and anisotropy ratios; Figure 4 presents a polar plot of the angular displacement-fidelity radius.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of resource comparison, defines the state families and metrics, presents the benchmarking results for scalar and directional metrics, and concludes with the implications for noise mitigation and sensing.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Squeezed Fock states, photon-subtracted squeezed states, and optical cat states are established non-Gaussian resources in continuous-variable quantum optics. Here we compare these known state families from a task-oriented perspective: matched mean photon number, scalar Wigner negativity, and directional displacement-fidelity response. Starting from squeezed vacuum, single-photon subtraction prepares a state proportional to \(S(r,θ)\ket{1}\), while two-photon subtraction prepares an even-parity squeezed Fock superposition rather than a pure \(S(r,θ)\ket{2}\). We benchmark photon-conditioned squeezed states against Fock and coherent-cat references using the integrated Wigner negativity \(δ\), the energy-normalized metric \(δ/\langle n\rangle\), and fidelity-threshold displacement radii \(R_F(φ)\). Cat benchmarks remain strong scalar Wigner-negativity resources, whereas photon-conditioned squeezed states provide an origin-centered alternative with tunable anisotropic displacement response. In particular, the two-photon-subtracted squeezed state shows favorable displacement-fidelity radii over selected quadrature directions at matched \(\langle n\rangle\). These results identify a regime relevant to homodyne-aligned continuous-variable control and anisotropic displacement-noise mitigation, with directional sensing as a natural dual application.

</details>

### [Finite-size occupancy scaling of apparent fractal dimensions in stochastic trajectories](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27925v1)

**Authors:** Bon A. Koo, Edward Ju  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** statistical mechanics · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27925v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium universality` **2/5** · `non-equilibrium dynamics` **1/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>Figure 1: Measured box-count scaling (markers) and the occupancy model (2) (solid, Df = 2, one fitted prefactor) for SRW at two d (N = 220). Dotted lines mark the fitted k⋆; the shaded band is the default window.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<sub>Figure 2: Scaling collapse of the box-counting bias against x = kmid −1</sub>

</details>
