<sub>FIG. 4. (a) Crystal structure of the fcc lattice in the achiral Fm¯3m phase. Displacement configurations that induce the (b) RH and (c) LH chiral P213 structures, respectively. (d) Schematic illustration of the relationship between the RH and LH structures.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27812_figures/2605.27812_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. (a),(b) Phonon dispersions of the (a) D2d and (b) D2 structures along k = (0, 0, k/2) (−1 ≤k ≤1). The color rep- resents the phonon angular momentum. The branches show- ing the splitting, ℓ= 1, 2, ℓ= 4, 5, and ℓ= 8, 9, are indicated by arrows. (c) The η dependence of the splittings ∆ω1,2(η), ∆ω4,5(η), and ∆ω8,9(η) for −0.01 ≤η ≤0.01. The splittings are measured at k = (0, 0, 1/5).</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a symmetry-based framework to quantify how structural phase transitions generate chirality in crystals. It demonstrates that the emergence of electric toroidal multipoles and observable effects like chiral phonons can be predicted directly from the symmetry of the parent lattice and the displacement modes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to quantify structural chirality during achiral-to-chiral (AtC) phase transitions and determine the functional relationship between structural order parameters and chirality-induced responses.

**Main result.** The authors establish a symmetry-based classification of AtC transitions, showing that the scaling of electric toroidal multipoles (monopoles and quadrupoles) is determined by the parent structure's symmetry and the character of the displacive mode.

**Method.** A group-theoretical approach combined with Landau theory is used to construct the electric toroidal multipoles as explicit functions of the displacement order parameter.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on crystalline solids undergoing structural phase transitions driven by atomic displacements, including specific cases like the rutile, diamond, and breathing pyrochlore lattices.

**Key observables.** Current-induced magnetization (CIM), chiral phonon splitting (CPS), and phonon angular momentum.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Displacement order parameter (eta), electric toroidal multipoles (G0 and Gu), and the ordering vector (Q).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis primarily focuses on continuous phase transitions, though conclusions are noted to extend to discontinuous cases.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 illustrates the achiral tetragonal phase and the resulting right-handed and left-handed displacement patterns in the chiral phase.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the concept of chirality via electric toroidal multipoles, develops a group-theoretical framework to relate these to structural order parameters, analyzes specific transition types (q=0 and q != 0), examines various lattice symmetries (rutile, diamond, pyrochlore), and concludes with the implications for phonon dynamics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Chirality, defined by the absence of mirror and inversion symmetries, has attracted considerable attention owing to its unique physical phenomena, including cross-correlated responses such as current-induced magnetization (CIM) and chiral phonons. Recently, it has been established that chirality is characterized by electric toroidal (ET) multipoles: the ET monopole $G_0$ in cubic systems and the ET quadrupole $G_u$ in noncubic systems. In this paper, we investigate achiral-to-chiral (AtC) structural phase transitions driven by atomic displacements and construct $G_{0,u}$ as explicit functions of the displacement order parameter $η$ based on a group-theoretical approach. We show that the leading-order dependence of $G_{0,u}(η)$ is determined by the symmetry of the parent structure and the character of the displacive mode, providing a symmetry-based classification of AtC transitions beyond a binary distinction between achiral and chiral phases. We also demonstrate that $G_{0,u}(η)$ is directly reflected in observable quantities such as CIM and CPS, both of which scale consistently with $G_{0,u}(η)$. We further clarify the microscopic mechanism by which AtC transitions give rise to chiral phonons and chiral phonon splitting (CPS) through the coupling between $G_{0,u}(η)$ and phonon degrees of freedom.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27979"></a>
### [Direction-selective intertwined charge, orbital, and lattice orders under uniaxial strain in hole-doped manganite: La0.75Ca0.25MnO3](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27979v1)

**Authors:** Ju Hyeon Lee, Beom Hyun Kim, Bongjae Kim  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27979v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27979_figures/2605.27979_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Schematic phase diagram of bulk La1−xCaxMnO3 as a function of doping, x. At x = 0, cooperative Jahn- Teller (JT) order, accompanied by |3x2 −r2⟩/|3y2 −r2⟩or- bital ordering, is stabilized within an A-type antiferromag- netic (AFM) insulating phase. At x = 1/4, this orbital or- der is suppressed and the ferromagnetic (FM) metallic phase becomes the ground state. At half doping (x = 1/2), a CE- type orbital and antiferromagnetic ordered insulating phase emerges.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27979_figures/2605.27979_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. (a) Schematic pseudocubic lattice of La0.75Ca0.25MnO3 (LCMO), indicated by dashed lines. The two inequivalent Mn sites are labeled 1 and 2. ϕ and θ denote the in-plane rotation and out-of-plane tilting angles, respectively. Purple and red spheres represent Mn and O atoms. The a, b, and c axes denote the global crystallographic axes, while x, y, and z denote the local axes of the MnO6 octahedron. (b) Schematic structural, electronic, and magnetic phase diagrams for different strain directions. Rhombus represent MnO6 units, with their shapes and sizes indicating the type and magnitude of the distortion. Red and blue indicate ferromagnetic (FM) and A-type antiferromagnetic (AFM) ground...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27979_figures/2605.27979_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Strain dependence of the averaged and site-difference of the asymmetric Jahn-Teller mode in (a) and (b), and of the breathing mode in (c) and (d), under uniaxial and biaxial strain. (e) and (f) show the in-plane rotation angle ϕ and out- of-plane tilting angle θ of the MnO6 octahedra, respectively, as defined in Fig. 2(a).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27979_figures/2605.27979_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Orbital angles for Mn sites 1 and 2, shown in (a) and (b), respectively, defined as tan−1(Q2/Q3) (see Fig. 2(a) for the site labels). The orbital characters |3x2 −r2⟩, |3y2 −r2⟩, and |x2 −y2⟩correspond to orbital angles of 120◦, 240◦, and 180◦, respectively. The black, red, green, blue, and purple curves represent biaxial strain and uniaxial strain applied along the (100), (010), (110), and (¯1¯10) directions, respec- tively.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27979_figures/2605.27979_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Partial charge density for the occupied eg char- acter along the c axis (Fig. 2 (b)) is presented. The strain along (100) and (010) directions has different orbital char- acters, which are |3x2 −r2⟩or |3y2 −r2⟩and |x2 −y2⟩, re- spectively. Further, the two different Mn sites have different electron occupancies. While the strain along (110) and (¯1¯10) directions has (100) and (010) directions orbital character, in between.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that applying uniaxial strain in specific crystallographic directions can selectively induce unique, intertwined charge, orbital, and lattice orders in manganites. It shows that strain direction, rather than just magnitude, is a critical parameter for engineering new electronic and magnetic phases in correlated oxides.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether direction-selective uniaxial strain can act as a qualitative tuning knob to stabilize previously inaccessible, intertwined electronic and magnetic phases in hole-doped manganites.

**Main result.** Different strain directions stabilize qualitatively distinct phases, such as cooperative Jahn-Teller, breathing-like, and site-selective modulations, with diagonal strain (110) specifically stabilizing a new CF-type order.

**Method.** Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations using the VASP package with the PBEsol functional and Hubbard U/Hund's J corrections.

**Model / system.** The prototypical Jahn-Teller system La0.75Ca0.25MnO3 (LCMO), modeled using an orthorhombic Pnma unit cell and an 8 formula unit supercell to capture structural and charge orders.

**Key observables.** Structural distortions (Jahn-Teller modes Q2, Q3 and breathing mode Q1), orbital polarization, charge disproportionation, and magnetic ground states (FM vs. A-type AFM).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Extreme uniaxial strain up to ~8-10% along (100), (010), (110), and (1-10) directions; Hubbard U = 4 eV; Hund's coupling J_H = 1 eV.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes the validity of the DFT+U approach for treating Mn-3d electron correlations and focuses on the extreme strain regime enabled by free-standing layer techniques.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the phase diagram of LCMO vs doping; Fig 2 illustrates the lattice structure and schematic phase diagrams for different strain directions; Fig 3 displays the strain dependence of JT and breathing modes and octahedral rotation/tilting angles.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of strain engineering, describes the DFT methodology, presents results for different strain directions (uniaxial vs biaxial), analyzes the driving mechanism of phase transitions, and provides supplementary structural and energy data.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The complex interplay of charge, spin, orbital, and lattice degrees of freedom governs emergent phases in quantum materials, making strain a powerful control parameter. Recent advances in free-standing layer techniques have enabled extreme strains of nearly 8%, opening access to novel and often unexpected electronic and magnetic phases. Here, using a density functional theory approach, we investigate the effect of direction-selective uniaxial strain on the prototypical Jahn-Teller system La1-xCaxMnO3 (x = 0.25). We find that different strain directions stabilize qualitatively distinct structural, charge, and orbital responses, rather than merely different strengths of the same phase. In particular, extreme uniaxial strain selectively induces cooperative Jahn-Teller, breathing-like, and site-selective modulations, thereby enabling previously inaccessible intertwined orders in manganites. These results establish direction-selective uniaxial strain as a powerful and selective route for engineering emergent phases in quantum materials.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.27522"></a>
### [Displaced Gaussian Boson Sampling for enhanced max-clique search](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27522v1)

**Authors:** Ewan Mer, Zhenghao Li, Shang Yu, Ian A. Walmsley, Raj B. Patel  
**Type:** both · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27522v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27522_figures/2605.27522_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Displaced Gaussian Boson Sampling for max-clique search. A weighted graph G = (V, E) is mapped to a set of (r, γ, U) where r and γ are the vectors of squeezing param- eters and displacements, respectively, and U represents the passive unitary operation. Photons are detected with click- detectors at each output mode. Each photon click pattern is considered a subgraph S ⊆G and is fed into a greedy shrink- ing/local search algorithm.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27522_figures/2605.27522_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Left panel: Probability of measuring the max-clique of a graph as a function of γ and c. Right panel: Adjacency matrix of the graph.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27522_figures/2605.27522_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. a) and b) Adjacency matrices. c) and d): Probability of measuring the max-clique of a graph as a function of γ and λmax = tanh rmax. e) and f): Maximal improvement of D-GBS with respect to GBS as a function of λmax by finding the optimal γ. a), c) and e): 8-node clique embedded in a 20-node Erd˝os-R´enyi graph with an edge probability of 0.2. b), d) and f): 14-node clique embedded in a 20-node Erd˝os- R´enyi graph with an edge probability of 0.2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27522_figures/2605.27522_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Success rate of finding a max-clique in the TaceAs [17] by using a D-GBS sampler (green) with a total displacement of |αtot|2 = 10 and total squeezing | sinh rtot|2 = 2.34, a GBS sampler (dashed line) with | sinh rtot|2 = 2.34, Oh’s sampler (blue) [34] and a uniform sampler (red) after running a classical post-processing routine that consists of 7 iterations of local search after greedy shrinking with 500 sam- ples.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27522_figures/2605.27522_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Probability of finding the max-clique as a func- tion of loss for different loop strengths γ. A low level of displacement like γ = 0.2 can be used to enhance the probabil- ity of the max-clique by increasing the probability of sampling the max-clique. For an arbitrary level of loss, the displace- ment can be tuned to maximize the sampling probability and outperform the ideal GBS case.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes Displaced Gaussian Boson Sampling (D-GBS) as a way to improve the efficiency of finding maximum cliques in graphs. By adding coherent state displacements to the GBS process, the researchers show that the success rate can be significantly boosted even in the presence of high photon loss or low squeezing. This method scales effectively to larger graphs with minimal additional resource overhead.

**Why it may be interesting.** This is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates how a specific type of non-classical resource (displacement) can be used to mitigate the detrimental effects of photon loss and limited squeezing in a computational task.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Enhancing the success rate of Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) for finding maximum-weighted cliques in undirected graphs, specifically overcoming performance degradation caused by photon loss and limited squeezing.

**Main result.** The introduction of Displaced Gaussian Boson Sampling (D-GBS) boosts the sampling probability of maximum-weighted cliques under high loss or low squeezing, providing a scalable enhancement with low resource overhead.

**Method.** The authors use numerical simulations (Strawberry Fields) to compare D-GBS against GBS and classical algorithms, employing a classical post-processing routine of greedy-shrinking and local search on photon click patterns.

**Model / system.** A photonic hardware platform utilizing Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) and its extension, Displaced Gaussian Boson Sampling (D-GBS), which incorporates single-mode squeezed vacuum states, a Haar-random linear optical network, and coherent state displacements.

**Key observables.** Photon number vectors, photon click patterns, and the success rate of identifying the maximum clique.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Squeezing parameters (r), displacement vectors (gamma), photon loss (eta), and the collision-free regime (M >> N^2).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes Haar-random unitaries, the anti-concentration property, and the collision-free regime; requires displacement to be a monotonic increasing function of node weight to avoid bias.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the D-GBS workflow; Figure 2 shows max-clique probability vs. gamma and squeezing; Figure 3 shows improvement across graph sizes; Figure 4 compares success rates against classical samplers; Figure 5 shows loss resilience; Figure 6 shows the effectiveness of local search; Figure 7 shows scaling of enhancement and resource ratios; Figure 8 shows probability distribution histograms.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of max-clique search in GBS, defines the D-GBS mathematical framework (loop hafnian), presents numerical results demonstrating enhancement under loss and low squeezing, analyzes scalability and complexity, and concludes with a comparison of resource overheads.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Gaussian Boson Sampling (GBS) is capable of solving certain classes of graph problems owing to the samples produced by such a device having a connection to the hafnian matrix function.   In particular, a GBS device has been shown to provide an enhancement in the search of cliques -- or complete subgraphs -- in undirected weighted graphs over classical algorithms.   A graph can be mapped to a GBS experiment by configuring the squeezing parameters of the input states and programming the linear optical network.   In practice, limited squeezing and photon loss degrade the performance of the GBS device for max-clique search.   In comparison, coherent states -- often considered a classical resource due to their Poissonian statistics -- can be readily prepared across many modes using an attenuated laser.   In this paper, we report an enhancement of the success rate of GBS in finding maximum weighted cliques by adding displacements under lossy conditions or when a limited amount of squeezing is available.   Moreover, we report that this enhancement can be scaled up to large graphs with limited resource overheads.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27923"></a>
### [Do We Really Need Quantum Machine Learning?: A Multidimensional Empirical Study](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27923v1)

**Authors:** Sudip Vhaduri, Ryan Gammon, Sayanton Dibbo  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27923v1>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive benchmark of quantum versus classical machine learning models for image recognition. It finds that while quantum models can be computationally expensive, they offer significant advantages in parameter and memory efficiency, particularly in high-dimensional data regimes.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Evaluating the practical utility and necessity of Quantum Machine Learning (QML) for computer vision tasks by identifying specific regimes where quantum models provide a tangible advantage over classical models.

**Main result.** Quantum models show greater relative accuracy advantages as feature dimensionality or sample size increases; specifically, QCNNs achieve comparable accuracy to CCNNs but with ~94% fewer parameters and ~75% less memory.

**Method.** A multidimensional benchmarking study comparing CSVM, QSVM, CCNN, and QCNN using the MNIST dataset, evaluating performance across varying feature counts, sample sizes, and CPU/GPU environments.

**Model / system.** The study uses simulated quantum hardware via the PennyLane library, implementing Quantum Support Vector Machines (QSVM) with angle embedding and Quantum Convolutional Neural Networks (QCNN) using variational quantum circuits with RX, RY, and CNOT gates.

**Key observables.** Classification accuracy, computational runtime, parameter count, and memory requirements.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Feature dimensionality (qubit count), sample size, and execution environment (CPU vs. GPU).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is limited to the MNIST dataset and relies on quantum simulations rather than real hardware; it assumes PCA components equal the number of qubits for encoding.

**Figures summary.** Bar graphs and dual-axis plots showing accuracy and runtime variations for SVMs across qubit/feature counts and sample sizes, and performance metrics (accuracy, runtime, parameters, memory) for CNNs across varying feature counts and sample sizes.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the motivation of QML in computer vision, details the experimental setup and models (SVM and CNN variants), presents a multidimensional comparative analysis of accuracy and efficiency, and concludes with potential applications in edge computing and limitations of the study.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The rapid growth of computer vision and increasingly complex image recognition tasks has exposed fundamental computational limitations of classical machine learning models, motivating the exploration of quantum computing as an emerging new paradigm. This paper presents a comprehensive benchmarking study of classical and quantum machine learning models for image recognition on the MNIST handwritten digit dataset, evaluating both traditional models, a Classical Support Vector Machine (CSVM) and a Quantum Support Vector Machine (QSVM), and deep neural network models, a Classical Convolutional Neural Network (CCNN) and a Quantum Convolutional Neural Network (QCNN), across four performance dimensions: classification accuracy, computational runtime, parameter count, and memory requirements. Experiments are conducted as functions of both feature dimensionality and sample size, and across CPU and GPU execution environments, providing a controlled, multidimensional comparison to address gaps in prior work. For the SVM-based models, QSVM consistently outperforms CSVM in accuracy, reaching $\sim$ 0.90 versus $\sim$ 0.85 at 1,000 samples, with a higher computational cost. A feature count of 10 qubits and a sample size in the range of 200 -- 500 emerge as practical operating points that balance accuracy and runtime. For the neural network models, CCNN and QCNN achieve comparable classification accuracy, both exceeding 0.96 at 64 features and 60,000 samples, yet QCNN offers substantially superior parameter and memory efficiency, requiring $\sim$ 94\% fewer parameters and $\sim$ 75\% less memory than CCNN at higher feature counts, while incurring higher runtime. Across both model families, quantum models consistently outperform classical models by greater margins in accuracy as feature dimensionality or sample size increases.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28795"></a>
### [Dynamic Entanglement Packet Scheduling for Quantum Networks](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28795v1)

**Authors:** Quang-Phong Tran, Claudio Cicconetti, Marco Conti, Andrea Passarella  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28795v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28795_figures/2605.28795_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1. A quantum network with a central controller that coordinates 5 nodes. Each has a certain number of quantum links.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28795_figures/2605.28795_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2. A toy example comparing the static (top) and dynamic (bottom) schedulers, where 3 applications a request one entanglement packet (∀a ∈A, Ia = 1) with periods Ta. 2 applications arrive at t0, while the last arrives at t6. Each scheduler schedules PGAa,k (time budgets), represented by solid double arrows. In each slot, an attempt of E2E entanglement is performed (entanglement generation and swapping with all the links of the selected path πa). Each color is associated with an application and an outcome represented by a symbol. The hyper-periods Hi are used only by the static scheduler. The dashed double arrows indicate specific runtime mechanisms of the dynamic scheduler (defer and...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28795_figures/2605.28795_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3. The static scheduler admission rate of feasible schedules vs. ppacket.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28795_figures/2605.28795_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Comparison between the static and dynamic schedulers: the left figure shows the completion ratio (top) and makespan (bottom) in logarithmic scale vs. ppacket; the right figure shows the 90th/95th percentile per-link utilization (top) and per-link waiting time (bottom) vs. ppacket.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28795_figures/2605.28795_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5. Performance on the load and the path length of the dynamic scheduler: the left figure shows the completion ratio and throughput according to an increasing load for different values of ppacket; the right figure shows the proportion of PGAs completed/dropped and fractions of deferred/retried based on the number of hops (for napps = 200, ppacket = 0.3). Note that a PGA can be deferred and retried as long as it can meet its deadline.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a dynamic scheduling algorithm for quantum networks to replace inefficient static schedules. By reacting to real-time successes and failures of entanglement generation, the scheduler improves network throughput and reliability. It is particularly effective at managing resource contention in high-load scenarios.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Efficiently sharing entanglement among multiple users in scalable quantum networks is difficult due to stochastic entanglement generation outcomes and asynchronous request arrivals, which cause static TDMA schedules to fail.

**Main result.** The proposed dynamic online scheduler achieves higher throughput, lower completion time, and a higher completion ratio than static EDF-based baselines, while demonstrating graceful degradation under network overload.

**Method.** The authors develop an online, event-driven scheduler that manages two priority queues (Event and Ready) to dynamically decide whether to admit, defer, retry, or drop entanglement distribution reservations based on real-time outcomes.

**Model / system.** A time-slotted quantum network model using an undirected graph where nodes perform entanglement generation and swapping. The architecture uses an on-demand 'entanglement packet' approach with Packet Generation Attempts (PGAs) across paths.

**Key observables.** Completion ratio, throughput, makespan, admission rate, link utilization, and waiting time.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Target success probability (p_packet), link generation probability (p_gen), Bell State Measurement success probability (p_bsm), application period (T_a), and path length (number of hops).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Entanglement generation is triggered only upon request; no multipartite entanglement is considered; routing is fixed to the shortest path; and the model assumes instantaneous entanglement swapping and simplified quantum memory.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a 5-node network topology; Figure 2 provides a toy example comparing static vs. dynamic scheduling success/failure; Figure 3 shows static admission rate vs. load; Figure 4 compares performance metrics (completion ratio, makespan, etc.) between the two schedulers; and a final figure shows the impact of path length on packet drops and retries.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the entanglement packet architecture, defines the mathematical network and application models, presents the proposed dynamic scheduling algorithm, describes the simulation setup using the GARR network, and concludes with a performance comparison and discussion of future work.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Sharing entanglement among multiple users remains a central challenge for scalable quantum networks. Recent work proposed an on-demand entanglement packet architecture in which a controller uses a Time Division Multiple Access (TDMA) approach to allocate network resources. Quantum nodes are assigned a periodic schedule that probabilistically fulfills application requests for end-to-end entanglements. The schedule is recomputed periodically using well-known algorithms, such as Earliest Deadline First (EDF). However, a static schedule offers limited flexibility when outcomes are stochastic and arrivals are asynchronous. To overcome this limitation, we propose an online scheduler that dynamically schedules, defers, retries, or drops entanglement distribution reservations. In our simulations, the dynamic scheduler achieves lower completion time, higher completion ratio, and higher throughput than the static baseline. Furthermore, when the network is overloaded, the dynamic scheduler continues to construct deadline-feasible schedules and degrades gracefully.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27540"></a>
### [EFaaS: A Quantum-Classical Serverless Entangled Scheduler for Hybrid Variational Algorithms](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27540v1)

**Authors:** Abolfazl Younesi, Nouhaila Innan, Alberto Marchisio, Muhammad Shafique  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27540v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27540_figures/2605.27540_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Iterative hybrid quantum-classical workflow for variational algorithms (VQE/QAOA), where a classical optimizer updates cir- cuit parameters based on measurements from a quantum processor, forming a feedback loop between classical and quantum domains</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27540_figures/2605.27540_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Session Awareness Engine state machine. A Hot Iterator cycles between SESSION ACTIVE and CACHE VALID provided each inter-shot delay ∆t remains within τd = τdrift. If the cache expires, the engine triggers a localized recalibration before resuming the active session.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27540_figures/2605.27540_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: EFaaS System Architecture. The middleware intercepts Clas- sical FaaS triggers and Quantum Execution requests (Control Plane). The “Session State Cache” maintains QPU calibration data (Cq) while the “Asynchronous Future Engine” allows the CPU to speculatively advance while awaiting QPU results.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27540_figures/2605.27540_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4: EF-QuantumFuture execution sequence. The Middleware returns an asynchronous Future f immediately upon submission, allowing the classical optimizer to perform speculative work (tasync) concurrently with QPU execution (tqpu). The f.resolve() call delivers ⟨H⟩once the QPU completes, and the optimizer proceeds to θi+1 without any blocking wait.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27540_figures/2605.27540_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 7: End-to-end convergence time and EFaaS speedup ratio relative to all four baselines. EFaaS converges in a mean of 22.9 s versus 213 s for SBQ and 320 s for PF, achieving speedups of 83.2%–98.3% across the benchmark suite.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes EFaaS, a serverless middleware designed to optimize the execution of hybrid quantum-classical algorithms. By treating classical optimization and quantum execution as entangled, session-aware events, it minimizes the latency and hardware drift that typically plague cloud-based quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** High latency and 'quantum drift' in hybrid variational quantum algorithms (VQAs) caused by decoupled, batch-queue cloud architectures that break the iterative loop between classical optimizers and QPUs.

**Main result.** The EFaaS middleware achieves convergence speedups of 83.2%-98.3% and significantly reduces Time-to-Next-Shot (TTNS) by up to 94.3% while maintaining near-zero hardware drift penalties.

**Method.** A serverless middleware architecture using a Calibration-Aware placement strategy, a Dual-Resource Fair Queuing (DRFQ) scheduler, and an 'EF-QuantumFuture' programming primitive for speculative classical execution.

**Model / system.** A distributed hybrid quantum-classical environment consisting of a pool of QPUs (superconducting and ion-trap) and classical FaaS nodes, simulating VQE and QAOA workloads on Hamiltonians like LiH and the transverse-field Ising chain.

**Key observables.** Time-to-Next-Shot (TTNS), Quantum Duty Cycle (QDC), end-to-end convergence time, and drift penalty events.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Drift threshold (tau_drift), calibration validity window, and scheduling weights (alpha, beta, gamma) for session awareness, drift urgency, and fair-share.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The effectiveness of stochastic optimizers relies on the assumption of i.i.d. sampling, which is violated by hardware drift; the system assumes a discrete-event simulation model for traffic.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the hybrid iterative workflow; Figure 2 details the EFaaS architecture; Figure 3 depicts the Session Awareness Engine state machine; Figure 4 illustrates the overlapping execution of classical and quantum tasks; Figure 8 and 9 show the reduction in drift events and calibration overhead.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the latency/drift problem, proposes the EFaaS architecture and its three technical innovations, provides a mathematical model for latency and scheduling, presents experimental results via a discrete-event simulator, and performs ablation and sensitivity studies.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

As quantum computing enters the Utility Era, realizing near-term advantage relies heavily on Hybrid Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs). These algorithms require a tightly coupled, iterative loop between a classical CPU optimizer and a Quantum Processing Unit (QPU). However, current quantum cloud access models are bottlenecked by decoupled batch-queues that sever this loop, introducing massive Time-to-Next-Shot (TTNS) latency. This delay inflates convergence time from minutes to hours and exposes the computation to quantum hardware drift, degrading algorithmic fidelity. Unlike prior works that rely on resource-wasting static hardware reservations or state-oblivious stateless functions, we propose EFaaS, a novel serverless middleware designed specifically for hybrid quantum workflows. EFaaS fundamentally departs from existing architectures by treating classical parameter optimization and quantum circuit execution as entangled, session-aware events. Our main technical innovations are threefold: (1) a Calibration-Aware placement strategy that dynamically routes circuits to QPUs with warm calibration caches, circumventing cold-start penalties, (2) a Dual-Resource Fair Queuing scheduler that maximizes quantum utilization by strictly prioritizing active iterative loops, and (3) the "EF-QuantumFuture" programming abstraction, a novel primitive enabling classical speculative execution to mask compute latency. Across the evaluated baselines, EFaaS achieves TTNS reductions of 11.4%-94.3%, QDC gains of 2.02%-15.78% points, and convergence speedups of 83.2%-98.3%, while eliminating drift penalties.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

**Summary.** This paper explores how disorder can induce new topological phases in interacting systems. It specifically demonstrates the emergence of an antiferromagnetic topological Anderson insulator in the Haldane model, showing that disorder can effectively replace a staggered mass to create a C=1 phase. The study also showcases the utility of neural networks in identifying topological phase boundaries in disordered many-body systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether an antiferromagnetic (AFM) C=1 topological phase can be induced by Anderson disorder in the absence of a staggered mass in the interacting Haldane model.

**Main result.** The study identifies an antiferromagnetic topological Anderson insulator phase, demonstrating that Anderson disorder can generate the charge imbalance necessary to induce the C=1 phase.

**Method.** The authors use Exact Diagonalization (ED) on a 12A cluster and a Convolutional Neural Network (CNN) trained on the single-particle density matrix to predict Chern numbers and resolve phase boundaries.

**Model / system.** A spinful Haldane model on a honeycomb lattice including Hubbard (U) and nearest-neighbor density-density (V) interactions, subject to onsite Anderson disorder (W) and a staggered potential (Delta).

**Key observables.** Many-body Chern number (C), Spin Density Wave (SDW) structure factor, and Charge Density Wave (CDW) structure factor.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Hubbard interaction (U), density-density interaction (V), Anderson disorder strength (W), and staggered mass (Delta).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The ground state is assumed to be non-degenerate for the use of the Abelian Berry connection; results are limited by finite-size effects of the 12A cluster.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows clean model phase diagrams; Figure 2 shows CDW/SDW diagrams; Figure 3 shows phase diagrams at specific disorder strengths; Figure 4 shows CNN-predicted phase diagrams; Figure 5 compares ED and CNN results; Figure 6 shows SDW structure factor and phase classification percentages.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins with the clean model phase diagrams, moves to the introduction of disorder and the identification of the AFM topological phase, presents the machine learning methodology for phase prediction, and concludes with a comparison of ED and CNN results and the analysis of phase competition.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We examine the emergence of topological Anderson insulating phases in the spinful Haldane model with Hubbard and next-neighbor density-density interactions, subject to Anderson disorder. Using finite-size exact diagonalization, we characterize the phases that arise from the interplay between topology, interactions, and disorder. In addition to standard $C=2$ topological Anderson phases, we observe an antiferromagnetic $C=1$ topological Anderson phase, consistent with the antiferromagnetic quantum anomalous Hall insulator previously identified in the clean model at finite staggered mass. We further analyze these phases using a neural network trained on the exact diagonalization data. Our results support the hypothesis that an explicit charge imbalance is required to induce the $C=1$ phase, generated by Anderson disorder rather than by a staggered mass.

</details>

**Summary.** This review provides a comprehensive overview of the mechanisms and engineering strategies used to create high-performance molecular rectifiers. **Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of developing molecular rectifiers that are competitive with silicon-based devices by overcoming limited rectification ratios, poor robustness, and low reproducibility.

**Main result.** The review provides a comprehensive framework for engineering molecular rectifiers through structural, interfacial, and quantum interference engineering, demonstrating that rectification can be tuned via molecular design and external stimuli.

**Method.** The paper integrates theoretical frameworks like DFT-NEGF and Landauer-Büttiker formalism with experimental platforms such as STM-BJ, MCBJ, and self-assembled monolayers.

**Model / system.** The physical system consists of single-molecule junctions and molecular ensembles (e.g., D-sigma-A architectures) linked to metallic or carbon-based electrodes.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a logical progression from historical evolution and fundamental transport mechanisms (AR, KBW, DP models) to specific modulation strategies (structural, interfacial, and quantum interference engineering) and concludes with future outlooks for device integration.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Molecular rectifiers, as prototypical components of molecular electronics, present unique opportunities for pushing device miniaturization to its ultimate limits. Nevertheless, challenges including limited rectification ratios (RR), insufficient robustness, and poor reproducibility impede their practical deployment. To make molecular rectifiers competitive with silicon-based devices, it is important to fully understand the design principles and fabrication methods from both mechanistic and experimental perspectives. By holistically considering the transport mechanisms, modulation strategies, fabrication, characterization techniques, and theoretical simulations, this review provides a comprehensive overview of molecular rectifiers. Representative examples of conceptually significant and high-performance molecular rectifier systems are highlighted to illustrate the relationships between rectification mechanisms, molecular design strategies, and device realization. Building on these discussions, we present an outlook for current bottlenecks and future directions to guide the development of molecular rectifiers. This review aims to serve as both a conceptual framework and a technical reference for researchers working at the intersection of molecular electronics and nanoscale device engineering in the post-CMOS era.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28326"></a>
### [Gauge Geometry of Hodge Zero-Mode Transport in Parameter-Dependent Topological Data Analysis](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28326v1)

**Authors:** Satoshi Kanno, Rei Nishimura, Hiroshi Yamauchi, Yoshi-aki Shimada  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28326v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28326_figures/2605.28326_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1 Vineyard-like tracking used in Experiment 1. The resulting vineyard-like tracking, including the elder vine and crossing windows.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28326_figures/2605.28326_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28326_figures/2605.28326_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4 Vineyard-style tracking of the top two persistence points in Experiment 2.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28326_figures/2605.28326_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 5 Time series of three quantities in Experiment 2: the distance between the top two persistence points, the tracking ambiguity margin, and the maximum curvature. The distance and ambiguity margin become nearly zero at the swap time, while the curvature attains its peak in the same region.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new way to track how topological features in data evolve over time or external parameters. By treating homology as a geometric bundle, it uses concepts like curvature and holonomy to detect structural changes that traditional methods miss.

**Why it may be interesting.** The use of Berry connection, curvature, and holonomy to describe the evolution of topological features is directly analogous to the study of geometric phases and topological invariants in many-body systems and driven quantum dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Standard Topological Data Analysis (TDA) tools like persistence diagrams fail to capture how homological feature spaces reorganize, mix, or transport when data depends on external parameters like time or control variables.

**Main result.** The authors develop a gauge-geometric framework using the Hodge Laplacian to describe the transport of homology, proving that curvature and holonomy are stable descriptors of structural reorganization in regular parameter regions.

**Method.** The framework uses the combinatorial Hodge Laplacian to represent homology as a zero-mode bundle, applying Berry-type connection, curvature, and holonomy to track feature evolution.

**Model / system.** A parameter-dependent family of simplicial complexes (e.g., Vietoris-Rips filtrations) where the topology is studied as a vector bundle over a parameter space (time, temperature, or external fields).

**Key observables.** Curvature (local reorganization), Holonomy (accumulated memory/monodromy), and zero-mode projections.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Filtration scale (a), external parameter (lambda), spectral gap (gamma), and smoothing parameter (epsilon).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The theory assumes 'regular regions' where the zero-mode multiplicity is constant and a spectral gap exists; it also uses a smooth approximation for the discrete Laplacian.

**Figures summary.** The figures likely include curvature heatmaps identifying reorganization regions, comparisons between vineyard-style tracking and holonomy-guided tracking, and stability plots showing error bounds under noise.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the limitations of existing TDA, constructs a smooth Hodge-type operator and a zero-mode vector bundle, defines geometric quantities (connection, curvature, holonomy), provides stability proofs via Riesz projection, and validates the method through numerical experiments on time-dependent point clouds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We propose a practical computational framework for detecting structural changes in parameter-dependent topological data. In many applications, such as time-series data analysis, anomaly detection, and monitoring of systems under changing control parameters, persistence diagrams describe the birth and death of topological features at each parameter value, but they do not fully capture how these features are reorganized over time. To address this limitation, we represent homological features by zero modes of the ordinary combinatorial Hodge Laplacian and track the corresponding feature spaces in a common ambient chain space. This allows us to compute curvature and holonomy as descriptors of local reorganization and accumulated memory in evolving topological structures. Curvature highlights parameter regions where homological features mix or change rapidly, while holonomy summarizes the net effect of such changes after a closed cycle. We also establish stability estimates showing that these descriptors are robust under perturbations of the Hodge Laplacian on regular regions. Numerical experiments on controlled time-dependent point-cloud data show that the proposed method detects tracking instability, distinguishes systems with nearly identical persistence diagrams, and captures cycle-level memory invisible to pointwise feature matching. These results suggest that zero-mode transport geometry can serve as a useful computational tool for analyzing dynamic topological data.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27795"></a>
### [Geometric Analysis of Variational Quantum Eigensolver](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27795v1)

**Authors:** Zhen Qin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27795v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27795_figures/2605.27795_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1: (a) Convergence behavior of RGD for varying N with µ0 = 0.2, n = 6, and σ = 0.05; (b) Initialization error for different σ and n with N = 16; (c) Comparison between uniform and non-uniform measurement allocation under varying total budget Mtot with µ0 = 0.2, n = 6, N = 8, and σ = 0.05.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a unified geometric analysis of the VQE optimization landscape, proving that the landscape is free of spurious local minima. It establishes convergence guarantees for Riemannian Gradient Descent and provides a theoretical link between circuit depth and the emergence of barren plateaus.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a rigorous geometric foundation for understanding why VQE can be trained and how circuit depth and measurement noise fundamentally impact the convergence of variational algorithms.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The lack of a unified geometric characterization of the VQE optimization landscape, specifically addressing the fragmentation between fixed-ansatz and adaptive-circuit formulations and the challenges of barren plateaus and noise robustness.

**Main result.** The paper establishes that the VQE landscape is geometrically benign (no spurious local minima, only global minima or strict saddles) and proves that Riemannian Gradient Descent (RGD) achieves linear convergence, though the rate deteriorates polynomially with circuit depth.

**Method.** The authors use Riemannian geometry on the unitary group, analyzing the landscape via the Riemannian Hessian and applying Riemannian Gradient Descent (RGD) with a polar-based retraction.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the Variational Quantum Eigensolver (VQE) framework using parameterized quantum circuits (product-unitary ansatz) to approximate the ground-state energy of a Hamiltonian H.

**Key observables.** Ground-state energy expectation value, Riemannian gradient, and Riemannian Hessian.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Circuit depth (N), spectral gap (delta_1), Hamiltonian norm (||H||), and measurement budget (M).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes smooth parametrization of the ansatz layers, use of Pauli-decomposed Hamiltonians for noise analysis, and an initial state within a specific energy error threshold.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a unified ansatz-free product-unitary formulation, analyzes the landscape geometry (global minima and strict saddles), investigates initialization feasibility via random Pauli rotations, and concludes with a study of robustness under finite-shot measurement noise.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Variational Quantum Eigensolver (VQE) is a fundamental algorithm in quantum computing, yet a coherent geometric characterization of VQE remains missing due to fragmented analyses across fixed-ansatz and adaptive-circuit formulations. In this paper, we establish a geometric analysis of VQE in terms of optimization landscape, initialization guarantee, and noise robustness. First, we study the optimization landscape via an ansatz-free product-unitary formulation over the unitary group, unifying both paradigms. For the single-unitary case, we establish linear convergence of Riemannian gradient descent (RGD) and prove the strict saddle property. For the product-unitary case, we show the convergence rate deteriorates polynomially with circuit depth, providing a geometric explanation of the barren plateau phenomenon. Second, we prove that small-angle random Pauli-rotation circuits satisfy the required initialization conditions with high probability. Third, we show that RGD retains linear convergence under finite-shot measurements, and that coefficient-adaptive allocation achieves strictly lower statistical error than uniform sampling under a fixed measurement budget.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper identifies Li3Co2SbO6 as a Kitaev A-type antiferromagnet using inelastic neutron scattering. It demonstrates that the magnetic excitations and field-induced transitions are accurately described by an extended Kitaev-Heisenberg model. This provides a new material platform for studying Kitaev physics in a ferromagnetic-plane system.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to characterize the magnetic properties and exchange interactions of the layered honeycomb magnet Li3Co2SbO6 to determine if it acts as a Kitaev ferromagnet.

**Main result.** The researchers established Li3Co2SbO6 as a Kitaev A-type antiferromagnet, where magnetic properties are well-modeled by an extended Kitaev-Heisenberg model including significant Kitaev and off-diagonal Gamma interactions.

**Method.** Inelastic Neutron Scattering (INS) was used for experimental measurements, while Linear Spin Wave Theory (LSWT) and numerical energy minimization were used for theoretical modeling and parameter fitting.

**Model / system.** The system is Li3Co2SbO6, a layered cobalt-based quantum magnet with an A-type antiferromagnetic ground state. The model is an extended Kitaev-Heisenberg Hamiltonian including bond-dependent Kitaev (K), Heisenberg (J1), and off-diagonal (Gamma and Gamma') exchange terms.

**Key observables.** Magnetic Bragg reflections, dynamical structure factor (DSF), magnon excitation spectra, and magnetization curves.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic ordering temperature TN = 14 K; exchange parameters (J1, K, Gamma, Gamma', Jperp) = (-1.57, -5.90, 0.97, 0.35, 0.50) meV; magnetic field strengths up to 7 T.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The magnetic unit cell is assumed to remain stable under an applied field, and the analysis uses a Holstein-Primakoff expansion truncated to leading order.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the crystal structure; Fig 2 shows INS spectra at different wavelengths; Fig 3/4 shows the calculated DSF compared to experiment; Fig 5 shows neutron powder diffraction under magnetic fields; Fig 7 shows INS spectra at 7 T; Fig 8 shows classical spin configurations; Fig 10/11 show the evolution of spectra and gap opening with field orientation; Fig 12 checks for sample reorientation.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the material and its importance, describes the crystal structure and experimental setup, presents zero-field INS and diffraction results, details the theoretical modeling using LSWT, investigates the effects of external magnetic fields on both diffraction and dynamics, and concludes with a comparison to related cobalt-based compounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Cobalt-based quantum magnets forming layered honeycomb arrangements have attracted much attention recently, as they are considered as a potential platform for materials with exotic Kitaev spin exchange. Amongst the discussed candidate materials are Na$_3$Co$_2$SbO$_6$ and Na$_2$Co$_2$TeO$_6$, both possessing a low-temperature ground state with magnetic zigzag ordering, similar to Na$_2$IrO$_3$ and $α$-RuCl$_3$. Here we report inelastic neutron scattering experiments on the quantum magnet Li$_3$Co$_2$SbO$_6$, which features ferromagnetic honeycomb planes with opposite magnetizations in neighboring planes. By comparing with linear spin wave theory, we show that the magnetic properties of Li$_3$Co$_2$SbO$_6$ can be well-modelled by an extended Kitaev--Heisenberg model, establishing it as a Kitaev-ferromagnet, or more specifically, as a Kitaev A-type antiferromagnet. Our analysis is complemented by magnetic field measurements and simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27777_figures/2605.27777_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Global phase shift due to mean-field interactions ob- served by biasing the interferometer arms with an imbalance IN of 0.1 (squares) and 0.7 (circles), for texp = 8.4 ms. Er- ror bars represent the standard error on the mean. Shaded bands show the theoretical results from a mean-field model in- corporating typical 5% number fluctuations (see text). Inset: absorption image of atoms after the first π/2 pulse showing a number imbalance of 0.7.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27777_figures/2605.27777_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Comparison of BEC (texp = 16 ms) fringes with those from a thermal source (initially at 350 nK, texp = 8.4 ms). (a) Fringes at three hold times for ∆z = 15d for thermal (red diamonds) and BEC (blue circles) sources. (b) Total atom number as a function of hold time. (c) Statistical phase error from sinusoidal fits. The thermal data has an average of 5 points per phase scanned, whereas the BEC has 4.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates an atom interferometer using a Ytterbium Bose-Einstein Condensate trapped in an optical lattice. The authors show that interatomic interactions can be controlled by tuning the density of the BEC, and that using a BEC source can significantly reduce statistical phase error compared to thermal sources.

**Why it may be interesting.** It explores the transition from single-atom physics to many-body dynamics in a sensing platform, providing insights into how mean-field effects can be harnessed or mitigated for improved quantum metrology.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how mean-field interactions and density-dependent effects impact the coherence, phase, and performance of a lattice-trapped atom interferometer using a BEC source.

**Main result.** The researchers demonstrated direct observation and control of atomic interactions by tuning BEC density, showing that a BEC source can outperform a thermal source in statistical phase error and atom flux.

**Method.** The experiment uses a 1D optical lattice-trapped interferometer with a Ytterbium BEC, employing Bragg pulses for splitting and Bloch oscillations for path separation, while varying expansion time and atom number to control density.

**Model / system.** A 1D optical lattice-trapped interferometer using a 174-Yb Bose-Einstein Condensate. The system is modeled using the Gross-Pitaevskii equation and a mean-field model for density-induced phase shifts.

**Key observables.** Interferometer contrast (C), global phase shift (delta-phi), atom number asymmetry (A), and statistical phase error.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Lattice depth (4-5 Erecc), peak density (approx 2.5e14 cm^-3), s-wave scattering length (5.6 nm), and Bloch oscillation period (836 us).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes a self-similar ballistic expansion of the BEC and neglects mean-field effects on the velocity distribution after the velocity selection pulse.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the interferometer sequence; Fig 2 shows contrast decay vs hold time and separation; Fig 3 shows contrast vs expansion time and atom number; Fig 5 compares BEC and thermal source performance; Fig 6 shows a single fringe at long hold time.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the lattice-trapped paradigm, describes the experimental setup and BEC preparation, details the interaction control methods, presents results on contrast and phase shifts, compares BEC vs thermal sources, and discusses decoherence and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Precision interferometry with atomic wavepackets confined in a one-dimensional optical lattice is an emergent paradigm in quantum sensing of forces and fields, with applications in gravimetry, accelerometry, geophysics, and fundamental physics tests. We report on the realization of a lattice-trapped interferometer where the two arms are sourced from a weakly-interacting ytterbium Bose-Einstein condensate, coherently split and trapped by pulsed optical standing waves before recombination. We directly observe atomic interactions through contrast changes and phase shifts of the interferometer. By changing either the atom number or the sample volume to vary the density, we demonstrate control over interactions and optimize interferometer performance. Our observations are effectively captured by a mean-field theoretical model of the system. This work experimentally probes the boundary where improved performance from source brightening through higher phase space density transitions into a regime beyond single-atom physics in lattice-trapped atom interferometry, and opens a door to incorporating many-body effects for metrological advances in such platforms.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28162"></a>
### [Learning Logical Operations for Arbitrary Quantum Error Correction Codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28162v1)

**Authors:** Nico Meyer, Christopher Mutschler, Dominik Seuß, Andreas Maier, Daniel D. Scherer

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

The parameters are trained with an operation loss, measuring the average fidelity over two-design states between prediction and target (blue lines). Structured operation ans¨atze are used to enforce transversal or low-depth properties. We extend this pipeline to incorporate an adaptable encoding Uenc(Θ), re- sulting in a co-design procedure called variational early fault- tolerant quantum computing...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28162_figures/2605.28162_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Variational learning pipeline for a logical operation U on a fixed code C using only the encoding unitary Uenc. Logical input states are drawn from a unitary two-design S on the relevant logical subsystem, encoded, and passed through the parameterized prediction UL(Ψ). The resulting encoded states are compared to the target encoded states (UρU †)L using the fidelity-based operation error ∆F , and the parameters Ψ are optimized to minimize the average-case operation loss OS(C; Ψ).</sub>

The resulting predicted state is compared to the target one using the operation error defined in Eq. (12).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28162_figures/2605.28162_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Illustration of different loss variants on the target two-design hexagon (shown in gray), embedded in the Bloch sphere (a). The diagonal variant in (b) ‘pulls’ the physical prediction (black hexagon) towards the logical target for each two-design state. The block-diagonal extension in (c) ad- ditionally ‘repulses’ physical predictions from logical targets that are to be orthogonal. The third variant of evaluating on the full set of state pairs is not explicitly shown.</sub>

<sub>FIG. 5. Schematic ansatz families used for learning logical op- erations. **Summary.** This paper introduces a variational framework called VarEFTQC for learning the physical implementation of logical gates in any quantum error-correcting code. By using a block-diagonal loss function, the method avoids local minima and can even co-design new non-additive codes tailored to specific hardware noise. This provides a practical tool for designing hardware-adapted logical gadgets for the early fault-tolerant era.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Discovering physical implementations of logical operations for arbitrary quantum error-correcting codes, particularly for non-additive codes that lack a stabilizer description.

**Main result.** The authors developed the VarEFTQC framework, which successfully re-discovers known transversal gates and can co-design noise-tailored non-additive encodings with desired logical gate sets.

**Method.** A variational learning-based framework using parameterized unitary ansätze and a block-diagonal loss function optimized via L-BFGS to minimize fidelity error over a unitary two-design.

**Model / system.** Quantum error-correcting codes (both stabilizer and non-additive/learned) where logical qubits are encoded into larger physical Hilbert spaces, subject to various noise models including Pauli, depolarizing, and biased noise.

**Key observables.** Fidelity-based operation loss, distinguishability loss, and the Gram matrix of pairwise fidelities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Code parameters (n, k, d), ansatz depth (layer repetition), and noise parameters (p_X, p_Y, p_Z).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The framework assumes access to the encoding unitary (U_enc) and focuses on gate implementation rather than decoder discovery; it also assumes independent errors on physical qubits.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the learning framework schematic; Figure 2 illustrates the variational pipeline; Figure 3 shows the circuit for two-qubit gates; Figure 4 visualizes the geometric effect of loss variants on the Bloch sphere; Figure 5 shows the ansatz hierarchy; Figure 6 compares loss function success rates and ansatz depth; Figure 8/9/10/12 visualize training dynamics and Gram matrix structures.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of logical gate discovery, defines the VarEFTQC co-design framework, details the learning pipeline and loss function variants (diagonal, block-diagonal, full), validates the method on stabilizer codes, and demonstrates application to noise-tailored non-additive codes.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Logical operations are essential for quantum computation within quantum error-correcting codes. However, discovering their physical realizations is challenging, especially for non-additive codes that lack a stabilizer description. We present a general learning-based framework that, given only an encoding circuit, constructs physical implementations of logical operations while enforcing structural properties such as transversality or shallow depth. Our approach is validated by rediscovering known logical operations of standard stabilizer codes. We then extend it to a co-design procedure, dubbed variational early fault-tolerant quantum computing (VarEFTQC), which tailors non-additive encodings to a given noise model and enforces desired logical gate sets, such as transversal IQP-type families or low-depth universal sets. A software library implements the complete learning pipeline, including loss-function variants, ansatz families, and optimization routines. Together, these results position VarEFTQC as a practical tool for discovering hardware-adapted logical gadgets for early fault-tolerant quantum computing.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27598"></a>
### [Leveraging Correlated Decoding for Bias-Tailored Compass Codes](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27598v1)

**Authors:** Arianna Meinking, Julie Campos, Kenneth R. Brown  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27598v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27598_figures/2605.27598_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. Schematic of an elongated compass code with elongation ℓ= 3 of distance d = 7. X stabilizers are blue, Z stabilizers are red. The qubits are at the vertices of the lattice. We apply the ZXXZ□Clifford deformation on this code by applying Hadamard transformation on the yellow qubits. The weight-2 X stabilizers detect the location of Z errors with high accuracy, leading to larger thresholds under noise models with dominant Z errors.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27598_figures/2605.27598_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

Readout in our circuit happens for each syndrome qubit at the end of each of d rounds.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27598_figures/2605.27598_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. CSS correlated decoding in (a), (b) compared to PyMatching correlated decoding in (c) and MWPM in (d). (a) The thresholds of the compass codes with CSS correlated decoding over bias η correcting X errors, then Z errors. (b) The thresholds for CORRZX (Z error then X error) decoding at each ℓ. (c) PyMatching correlated decoder thresholds over a range of η. To achieve a similar to code-capacity decoding scheme with the PyMatching correlated decoder, we use d rounds of memory with the Pauli channel in section II acting on each data qubit before the rounds (d) Standard MWPM thresholds compared to the bias η for different ℓ.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27598_figures/2605.27598_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. The circuit-level correlated decoding of the Clifford-deformed elongated compass codes and the standard MWPM directly compared over η. (a) The CSS elongated compass codes threshold compared to bias η with MWPM, and (b) with correlated MWPM. (c), (d) The thresholds of the ZXXZ□-deformed code over a range of η with MWPM in (c) and correlated MWPM in (d). The surface code (ℓ= 2 CSS) experiences no change with η.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27598_figures/2605.27598_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5. Threshold gain (∆CORR) and relative gain (∆CORR/pMW P M th ) of correlated decoding compared to standard MWPM over a range of biases η for CSS and ZXXZ□-deformed elongated compass codes. The CSS codes experience tradeoffs in threshold since high stabilizer asymmetry is preferred to maximize correlated decoding gains. The ∆CORR of ZXXZ□codes with increasing η, though the maximum gain is achieved by the CSS codes. The relative correlated decoding gain, ∆CORR/pMW P M th , plateaus at high η for both the CSS and ZXXZ□-deformed codes. The relative gain of the correlated decoder on the ZXXZ□-deformed compass codes improves with elongation for all biases.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper demonstrates that using correlated decoding techniques can significantly boost the performance of quantum error correction for codes specifically designed for biased noise. By accounting for the relationship between X and Z errors, the researchers found higher error thresholds in realistic circuit-level noise environments.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Improving the error-correction thresholds of Clifford-deformed compass codes under biased circuit-level noise by leveraging correlations between X and Z errors.

**Main result.** Correlated decoding significantly enhances error thresholds for all noise biases under circuit-level noise, with the relative gain being higher for codes with asymmetric stabilizers.

**Method.** Large-scale Monte Carlo simulations using Stim and PyMatching, comparing standard MWPM with correlated MWPM (two-stage and hyperedge-informed) using conditional probabilities.

**Model / system.** Clifford-deformed elongated compass codes (specifically the ZXXZ deformation) subject to an asymmetric Pauli noise channel (biased noise) and circuit-level noise models.

**Key observables.** Error thresholds (p_th), logical error rates for X and Z memories, and the complementary gap (g) for decoder confidence.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Noise bias (eta), elongation parameter (l), code distance (d), and decoding order (ZX vs XZ).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study assumes a biased noise model typical of platforms like trapped ions or superconducting qubits and relies on the accuracy of the estimated conditional error probabilities.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the code schematic; Figure 2 depicts the deformed stabilizer and its extraction circuit; Figure 3 shows thresholds for different decoding orders; Figure 4 compares thresholds for different decoders; Figure 5 plots absolute and relative gains vs bias; Figure 6 shows gate ordering to avoid hook errors.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of biased noise in QEC, describes the Clifford-deformed compass code structure, details the correlated decoding algorithm and noise models, presents simulation results comparing thresholds and gains, and concludes with performance analysis across different parameters.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Quantum error correction (QEC) is often implemented on hardware that experiences biased noise, where dephasing errors occur more frequently than other errors. This has motivated many recent efforts to develop bias-tailored QEC codes, such as the Clifford-deformed compass codes: a family of codes that achieve high thresholds under biased dephasing noise. We perform circuit-level simulations of the Clifford-deformed elongated compass codes under a biased noise model and evaluate code thresholds using standard minimum weight perfect matching (MWPM) and correlated MWPM. We find that correlated decoding enhances thresholds for all noise biases relative to standard MWPM under circuit-level noise. Our results demonstrate that correlated decoding leads to a higher relative gain in thresholds compared to standard MWPM when applied to codes with asymmetric stabilizers under biased noise.

</details>

### [Projection operator onto spin-S eigenspaces of total and orbital angular momenta](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27637v1)

**Authors:** M. I. Krivoruchenko  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27637v1>

**Summary.** This paper develops an algebraic method to construct projection operators for total and orbital angular momentum. By providing an expansion in terms of polarization operators, it allows for the efficient separation of radial and angular components in calculations involving higher-spin particles in symmetric potentials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The construction of an efficient projection operator onto the simultaneous spin-S and orbital angular momentum eigenspaces to simplify matrix element calculations.

**Main result.** The derivation of a rotation-covariant projection operator expressed as both a polynomial in the scalar product of spin and orbital angular momentum and a finite expansion of polarization operators.

**Method.** The use of the Frobenius covariant and algebraic expansion techniques involving irreducible tensor operators and polarization operators.

**Model / system.** Particles with spin-S in spherically symmetric potentials, with applications ranging from nuclear shell models to relativistic particles after 3D reduction.

**Key observables.** Transition matrix elements, decay rates, magnetic dipole moments, and electric quadrupole moments.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Quantum numbers J, L, S, and M; particle energy E; and spatial coordinates r, r'.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The derivation assumes spherical symmetry in the potential or reference frame.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of angular momentum projection, develops the Frobenius covariant approach, provides two equivalent algebraic representations, establishes a connection to Villars' projection, and demonstrates applicability to higher-spin particles and relativistic reductions.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The Frobenius covariant is used to construct a projection operator onto the spin-S eigenspaces associated with the squares of the total and orbital angular momenta. The covariant admits two equivalent representations: as a polynomial in powers of the scalar product of the spin and orbital angular momentum operators, and as a finite expansion in terms of their respective polarization operators. A correspondence is established with Villars' angular momentum projection, used in nuclear structure studies.

</details>
