<a id="paper-2605.28754"></a>
### [Quantum Geometric Limits for Non-Abelian Holonomies](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28754v1)

**Authors:** François Impens, David Guéry-Odelin  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28754v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28754_figures/2605.28754_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Non-Abelian brachistochrones: Open (red) and closed (blue) brachistochrones for (a,b) U ∗ 1 = ei π</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28754_figures/2605.28754_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. QGL efficiency η = A(Σ(C))/Φ as a function of the rotation-axis orientation for the target gate U ∗(α) = eiΦ ˆn(α)·σ, with fixed angle Φ = π/4 and ˆn(α) = cos α ˆy + sin α ˆz. For each axis orientation, the optimization was re- peated 10 times from different initial seeds. The solid black line shows the lowest-cost solution found for each angle, the dashed line the median over all runs, and the blue shaded region the dispersion across runs. Insets (1,2) display the scalar field ˆvσ(s1, s2)·ˆv0, which measures the local alignment of the transported curvature over the optimal surface. Here ˆvσ(s1, s2) is the unit vector associated with the Lie-algebra di- rection of the transported...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes a universal Quantum Geometric Limit for non-Abelian holonomies, proving that the magnitude of a holonomy is bounded by the surface integral of its curvature norm. It introduces a method to find optimal control paths by treating the problem as a non-Abelian version of the brachistochrone problem. This is significant for optimizing the efficiency of non-Abelian gate implementations in quantum computing.

**Why it may be interesting.** It provides a fundamental geometric counterpart to Quantum Speed Limits, offering a way to quantify the 'geometric overhead' and resource costs for implementing non-Abelian gates in holonomic quantum computing.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Establishing fundamental geometric bounds (Quantum Geometric Limits) for non-Abelian Wilczek-Zee holonomies and finding optimal control protocols to implement target gates.

**Main result.** The authors derive universal bounds where the holonomy magnitude is limited by the surface integral of the non-Abelian curvature norm, and show that optimal protocols can minimize geometric cost by aligning curvature along a single Lie-algebra direction.

**Method.** A variational approach using an effective Stokes-Schrödinger dynamics and a non-Abelian brachistochrone ansatz, mapping the optimization to a particle subject to a non-Abelian Lorentz force.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on non-Abelian adiabatic transport within an N-dimensional degenerate subspace, specifically using a four-level 'tripod' atomic system with a two-dimensional dark subspace.

**Key observables.** Holonomy magnitude U(C), Hilbert-Schmidt and operator-norm curvature norms, and the efficiency ratio of surface area to curvature flux.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The adiabatic regime, the non-Abelian curvature F, and the Fubini-Study metric g.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The dynamics are assumed to be in the adiabatic regime involving degenerate eigenspaces.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows non-Abelian brachistochrone trajectories for target gates; other plots illustrate the cost of closed-loop holonomies versus angle and the local alignment of transported curvature.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of non-Abelian holonomy bounds, derives the Quantum Geometric Limit (QGL) using a Stokes-Schrödinger framework, formulates the optimization as a variational problem, and demonstrates near-optimal solutions using a tripod model.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Stokes' theorem turns Abelian Berry phases into curvature fluxes, whereas path ordering precludes such a simple formula for non-Abelian holonomies. We show that a quantitative form of this intuition survives: arbitrary Wilczek--Zee holonomies obey a universal quantum geometric limit~(QGL), in which the holonomy magnitude is bounded by a surface integral of the non-Abelian curvature norm. Recasting holonomic evolution as an effective Stokes--Schrödinger dynamics driven by transported curvature, we identify the QGL as the geometric counterpart of conventional quantum speed limits, with a time-integrated generator norm replaced by a surface-integrated curvature cost. The induced contour--surface variational problem is governed by a non-Abelian Lorentz force, which we address with a brachistochrone ansatz of curvature-weighted geodesics. Applied to an SU(2) tripod dark subspace, near-optimal protocols spontaneously align the transported curvature along a single Lie-algebra direction, effectively taming non-Abelianity.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28821"></a>
### [Realization of the Ruby Lattice Antiferromagnet in Layered Transition-Metal Fluorides](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28821v1)

**Authors:** Harald O. Jeschke, Daniel Guterding, Pratyay Ghosh  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28821v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28821_figures/2605.28821_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Ruby lattice and the ground state of the classical Heisenberg antiferromagnet. The ideal ruby lattice geometry constructed from a periodic tiling of trian- gles, squares, and hexagons. The unit cell is indicated by dashed lines. The lattice contains two symmetry-inequivalent nearest-neighbour bonds, one set forming triangular motifs and another forming hexagonal loops. The classical antiferro- magnetic Heisenberg model on this lattice has a ground state in which each hexagon develops local N´eel order, with a rela- tive 120◦spin canting between neighbouring hexagons.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

**Summary.** This paper identifies two layered transition-metal fluorides as experimental realizations of the elusive ruby lattice antiferromagnet. By combining DFT and Monte Carlo simulations, the authors show that these materials host incommensurate spin-spiral states driven by frustrated exchange interactions. The work provides quantitative predictions for future neutron-scattering experiments.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty in finding experimental material realizations of the ruby lattice antiferromagnet, which is expected to host exotic emergent phenomena and topological orders.

**Main result.** The study identifies CsBaFe3F12 and CsBaCr3F12 as excellent candidates for 2D ruby-lattice magnetism, demonstrating that both compounds realize an incommensurate spiral low-temperature state.

**Method.** The researchers used DFT+U energy mapping to extract Heisenberg exchange interactions, Classical Monte Carlo simulations to study thermodynamic behavior, and Luttinger-Tisza analysis to identify magnetic ordering vectors.

**Model / system.** The physical systems are the layered transition-metal fluorides CsBaFe3F12 (S=5/2) and CsBaCr3F12 (S=3/2) featuring a distorted ruby lattice geometry. The model is a Heisenberg antiferromagnet on a 12-site basis lattice.

**Key observables.** Magnetic specific heat, spin structure factor S(q), magnetic ordering temperature (Neel temperature), and magnetic order parameter.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Exchange interaction strengths (J1 through J7), Hubbard U, spin magnitude S, and the stiffness (kappa) of the energy landscape.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study uses classical Monte Carlo rather than quantum Monte Carlo and assumes the magnetic interactions can be accurately captured by a Heisenberg Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the ruby lattice geometry and ground state; Figure 2 displays crystal structures, exchange paths, and exchange interaction values as a function of U.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the ruby lattice problem, presents the structural and exchange characterization of the two fluorides via DFT, details the Monte Carlo and Luttinger-Tisza results for magnetic ordering, and concludes with an analytical framework explaining the difference in magnetic stiffness between the two compounds.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The antiferromagnet on the ruby lattice is expected to host a range of exotic emergent phenomena, yet its material realization has remained elusive. Here we show that the layered transition metal fluorides CsBaFe$_3$F$_{12}$ and CsBaCr$_3$F$_{12}$ with Fe$^{3+}$ and Cr$^{3+}$ ions realize only slightly distorted ruby lattice geometries with spin moments $S=5/2$ and $S=3/2$, respectively. Their microscopic Hamiltonians, calculated with DFT energy mapping, are dominated by short-ranged antiferromagnetic interactions within the ruby layers. Classical Monte Carlo simulations reveal strong frustration in both compounds, with local Néel correlations on the hexagonal plaquettes and distinct long-range ordering tendencies governed by weaker triangular links. For CsBaFe$_3$F$_{12}$, the calculated thermodynamic behaviour is consistent with the experimentally reported magnetic ordering scale. For CsBaCr$_3$F$_{12}$, classical Monte Carlo and Luttinger-Tisza analysis reveal competing low-energy ordering wave vectors, strong finite-size sensitivity, and a tendency toward incommensurate order. Overall, our results establish these fluorides as experimentally accessible ruby-lattice antiferromagnets and provide quantitative predictions for future neutron-scattering studies.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Thermodynamic properties of chemically disordered compounds via AI-driven estimation of partition function with the PULSE method](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28594v1)

**Summary.** This paper introduces an improved AI-driven method called PULSE to estimate the partition function and thermodynamic properties of chemically disordered materials. By using an inverse Variational Autoencoder, the method achieves high precision and efficiency, particularly in high-temperature regimes, while scaling effectively with system size.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Estimating the thermodynamic properties and partition functions of chemically disordered compounds is computationally expensive due to the massive configuration space required for traditional Monte Carlo convergence.

**Main result.** The improved PULSE method accurately and efficiently estimates partition functions and thermodynamic properties, outperforming Wolff and Wang-Landau algorithms in the high-temperature disordered regime and demonstrating excellent scalability.

**Method.** An improved version of the PULSE method using an 'inverse' Variational Autoencoder (VAE) architecture and unsupervised learning to optimize a distribution that minimizes Kullback-Leibler divergence.

**Model / system.** The method is validated using the 2D Ising model on a square lattice, serving as a proxy for complex chemically disordered materials like high-entropy alloys and MOX fuels.

**Key observables.** Partition function (Z), average energy, specific heat (Cv), magnetic susceptibility, and magnetization.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (T), inverse temperature (beta), system size (N), and number of samples (m).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The observable property must be strictly positive for the logarithmic formulation; high-energy configurations are assumed to be negligible at low temperatures.

**Figures summary.** The figures illustrate the PULSE architecture, compare PULSE estimates to exact solutions for the partition function and magnetization, show the reconstruction of thermodynamic properties, and demonstrate scalability and error comparisons against Wolff and Wang-Landau algorithms.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the scientific problem of chemical disorder, presents the mathematical framework of the improved PULSE method, validates the approach using the 2D Ising model, compares performance against traditional Monte Carlo methods, and discusses scalability and limitations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

In this article, we present an improved version of the PULSE method (Partition function Unsupervised Learning Sampling and Evaluation) for estimating the thermodynamic properties of chemically disordered compounds. The aim is to reduce the computational cost of Monte Carlo approaches for this type of material and to demonstrate that this generative tool can estimate thermodynamic properties by sampling and estimating the partition function of the system. To validate this innovative approach, we use the 2D Ising model as a benchmark. We demonstrate that our method accurately reproduces average properties with high precision and efficiency compared to traditional Monte Carlo sampling methods. Our results highlight the efficiency and adaptability of the PULSE method, making it a valuable tool for studying materials for which conventional methods are too inefficient to compute properties affected by chemical disorder at low cost.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces an efficient orbital-free density functional theory approach to simulate the complex, non-spherical structures known as nuclear pasta in neutron stars. By avoiding pre-defined geometric shapes, the method successfully discovers exotic new configurations and provides a computationally scalable way to study the neutron star crust.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The high computational cost of 3D microscopic calculations (Kohn-Sham DFT) makes it difficult to systematically study the complex, varying geometric structures of 'nuclear pasta' in neutron star inner crusts.

**Main result.** The authors developed a self-consistent orbital-free density functional theory (SC-ETF) that efficiently identifies both standard and exotic pasta structures (like 'nuclear waffles' or connected rods) without prior geometric assumptions.

**Method.** A self-consistent Extended Thomas-Fermi (SC-ETF) method using a second-order expansion of the Skyrme energy density functional, solved via a 3D gradient descent method on a Cartesian mesh.

**Model / system.** The system is the inner crust of a neutron star, consisting of a periodic lattice of protons and neutrons (nuclear pasta) immersed in a background gas of dripped neutrons and electrons.

**Key observables.** Nucleon number densities (neutron and proton), total energy per nucleon, proton fraction, and geometric configurations of the pasta phases.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Average nucleon number densities, proton fractions, simulation box sizes (up to 40 fm), and mesh spacing (delta x).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The method uses a second-order (hbar^2) expansion, omitting higher-order shell effects and pairing correlations; electrons are treated as a relativistic homogeneous gas.

**Figures summary.** Figures show visual examples of exotic pasta shapes (e.g., x-cylinders, slabs with holes), phase diagrams of structures vs. density, and stability analysis of the method as a function of mesh spacing.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational challenge of nuclear pasta, proposes the SC-ETF/OF-DFT method, demonstrates its ability to find non-empirical shapes, discusses the impact of box size, and concludes with a numerical stability analysis of various Skyrme functionals.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Background: In the bottom layer of the inner crust of neutron stars, various crystalline structures are expected to emerge that are collectively called ``nuclear pasta.'' It is desired to know properties of nuclear pasta in a wide variety of conditions for astrophysical applications. However, three-dimensional fully-microscopic calculations require huge computational effort that makes it still challenging to carry out systematic calculations.   Purpose: In this paper, we propose an efficient method to calculate various nuclear pasta configurations in a non-empirical manner, based on three-dimensional orbital-free density functional theory (OF-DFT). We demonstrate the feasibility of the proposed approach by applying it to densities throughout the inner crust of neutron stars.   Methods: As a first application of OF-DFT for nuclear pasta, we employ the second-order extended Thomas-Fermi (ETF) expansion of Skyrme energy density functional (EDF) to construct an EDF that depends only on neutron and proton number densities. Based on the variational principle, we derive Euler-Lagrange equations to determine optimal neutron and proton density distributions and solve them self-consistently. In this work, we call this approach the self-consistent ETF (SC-ETF) method.   Results: We perform three-dimensional SC-ETF calculations with various box sizes. We successfully obtain various pasta structures, depending on given average nucleon number densities, consistent with earlier studies. Moreover, we find other exotic structures, such as bending and/or connected rods, slabs with a hole, etc., underlining the advantage of the self-consistent formalism.   Conclusions: We demonstrate that the SC-ETF method proposed in this study, which can be regarded as a realization of OF-DFT, is a promising tool that can efficiently describe complex pasta structures without empirical assumptions on geometric shapes.

</details>

### [Topological lattice gauge theory enriched by non-invertible symmetry](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28688v1)

**Authors:** Lea E. Bottini, Clement Delcamp, Edmund Heng, Campbell K. McLauchlan, Dominic J. Williamson  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28688v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28688_figures/2605.28688_page2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 2</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28688_figures/2605.28688_page3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 3</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28688_figures/2605.28688_page4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 4</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28688_figures/2605.28688_page5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 5</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28688_figures/2605.28688_page6.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Low-resolution page preview, page 6</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper provides a rigorous mathematical foundation for topological phases enriched by non-invertible symmetries. It demonstrates how anyon condensation leads to a hypergroup-graded structure and shows that the resulting non-invertible symmetry can be captured using Hopf monads.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper aims to formalize and generalize the notion of Symmetry Enriched Topological (SET) order to cases where the enriching symmetry is non-invertible, specifically through anyon condensation.

**Main result.** The authors establish a mathematical framework using hypergroup-graded fusion categories and Hopf monads to describe the localized excitations and the non-invertible symmetry action in condensed topological lattice gauge theories.

**Method.** The study employs the tube algebra approach, the relative Drinfeld center construction, and the theory of Hopf monads to classify excitations and encode symmetry actions.

**Model / system.** The primary models are finite group topological lattice gauge theories (quantum double models) and generic string-net models, specifically examining the condensation of an arbitrary algebra of charges.

**Key observables.** Localized excitations (anyons), domain walls, and the categorical action of the non-invertible symmetry.

**Important parameters / regimes.** The group G (parent theory) and subgroup H (condensed theory), and the hypergroup grading of the resulting theory.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction assumes the condensation of pure charges and relies on the existence of a spherical fusion subcategory within a larger category.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the lattice gauge theory and anyon condensation mechanism, moves to the mathematical classification of excitations using tube algebras, introduces the Hopf monad framework for non-invertible symmetries, provides explicit calculations using the D3 and D6 groups, and concludes with a generalization to generic string-net models.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We use finite group topological lattice gauge theory, also known as the quantum double model, as a lens to explore a notion of topological order enriched by a non-invertible symmetry. For invertible symmetry enriched topological order, there is an established axiomatisation in terms of a G-crossed braided fusion category. We lay the foundations for a generalisation of this notion. By condensing an arbitrary algebra of charges in a quantum double model, we demonstrate that the category of localised excitations in the resulting theory forms a hypergroup-graded extension of the category of deconfined excitations. For every element in the hypergroup, the associated domain wall acts in a typically non-invertible way on these localised excitations. Both this action and the monoidal structure are compatible with the hypergroup grading. The actual categorical action is encoded in a Hopf monad on the category of localised excitations, and gauging the non-invertible symmetry amounts to computing the category of modules over this Hopf monad. Finally, we outline how this framework naturally extends to theories obtained by condensing algebras in a generic string-net model.

</details>

### [Variational Quantum Models for Knowledge Graph Embeddings on NISQ Devices](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28723v1)

**Authors:** Guido Bellomo, Martín Santesteban, Patricio Bruno, Santiago Cifuentes, Gustavo Martín Bosyk  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28723v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28723_figures/2605.28723_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 1: Variational quantum circuit architectures for knowledge graph embedding. (a) Circuit implementing the SWITCH test to estimate the score function. (b) Circuit implementing the standard SWAP test for score computation.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28723_figures/2605.28723_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 2: General hybrid quantum–classical pipeline for VQA- based knowledge graph embedding. (i) Classical inputs are encoded into a parameterized quantum circuit that computes a score; (ii) a loss function is evaluated classically using the predicted scores and target values; (iii) a classical optimizer updates the parameters of the quantum ansatz. Dashed lines indicate classical computation steps.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28723_figures/2605.28723_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3: Quantum circuit architecture for the proposed VQA model, using a compute–uncompute scheme to evaluate the score without ancillary qubits.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper proposes a new way to perform knowledge graph embeddings on near-term quantum computers. By using a 'compute-uncompute' method, the authors reduce the number of qubits and the complexity of measurements required. This makes the algorithm more compatible with current noisy quantum hardware.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Reducing the hardware demands, such as qubit overhead and circuit depth, for Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) used in Knowledge Graph Embedding on NISQ devices.

**Main result.** The introduction of the qCUERO framework and a new 'compute-uncompute' model that eliminates the need for ancillary qubits and entangled measurements while maintaining an interpretable score function.

**Method.** A hybrid quantum-classical pipeline using a compute-uncompute strategy where the score is estimated from the probability of the all-zero outcome in a computational basis measurement.

**Model / system.** A variational quantum model where entities are represented as quantum states in a Hilbert space of dimension 2^n and relations are represented as unitary operations.

**Key observables.** The score function (delta_hrt) representing the plausibility of a triple (head, relation, tail).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Trainable parameters for entities (theta_e) and relations (theta_r); Hilbert space dimension d = 2^n.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The study is primarily theoretical; the impact of quantum noise and performance on real-world knowledge graphs are yet to be fully validated.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 compares existing Switch and Swap test architectures; Fig 2 shows the general hybrid quantum-classical pipeline; Fig 3 illustrates the proposed compute-uncompute architecture.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces a unified framework (qCUERO), analyzes existing architectures (Switch and Swap tests), proposes a new compute-uncompute model, and discusses trade-offs regarding circuit depth and error robustness.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Variational Quantum Algorithms (VQAs) combine quantum circuits with classical optimization to tackle problems that may benefit from the capabilities of near-term quantum hardware. In knowledge graph embedding, recent proposals based on this approach follow a similar overall architecture but differ in the way they compute the score function and in the number of qubits they require. One design uses $n+1$ qubits and obtains the score through a switch test on an ancillary qubit, while another employs $2n+1$ qubits and applies a swap test between two registers. In both cases, entities and relations are represented in a Hilbert space of dimension $d = 2^n$, with comparable computational cost and the same mean squared error loss. This work introduces a unified framework that captures the two schemes and makes it possible to explore new variants. Within this setting, we propose an alternative that keeps the intuitive meaning of the score function while dispensing with ancillary qubits and entangled measurements. The result is a model better suited to current NISQ devices, reducing hardware demands without sacrificing interpretability.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.27608"></a>
### [Zero modes of non-abelian Dirac operator in topologically non-trivial band insulator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27608v1)

**Authors:** Neha Kumari, Sankalpa Ghosh  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27608v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27608_figures/2605.27608_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: (a) Dispersion plot for lattice Dirac model (b) Continuum dispersion of rhombohedral stacked (NL = 4 and NL = 5 layer of graphene, shown for displacement energy of |D| = 2.25|eV).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27608_figures/2605.27608_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: (a) Reciprocal lattice of the lattice Dirac model with reciprocal lattice vectors b1 = π(1 −i) and b2 = π √</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper establishes that zero modes of a non-abelian Dirac operator are a generic feature of topologically non-trivial band insulators. By linking the geometry of the Quantum Geometric Tensor to these zero modes, the authors provide a unified non-interacting foundation for understanding complex phases like Fractional Chern Insulators.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the existence and implications of zero modes of a non-abelian Dirac operator in the momentum space of topologically non-trivial band insulators.

**Main result.** The authors prove that the local gauge-invariance of the Quantum Geometric Tensor (QGT) implies the existence of zero modes for a non-abelian Dirac operator, providing a theoretical framework for understanding Fractional Chern Insulator (FCI) phases.

**Method.** The study utilizes non-abelian gauge theory, the quantum adiabatic approximation, and the minimization of a Euclidean action, with explicit solutions for the zero modes expressed via Jacobi Theta functions.

**Model / system.** The framework applies to generic N-level (sublattice/orbital) band insulators with degeneracy points in the Brillouin zone, specifically analyzing the lattice Dirac (QWZ) model and rhombohedral N-layer graphene.

**Key observables.** Quantum Geometric Tensor (QGT), non-abelian Berry connection (Wilczek-Zee connection), Berry curvature, and Fubini-Study metric.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Number of bands (N), displacement field energy (D), generalized Dirac velocity, and the presence of degeneracy points in the Brillouin zone.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is assumed to be in the quantum adiabatic limit, and the derivation relies on the local gauge-invariance of the QGT.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows dispersion plots for the lattice Dirac model and continuum dispersion for rhombohedral multilayer graphene; Figure 2 shows the reciprocal lattice of the lattice Dirac model.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by deriving the gauge-invariant QGT and zero-mode equations, moves to solving these equations using holomorphic representations and Jacobi Theta functions, applies the framework to specific models (QWZ and rhombohedral graphene), and concludes with a mathematical appendix on theta function properties.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We show that the local gauge-invariance of the quantum geometric tensor (QGT) defined in the Block-momentum space of a generic $N$-level (sublattice degrees of freedom) band insulator implies the existence of zero modes of non-abelian Dirac operator in such momentum space. Solutions of these zero modes equations in the two-dimensional Brillouin zone torus, in terms of Jacobi Theta function determine the probability amplitudes associated with the $N$-component ground state wave-function under adiabatic approximation in this Hilbert space. These solutions subjected to normalization, defines a complex projective ($CP$) space of $N-1$ dimension ($CP^{N-1}$ space) when one or more degeneracy points exist in the dispersion spectrum of such band-isulator. We show how the non-abelian generalization of the vortexability criterion of Chern bands automatically follows from these zero-mode equations, and also demonstrate their connection with momentum space-version of Lowest landau level algebra. Subsequently we write an Euclidean action from which these zero mode equations follow. We point out that the non-interacting part of different paradigms used to understand fractional Chern insulator(FCI) like phases in a host of two-dimensional material can be understood within this approach. We analyse two effective hamiltonian : lattice Dirac (QZW) model and two-band model for rhombohedral $N$-layer graphene in our propsoed framework and obtain important conclusions.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<a id="paper-2605.28249"></a>
### [Zero-Field Thermal Hall Effect in Insulator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28249v1)

First, a weak trained field +Btrain is applied with the sample temperature above its Néel temperature TN ≈ 27 K. The sample is then cooled down to Tbase. Finally, the field is removed, leaving Na2Co2TeO6 in an antiferromagnetic ground state with single domain (concerning magnetic...</sub>

<sub>Fig. 2 | Thermal Hall effect at zero magnetic field. a, Schematic illustration of the spin arrangement and heat flow. For a given training process, the nearly compensated out-of-plane magnetization m and the in-plane vortices v act together to the deflection of heat flow. b, Temperature dependence of -κxy at zero magnetic field. The solid line is a guide to the eyes. The error bars are obtained from the measurement noises, as shown the grey bar in Fig. 1c. Inset shows both κₓₓ and -κxy in units of W m-1 K-1. Remarkably, reversal point of -κxy coincides with the compensation point T* of m, while above TN there is no detectable -κxy, including the temperature Tp of maximum κₓₓ.</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28249_figures/2605.28249_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 3 | Thermal Hall effect under effective magnetic field Beff and external magnetic field Bext. a, κxy/T as a function of Beff at Bext = 0 T , where Beff ∝𝑚∙𝑣2 . Top-left inset: Comparison of Beff and m with κxy/T. Bottom-right inset: Illustration of heat flow deflected by Beff. b, κxy/T as a function of Bext at different temperatures above TN. The dashed lines are linear fittings. Top-right inset: κxx under Bext = 9 T, -κxy under Bext = 9 T, 6 T, and 3 T. For -κxy, two distinct regions are highlighted with blue and purple shades, which are separated by T = TN. At T &lt; TN, THEs induced by Beff and Bext coexist; while at T &gt; TN, only Bext acts on phonons. Bottom-left inset: Illustration of...</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper reports the discovery of a spontaneous thermal Hall effect in the antiferromagnetic insulator Na2Co2TeO6 at zero external magnetic field. The effect is driven by an intrinsic effective field arising from a unique non-coplanar spin texture. This finding challenges traditional views on heat conduction and provides a new way to explore topological responses in quantum materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the origin of a spontaneous thermal Hall effect (STHE) in an antiferromagnetic insulator in the absence of an external magnetic field.

**Main result.** The researchers discovered a large zero-field thermal Hall effect in Na2Co2TeO6, which is attributed to an intrinsic effective magnetic field generated by a non-coplanar spin texture (vortices and magnetization) rather than a net magnetic moment.

**Method.** Thermal Hall conductivity measurements were performed on single-crystal Na2Co2TeO6 using a cryostat, applying a longitudinal heat flow and detecting transverse temperature gradients with high-resolution thermocouples.

**Model / system.** The physical system is the honeycomb oxide Na2Co2TeO6, an antiferromagnetic insulator characterized by in-plane vortices and nearly compensated out-of-plane magnetic moments.

**Key observables.** Transverse thermal conductivity (kappa_xy), longitudinal thermal conductivity (kappa_xx), thermal Hall angle (kappa_xy/kappa_xx), and magnetization (m).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Neeel temperature (TN approx 27 K), compensation temperature (T*), and effective magnetic field (B_eff).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The sample is assumed to be in a single antiferromagnetic domain achieved through magnetic field training.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the experimental setup, sample training procedure, and raw temperature data; Fig 2 illustrates the spin arrangement and the temperature dependence of the zero-field thermal Hall signal; Fig 3 compares the thermal Hall response under effective and external magnetic fields.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the phenomenon of the thermal Hall effect, describes the experimental setup and sample preparation, presents the discovery of the zero-field effect and its temperature dependence, proposes a mechanism based on an effective field from spin textures, and compares the results to external field-induced effects.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Fourier's law dictates that heat flow is usually parallel to the applied temperature gradient. However, under a high magnetic field, heat flow carried by both electrons in conductors and phonons in insulators can be deflected, a phenomenon known as thermal Hall effect. Intriguingly, we observe at zero field a spontaneous thermal Hall effect in an antiferromagnetic insulator. Despite a vanishingly small uncompensated magnetization, the magnitude of this effect is surprisingly large, comparable to typical responses induced by several teslas of external field. This zero-field behavior indicates that charge-neutral heat carriers can be governed by an intrinsic effective field arising from the unique spin arrangement. Our discovery challenges the centuries-old preconception of heat conduction and open up new avenues for exploring non-trivial topological responses in quantum materials.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28425"></a>
### [Symmetry-Selective Topological Magnon Engineering by Phonon Angular Momentum](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28425v1)

**Authors:** Markus Weißenhofer, Philipp Rieger, Chandan K. Singh, M. S. Mrudul, Sergiy Mankovsky, Peter M. Oppeneer  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28425v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** 🔥 `non-equilibrium dynamics` **4/5** · `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28425_figures/2605.28425_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 1. (a) Top view of the atomic lattice of monolayer CrI3. J1 to J3 denote the nearest- to third-nearest-neighbor exchange interactions between magnetic Cr atoms. (b) and (c) Schematic representations of one representative eigenvec- tor from each pair of analyzed degenerate phonon modes with energies and group representations as labeled.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28425_figures/2605.28425_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 2. Engineering of magnon bandstructure of CrI3. Ar- rows indicate the opening of a gap at the Dirac points K and K′ upon driving by clockwise circular Eg phonon mode and the color coding shows the Berry curvature of the magnon bands. The inset shows the magnon energy gap versus Cr displacement.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28425_figures/2605.28425_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 3. PAM-selective control of magnon gap and topologies in the absence of SOC. (a) PAM and (b) magnon gap at K, K′</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28425_figures/2605.28425_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4. Engineering of magnon bandstructure of CrI3 in the presence of SOC, assumed to manifest in DMI. (a) and (b) show the magnon energies around K driven by clockwise (CW) and counter-clockwise (CCW) Eg phonons of different amplitude. Arrows indicate gap widening (a) and closing (b) and the color coding the Berry curvature. (c) and (d) depict the magnon energy gap and the anomalous thermal Hall con- ductivity versus Cr displacement.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper shows that driving lattice vibrations with specific angular momentum can selectively control the topology of magnon bands in 2D magnets. By using circularly polarized phonons, one can open, tune, and even reverse the topological phases of magnons. This provides a versatile platform for the Floquet engineering of bosonic excitations in van der Waals materials.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work demonstrates a method for Floquet engineering of topological excitations using driven lattice dynamics, which is highly relevant to the study of non-equilibrium many-body dynamics and the control of Berry curvature.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of achieving dynamical control over Berry curvature and magnon band structures to engineer and reverse topological phases.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that circularly and elliptically polarized phonons carrying phonon angular momentum (PAM) can selectively induce chiral interactions, opening and tuning topological magnon gaps and reversing Chern numbers.

**Method.** The study uses an ab initio framework (SPRKKR, VASP, Phonopy) combined with Floquet theory and a van Vleck expansion to derive an effective time-independent Hamiltonian.

**Model / system.** The primary system is monolayer CrI3, a 2D van der Waals ferromagnet, modeled using an atomistic Heisenberg Hamiltonian with spin-lattice coupling and Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions.

**Key observables.** Magnon band gaps, Chern numbers, and anomalous thermal Hall conductivity.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Phonon angular momentum (PAM), phonon polarization phase, and the high-frequency phonon regime where phonon frequency exceeds magnon frequencies.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The reliability of the effective Hamiltonian relies on the vanishing of higher-order corrections in the van Vleck expansion and the high-frequency approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the CrI3 lattice and phonon mode representations; Figure 2 illustrates the modification of the magnon spectrum and Berry curvature by Eg phonons; Figure 3 relates phonon polarization to the magnon gap and Chern numbers; Figure 4 shows magnon bandstructure engineering.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of dynamical topological control, presents an ab initio-based Floquet framework, analyzes the symmetry-selective role of phonon angular momentum, demonstrates the results on CrI3, and discusses experimental signatures like thermal Hall conductivity.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dynamical control of Berry curvature remains an outstanding challenge in the engineering of topological phases. Here, we demonstrate control of magnon band structures via coherently driven phonons, based on \textit{ab initio} spin-lattice coupling and Floquet theory. We show that this control is symmetry selective: linearly polarized phonons leave the spectrum unchanged, whereas circular and elliptical phonons carrying finite phonon angular momentum (PAM) induce chiral interactions that open and tune gaps at Dirac points, generating and reversing topological magnon phases. The gap magnitude and Chern numbers are directly governed by the PAM, enabling handedness-selective topology control. Applied to monolayer CrI$_3$, and supported by symmetry analysis, our results establish driven lattice dynamics as a general route to engineering topological bosonic excitations and a versatile platform for Floquet control of magnetism.

</details>

### [Electrically driven Rabi dynamics of magnetic-field-induced corner states in a two-dimensional topological insulator](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28499v1)

**Authors:** D. V. Khomitsky, E. A. Lavrukhina, D. P. Krasavin, S. S. Krishtopenko, F. Teppe  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** quantum information and computing · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28499v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **3/5**

<sub>FIG. 4. (a) Probability-density distributions |Ψ|2 for the lev- els E1,2 marked in Fig. 3. Each panel shows transitions from the lower discrete level E1 and from the upper discrete level E2.</sub>

**Summary.** This paper explores how to use electric fields to drive coherent Rabi oscillations between localized corner states in a 2D topological insulator. It identifies an optimal regime where high-frequency manipulation is possible while keeping population loss to the edge-state continuum extremely low. This provides a potential pathway for creating lithographically defined, electrically controllable qubits.

**Why it may be interesting.** It presents a concrete realization of an electrically driven two-level system where the competition between coherent driving and leakage into a continuum can be precisely controlled, relevant to open quantum systems.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the feasibility of using magnetic-field-induced corner states in a 2D topological insulator as a controllable two-level subsystem (qubit) driven by electric fields.

**Main result.** The authors demonstrate that GHz-scale Rabi oscillations can be achieved with leakage into the continuum suppressed below 1% by optimizing the electric field amplitude.

**Method.** The study uses an effective edge-state model to solve the time-dependent Schrödinger equation, explicitly accounting for both discrete localized levels and the continuum of edge states.

**Model / system.** A 1D edge of a HgTe/CdHgTe quantum well with a double-kink geometry, subject to an in-plane magnetic field that induces a gap and localized states.

**Key observables.** Rabi frequencies, population of discrete levels, leakage probability into the continuum, and oscillations in the mean coordinate and spin projection.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Rabi frequencies of 20-40 GHz, electric field amplitudes of 0.05-0.1 ueV/nm, and Zeeman energy scales.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The edge orientation is piecewise constant, the magnetic field is strictly in-plane, and the outer edge segments are sufficiently long.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the physical geometry of the kinked edge; Figures 6 and 7 illustrate the time-dependent populations, leakage, and spin/spatial oscillations under different electric field strengths.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical system and Hamiltonian, derives the localized states and their dipole matrix elements, and then performs time-dependent simulations to analyze the trade-off between driving strength and population leakage.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We study coherent electric manipulation of magnetic-field-induced localized states at a double kink of a helical edge in a HgTe/CdHgTe quantum well. An in-plane magnetic field opens a gap in the one-dimensional edge spectrum, while changes in the edge orientation generate localized in-gap states at the kinks. We show that, for suitable geometry and magnetic-field direction, two such states form an effective lithographically defined two-level subsystem. Using an edge-state model that includes both the localized levels and the continuum states outside the magnetic-field-induced gap, we calculate the electric-dipole matrix elements and solve the time-dependent problem under resonant driving. The resulting dynamics exhibits Rabi oscillations with linear frequencies of $20$--$40$~GHz for realistic parameters. We find that the continuum states provide a leakage channel whose strength is strongly controlled by the driving amplitude: reducing the electric field suppresses leakage below the percent level while preserving GHz-scale coherent oscillations. These results establish a route from magnetic-field-induced corner-state physics to electrically driven two-level dynamics in a realistic two-dimensional topological-insulator edge geometry.

</details>

### [Full Quantum and Mixed Quantum--Classical Dynamics of Hot Exciton Cooling in Semiconductor Nanocrystals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27708v1)

<sub>Fig. 4 panel (b) compares the dynamics generated by this short-time expression with the normalized popula- tion ˜Pn(t) = Pn(t)−Pn,eq Pn(0)−Pn,eq and shows that it captures the fast decay observed in both the QME and MASH dynamics quite well. Analyzing Eq. (26) suggests that</sub>

**Summary.** This study benchmarks various quantum and mixed quantum-classical methods against exact dynamics to study hot-exciton cooling in nanocrystals. It reveals that the initial ultrafast cooling is caused by rapid state mixing from low-frequency phonons rather than traditional energy relaxation. The results identify MASH as a highly reliable approximation for these complex vibronic systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** The paper provides a rigorous benchmark for non-adiabatic dynamics in the intermediate-coupling regime, specifically addressing how thermal fluctuations of couplings drive rapid dephasing/mixing, which is highly relevant to open quantum systems and many-body dynamics.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Assessing the accuracy of various perturbative and mixed quantum-classical dynamical methods for describing hot-exciton relaxation and cooling in semiconductor nanocrystals.

**Main result.** The MASH (Mapping Approach to Surface Hopping) method provides the most consistent agreement with exact quantum benchmarks, while the ultrafast cooling component is identified as rapid diabatic state mixing driven by low-frequency phonons.

**Method.** Benchmarking various approximations (QME, Ehrenfest, Spin-LSC, FSSH, MASH) against numerically exact ML-MCTDH dynamics and PIMC equilibrium populations.

**Model / system.** Atomistically parameterized models of CdSe core and CdSe/CdS core-shell nanocrystals using a linear vibronic coupling Hamiltonian and semi-empirical pseudopotentials.

**Key observables.** Time-dependent average exciton energy, diabatic and adiabatic populations, and exciton-phonon spectral densities.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (300 K), nanocrystal size (3 nm), and the separation of timescales between ultrafast (~10 fs) and slow (~100 fs) relaxation.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Linear vibronic coupling (first-order expansion), static screening approximation for the Bethe-Salpeter equation, and use of the harmonic approximation for the nuclear Hamiltonian.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Exciton energies and spectral densities for CdSe and CdSe/CdS. Fig 2: Real-time diabatic population dynamics comparison. Fig 3: State-resolved diabatic equilibrium populations. Fig 5: Exciton energy relaxation over time.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the physical problem of exciton cooling, defines the atomistic models and Hamiltonian, details the benchmark and approximate dynamical methods, presents comparative results for population dynamics and energy relaxation, and concludes with mechanistic insights.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Hot-exciton relaxation in semiconductor nanocrystals (NCs) is often described using perturbative theories, but their accuracy is difficult to assess for realistic exciton--phonon Hamiltonians. Here, we benchmark the perturbative quantum master equation (QME) and several mixed quantum--classical (MQC) methods against fully quantum mechanical dynamics. Using atomistically parameterized models for CdSe core and CdSe/CdS core--shell NCs, we find that bare CdSe exhibits an ultrafast initial decay followed by slower cooling, whereas the core--shell system is dominated by the slower component. Analysis of reduced models shows that the ultrafast component arises from rapid diabatic state mixing driven by thermal fluctuations of low-frequency phonons, rather than from nuclear-assisted energy relaxation. The QME captures the initial fast decay but can fail for the slower relaxation in the diabatic representation, while the mapping approach to surface hopping (MASH) gives the most consistent agreement with both benchmark dynamics and equilibrium populations. These results establish a benchmark for exciton-cooling dynamics in NCs and clarify the physical regimes in which widely used approximate methods are reliable.

</details>

### [Substrate-driven topological engineering in plasmonic Su-Schrieffer-Heeger chains](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28463v1)

**Authors:** Florian Herz, Alireza Naeimi, Svend-Age Biehs  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28463v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `correlated / nonlocal dissipation` **2/5** · `driven-dissipative phase transition` **2/5** · `exotic spin models in light-matter` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28463_figures/2605.28463_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1: Sketch of the considered system. A bipartite SSH chain of nanoparticles of radius R with lattice constant d in each sublattice A and B and separation distance t within each unit cell is placed at a distance z in close vicinity to a semi- infinite planar substrate.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.28463_figures/2605.28463_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2: Eigenfrequencies and IPR values for IP (a),(b) and OP modes (c),(d) for nsub = 1.34 × 1019 cm−3 (a),(c) and nsub = 1.36 × 1019 cm−3 (b),(d) at z = 500 for different β and a total of N = 80 particles.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28463_figures/2605.28463_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3: IP band structure for z = 500 nm and nsub = 1.36 × 1019 cm−3 for different β values. For each band, the Zak phases are given in the corresponding colors. The dashed lines correspond to the light lines in vacuum (black) and the surface polariton light line, i.e. the surface mode dispersion relation, of the InSb substrate (green) taken from Eq. (10).</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28463_figures/2605.28463_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4: Polarizations of the unit cell |pA|2 + |pB|2 in x (solid) and z (dashed) direction for each band in Fig. 3 at β = 0.77.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28463_figures/2605.28463_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 6: Polarizations of the unit cell |pA|2 + |pB|2 in x (solid) and z (dashed) direction for each band in Fig. 3 at β = 0.91.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** The paper demonstrates that the topological properties of a plasmonic SSH chain can be controlled by its interaction with a substrate. By tuning the distance to the substrate or the substrate's doping, one can induce topological phase transitions and create protected edge modes. These findings also show that such topological features can be used to modulate spectral radiative heat transfer.

**Why it may be interesting.** This work is highly relevant to quantum optics and open quantum systems as it demonstrates how controlling the external electromagnetic environment (the substrate) can be used to manipulate topological edge states and near-field energy transport.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating how the electromagnetic environment of a substrate can be used to engineer the topological band structure and induce topological phase transitions in a plasmonic Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) chain.

**Main result.** The substrate interaction introduces two mechanisms—long-range band hybridization and short-range band touching—that can trigger topological phase transitions and enable the emergence of protected edge modes even in previously trivial regimes.

**Method.** The study uses a Green's function approach within the dipole approximation and the quasistatic limit, applying Bloch's theorem and calculating the Zak phase and Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR).

**Model / system.** A bipartite array of N identical InSb nanoparticles arranged in an SSH chain, placed at a distance z from a semi-infinite InSb planar substrate.

**Key observables.** Band structure, Zak phase, Inverse Participation Ratio (IPR), polarization (in-plane vs. out-of-plane), and spectral radiative heat flux.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Dimerization parameter (beta), chain-substrate distance (z), substrate carrier density (n_sub), and disorder strength (rho).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is modeled using the dipole approximation in the quasistatic limit, and higher-order multipole moments are neglected.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: System schematic; Fig 2: Eigenfrequencies and IPR; Fig 3: Infinite chain band structure and SPP interaction; Fig 4: Unit cell polarization; Fig 5: Nearest-neighbor approximation comparison; Fig 8: Eigenfrequencies/IPR vs distance; Fig 9: IP band structure; Fig 10-11: Band structure under disorder; Fig 12-14: Spectral radiative heat flux and robustness analysis.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the plasmonic SSH system, details the mathematical framework for substrate-induced interactions, analyzes the two distinct topological engineering mechanisms (hybridization and band touching), evaluates the robustness of edge modes against structural disorder, and concludes with the implications for near-field radiative heat transfer.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate the possibility of engineering the topological band structure of a plasmonic Su-Schrieffer-Heeger (SSH) chain through the interaction with its electromagnetic environment. We find that the long-range interaction of the in-plane modes of the SSH chain with the surface plasmon polaritons of a planar substrate introduces a band hybridization connected to a change of the Zak phase. On the other hand, the short-range interaction with the substrate introduces a band touching, again with a change in the Zak phase. Surprisingly, this second mechanism enables the emergence of topologically protected edge modes for parameters which correspond to the topologically trivial phase for an isolated plasmonic SSH chain. We study these mechanisms by changing the chain-substrate distance and the dimerization parameter. Finally, we discuss the robustness against disorder and, as one example, the impact of the observed effects on the near-field radiative heat transfer along the chain. Our findings pave the way to the engineering of edge modes in plasmonic topological configurations via the coupling to a plasmonic environment.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.28383"></a>
### [Unity-order coupling between free electrons and multiphoton waveguided Fock states](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28383v1)

**Authors:** L. Prelat, S. Abdullah, C. I. Velasco, F. J. García de Abajo

**Summary.** This paper proposes using electrostatic fields to steer electron beams into grazing trajectories near silicon waveguides. This technique allows for tunable control over the interaction, significantly increasing the number of photons generated per electron while suppressing unwanted energy losses.

**Why it may be interesting.** The work demonstrates a method for generating tunable, high-yield multiphoton Fock states and entangled electron-photon states, which is highly relevant for quantum nanophotonics and quantum light-matter interfaces.

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The coupling between free electron beams and waveguided optical modes is typically limited by short interaction times and a lack of spectral selectivity due to fixed straight-line trajectories.

**Main result.** Electrostatic steering of electron trajectories creates a tunable turning point that enhances coupling strength, enables modal selectivity, and can produce high photon yields exceeding ten photons per electron.

**Method.** The authors use the Boundary Element Method (BEM) to calculate electrostatic potentials and the local-response approximation to determine momentum-resolved Electron Energy-Loss Spectroscopy (EELS) probabilities.

**Model / system.** A system consisting of a 100-200 keV electron beam interacting with a rectangular silicon waveguide, utilizing either vertical or lateral electrostatic gating to deflect the electron trajectory.

**Key observables.** EELS probability (energy-loss probability), photon yield per electron, and modal selectivity (TE vs. TM modes).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electron kinetic energy (100-200 keV), impact parameter (b), electron incidence angle, and applied electrostatic bias voltage.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The silicon waveguide is modeled as a Perfect Electric Conductor (PEC) for DC potential calculations, and the electron velocity is assumed to be nearly constant during the interaction.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the electron-waveguide geometry and EELS probability for different impact parameters; Figure 3 illustrates the lateral gating configuration and photon yield vs. incidence angle; Figures 5-7 detail the EELS spectrum, decay of probability with distance, and the electrostatic potential distributions for various gating geometries.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of limited electron-waveguide coupling, proposes electrostatic steering as a solution, details the theoretical framework and numerical methods (BEM/EELS), compares different gating configurations (planar vs. lateral), and quantifies the resulting photon yield and tunability.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Electron beams enable highly localized near-field excitation of waveguided optical modes, yet their coupling is typically limited by short interaction times along straight-line trajectories with fixed impact parameters. Here, we theoretically demonstrate that electrostatic steering overcomes this limitation by introducing a tunable turning point in grazing electron trajectories, thus controlling the minimum electron--waveguide separation and producing strong coupling to waveguided modes. Specifically, we consider a biased rectangular silicon waveguide, where a repulsive static field deflects a grazing electron. In this configuration, the electron turning point governs both the coupling strength and the modal selectivity, which can be dynamically tuned through the electron incidence angle and the applied bias. In addition, the aloof electron--waveguide interaction suppresses lossy high-energy channels (e.g., above the silicon band gap) while preserving substantial excitation of the targeted waveguided modes. Using a practical biasing configuration and 100~keV electrons, we predict an average yield exceeding ten photons per electron, with voltage-tunable control of the interaction. Our results establish electrostatic steering as a practical route for engineering and enhancing free-electron coupling to waveguided photonic modes.

</details>

### [A supersymmetric study of charge and spin transport in Weyl semimetals under axionic electrodynamic response](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27657v1)

**Authors:** J. C. Pérez-Pedraza, A. Martín-Ruiz, L. F. Urrutia  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27657v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `non-equilibrium dynamics` **2/5**

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<sub>FIG. 1. System under study consisting of a semi-infinite slab of a Weyl semimetal of thickness 2L in z-direction immersed in an external magnetic field in z-direction. The axion magnetoelectric response in the material induces a non-homogeneous electric field in the same direction. We consider the interior problem only, i.e. in the region −L &lt; z &lt; +L.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27657_figures/2605.27657_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

</details>

<img src="2605.27657_figures/2605.27657_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. The dimensionless energy eigenvalues εn,m, for m = −2, −1, 0, 1, 2 (in ascendant order) as a function of the quantum number n. a) Complete spectrum (even and odd n). </details>

**Summary.** This paper explores how the interplay between axionic electrodynamics and supersymmetry governs transport in Weyl semimetals. By using supersymmetric quantum mechanics, the authors solve the Dirac equation to reveal how magnetic and electric fields induce chiral imbalances and specific spin currents. The results provide a theoretical framework for understanding the effective field theory of Weyl semimetals under external magnetic influence.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating the charge and spin transport properties of Weyl semimetals under an external magnetic field, specifically focusing on the effects of axionic electrodynamic response.

**Main result.** The study demonstrates that the system's dynamics separate into a magnetic sector with supersymmetric factorization and an electric sector with PT-supersymmetric structure, leading to a chiral imbalance and specific spin-current patterns.

**Method.** The authors use a low-energy effective Dirac-Weyl theory, applying chiral transformations, supersymmetric quantum mechanics (SUSY QM), and solving the resulting equations using tri-confluent Heun functions and numerical methods.

**Model / system.** A Weyl semimetal (WSM) modeled as a plane slab of thickness 2L in a uniform external magnetic field, characterized by axion electrodynamics where the axial term is replaced by an effective electromagnetic response.

**Key observables.** Chiral projections of probability and current densities, spin density, spin currents, and axial probability density.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fermi velocity (vF), axial 4-vector (b), magnetic field (B0), slab thickness (L), and the axion-like field theta(x).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Low-energy approximation near Weyl nodes, neglect of lattice regularization and band curvature, and a one-particle domain where the axial anomaly is absent.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows a schematic of the WSM slab; Figures 5-6 show energy eigenvalues as a function of quantum numbers and magnetic field; Figures 7-10 display probability and current densities for different chiralities.

**Paper structure.** The paper begins by defining the WSM Hamiltonian and applying a chiral transformation to derive axion electrodynamics, then proceeds to decouple the Dirac equation into magnetic and electric sectors, solves these sectors using SUSY and PT-SUSY techniques (including Heun functions), and concludes with the calculation of transport observables and conservation laws.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate the charge and spin transport properties of a Weyl semimetal under an external magnetic field using a low-energy effective theory. By performing a chiral transformation, we remove the axial term from the fermionic Lagrangian, while its physical effects are retained through the system's electromagnetic response. This response, derived from integrating out the fermionic degrees of freedom, takes the form of axionic electrodynamics and enters the Dirac equation via minimal coupling. The resulting dynamics separate naturally into magnetic and electric components: the magnetic sector admits a supersymmetric factorization, while the electric part exhibits a PT-supersymmetric structure. Robin boundary conditions are applied to the spinor, setting the energy spectrum and defining exact spinor solutions. We obtain the chiral projections of probability and current densities, exhibiting a chiral imbalance in the material. We show that in the x-direction, only one chirality contributes to the chiral current ($j^x_l = 0$). Spin density and currents where also computed. Our results demonstrate how the interplay between axionic response and supersymmetry governs transport phenomena in our specific setup, offering novel insights into the effective field theory description of Weyl semimetals.

</details>

### [Magneto-Optical Detection of Anisotropic Spin Currents in Altermagnetic RuO2](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27830v1)

**Authors:** Joongwon Lee, Jeonglyul Kim, Sreejith Nair, Seung Gyo Jeong, Changi Kim, Jae-Pil So, Bohm-Jung Yang, Bharat Jalan, Hyobin Yoo, Farhan Rana, Taekoo Oh, Hong-Gyu Park  
**Type:** both · **Category:** strongly correlated electrons · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27830v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks
**Topic relevance:** `spintronics-quantum-optics interface` **2/5**

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27830_figures/2605.27830_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>

<img src="2605.27830_figures/2605.27830_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27830_figures/2605.27830_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. STEM imaging of thin-film strain. a, Virtual annular dark-field (VADF) image generated</sub>

**Summary.** This paper provides experimental evidence for the altermagnetic spin-splitter effect in RuO2 thin films using magneto-optical probes. By demonstrating directionally anisotropic spin accumulation, the study establishes RuO2 as a promising material for field-free spintronic devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to determine if metallic RuO2 sustains robust altermagnetic order in films thicker than the ultrathin limit and to distinguish the intrinsic altermagnetic spin-splitter effect (ASSE) from extrinsic mechanisms like the Spin Hall Effect or Rashba-type splitting.

**Main result.** The researchers experimentally demonstrate directionally anisotropic spin accumulation in 12-nm RuO2 films, confirming the presence of the altermagnetic spin-splitter effect and establishing RuO2 as an efficient, symmetry-controlled spin source.

**Method.** The study combines polarization-resolved Second-Harmonic Generation (SHG) and high-sensitivity polar Magneto-Optical Kerr Effect (MOKE) with 4D-STEM structural analysis and Kubo-formalism-based electronic structure calculations.

**Model / system.** The physical system is a 12-nm-thick (1-01)-oriented RuO2 thin film grown on r-plane sapphire via molecular beam epitaxy, characterized by altermagnetic spin-split electronic bands and Néel order.

**Key observables.** SHG intensity and asymmetry, MOKE rotation (Kerr rotation), spin Hall angle, and local lattice strain.

**Important parameters / regimes.** 12-nm film thickness, Néel temperature (T_N ~ 500 K), current densities up to 6 x 10^6 A/cm^2, and MOKE sensitivity of ~10 nrad.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis assumes that the observed optical signals are intrinsic to the altermagnetic spin accumulation and not artifacts of Joule heating or thermal gradients.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the crystallographic/magnetic structure, spin-split Fermi surfaces, and optical probing geometry; Figure 3 involves 4D-STEM analysis of structural clusters and strain mapping.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the altermagnetic properties of RuO2, details the experimental setup for optical and structural characterization, presents the symmetry-driven anisotropic spin response, discusses the role of strain and structural heterogeneity, and concludes with the implications for spintronics.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Altermagnets are a recently identified class of collinear antiferromagnets that host large spin-split electronic bands, offering a promising platform for efficient spin-current generation. Among proposed candidates, the metallic oxide RuO2 is predicted to exhibit strong altermagnetic spin splitting; however, whether it sustains robust magnetic order beyond the ultrathin thickness limit remains unresolved. Here, we employ optical probes to investigate charge-to-spin conversion in a 12-nm-thick (101)-oriented RuO2 film grown on sapphire. Polarization-resolved second-harmonic generation reveals nonlinear optical responses consistent with the surface symmetry and Néel order of RuO2. Under an applied current, both second-harmonic generation and polar magneto-optical Kerr effect measurements detect a pronounced, directionally anisotropic spin polarization, exhibiting enhanced signals for current along [010] and strongly suppressed responses for current along [-101], in agreement with the symmetry of the altermagnetic spin-splitter effect. Non-magnetic or Rashba-type mechanisms cannot explain this symmetry-selective response. Scanning transmission electron microscopy further reveals that substantial strain persists even in relatively thick films, providing a possible explanation for the observed behavior. Therefore, these results establish RuO2 as an efficient spin source and demonstrate the potential of altermagnets for field-free spintronic devices.

</details>

### [Quantum anomalous Hall effect in chiral semimetals](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27826v1)

<sub>FIG. 5. (a) the Hall conductivity σT xy vs. the dephasing strength Γd, with four different L0 and EF = 0.1. (b) σT xy vs. Γd, with three different Lx and EF = 0.1. **Summary.** This paper demonstrates that the Quantum Anomalous Hall effect is not restricted to insulators and can exist in chiral semimetals. It shows that while the Hall response remains quantized, the longitudinal conductivity stays finite, creating a unique 'dual-channel' transport regime. This discovery extends the fundamental understanding of topological transport in gapless systems.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Investigating whether the Quantum Anomalous Hall (QAH) effect can persist in chiral semimetals where conduction and valence bands touch at zero energy, and identifying its unique transport signatures.

**Main result.** The Hall conductivity remains quantized even in the semimetallic regime due to the cancellation of Berry curvature at specific valleys, while the longitudinal conductivity remains finite and exhibits semimetallic behavior.

**Method.** Nonequilibrium Green's function (NEGF) calculations using a six-terminal Hall bar geometry and Büttiker virtual leads to simulate dephasing.

**Model / system.** A 2D chiral semimetal described by a two-band tight-binding model on a square lattice, featuring massive Dirac fermions at Gamma and M points and gapless cones at X and Y points.

**Key observables.** Hall conductivity (sigma_xy), longitudinal conductivity (sigma_xx), Hall resistivity (rho_xy), and equilibrium current density (Jy).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Fermi energy (Ef), dephasing strength (Gamma_d), mass term (m), and device dimensions (Lx, Ly).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is modeled using a clean tight-binding Hamiltonian with dephasing introduced via virtual leads to simulate realistic experimental conditions.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the 2D band structure and nanoribbon spatial distribution; Fig 2 displays the Hall bar setup and transport results (sigma_xx, sigma_xy vs Ef); Fig 4 shows the Hall response and current decay; Fig 5 analyzes the dependence of conductivities on dephasing, device size, and probe distance.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of QAH in semimetals, presents the tight-binding model and band structure, details the NEGF transport simulation setup, analyzes the quantization and semimetallic signatures, explores the semimetal-to-insulator transition, and discusses the role of dephasing and device geometry.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

The quantum anomalous Hall (QAH) effect is conventionally understood to exist only in Chern insulators, while a recent study has shown that ferromagnetic metals can also host the QAH effect. Between insulators and metals, we demonstrate that QAH can persist even in a chiral semimetal, where conduction and valence bands touch at zero energy. Transport calculations demonstrate that the Hall conductivity of such a system can be quantized in the presence of dephasing. Interestingly, its longitudinal conductivity remains finite and exhibits semimetallic behavior, in contrast to Chern insulators. This unusual transport behavior originates from the quantization of the Berry curvature integral over occupied states and the semimetallic band structure. This chiral semimetal can transition into a Chern insulator, accompanied by the vanishing of longitudinal conductivity and a reduction of the intrinsic length scale of the Hall response. Our results extend the concept of QAH and uncover the semimetallic QAH transport signatures.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.28250_figures/2605.28250_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 4: Transport across an atomic chain junction in perpendicular magnetic field. (a) Sketch of the system with the orientation of ⃗B and the vector potential ⃗A(y). Hopping integrals along x are modified by the Peierls phase, opposite on chains α and β. (b) The electronic bands are shifted by the Peierls phase, +φ on chain α and −φ on chain β. In the junction the shifted bands cross only at k = 0, π, and only in the neighborhood of these points the electronic states of the two chains can hybridize. These energy ranges are also those where transport is facilitated by gateway states, and with increasing ⃗B they shift towards E = 0. (c) Numerically calculated T(E, φ) for a junction with 49...</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.28250_figures/2605.28250_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Figure 5: Electron transport through simple junctions of single-walled CNTs (type A systems) under an external perpendicular magnetic field. The junction schematics above the panels show some of the possible electron paths (red arrows). (a–d) Oscillations of the computed zero-bias transmission as a function of electron energy E for (9,9)+(9,9) SWCNT junctions with overlap lengths of (a) 49.19 nm (200 unit cells) and (b) 196.76 nm (800 unit cells), and for (12,6)+(12,6) SWCNT junctions with overlap lengths of (c) 45.08 nm (40 unit cells) and (d) 180.33 nm (160 unit cells), shown for magnetic fields B up to 200 T in the energy window [-0.6,0] eV.
