</details>

**Summary.** This paper investigates how quantum interference at the junctions between carbon nanotubes affects the overall electrical conductivity of nanotube networks. By combining atomistic simulations with high-field experiments, the authors show that complex multi-walled structures can suffer from interference-induced conductance suppression, providing a blueprint for designing more stable and conductive CNT-based electronics.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Understanding and controlling quantum interference at inter-nanotube junctions to achieve high electrical conductance and field stability in carbon nanotube (CNT) assemblies.

**Main result.** The study identifies that multi-walled CNT architectures introduce complex, field-sensitive interference that reduces conductance, whereas SWCNT junctions can be tuned via overlap length and magnetic fields to achieve high-transmission 'gateway' states.

**Method.** The work combines atomistic tight-binding non-equilibrium Green's-function (NEGF) simulations and analytical waveguide models with experimental magnetotransport measurements on CNT fibers using pulsed magnetic fields up to 60 T.

**Model / system.** The physical systems include SWCNT, DWCNT, and TWCNT junctions and fibers. Theoretical models include an atomistic tight-binding Hamiltonian with Peierls substitution for magnetic fields and a minimal 1D atomic chain model.

**Key observables.** Electrical conductance (G), specific conductivity (normalized by linear density), magnetoresistance (MR), and transmission amplitude (T).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Nanotube diameter, chirality, overlap length, doping, perpendicular magnetic field strength, and inter-tube coupling.

**Assumptions / limitations.** Assumes current injection is primarily through outer shells in MWCNTs; neglects inelastic processes like electron-phonon scattering; assumes magnetic fields are present only in the scattering region.

**Figures summary.** Figures illustrate junction architectures (SWCNT, DWCNT, TWCNT), transport regimes in atomic chains (standing waves, gateway states), and transmission maps/spectra as a function of energy and magnetic field.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of junction-governed transport, presents theoretical models (atomic chains and waveguide analogy), details the atomistic NEGF simulations, compares these to experimental magnetotransport data in CNT fibers, and concludes with design principles for high-conductance conductors.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Miniaturized electronics require lightweight conductors that maintain high conductance under demanding conditions. CNT networks are promising candidates, but their transport is governed by inter-nanotube junctions where electron waves interfere. Controlling this interference requires understanding how junction architecture shapes transmission. We explore coherent transport through experimentally relevant junctions, from single and multiple single-walled CNT (SWCNT) contacts to double-walled CNT (DWCNT) and triple-walled CNT (TWCNT) junctions, with atomistic tight-binding non-equilibrium Green's-function calculations, also under a perpendicular magnetic field. We use analytically solvable minimal models to identify transport regimes expected for quasi-1D nanoscale junctions, and an electron-waveguide picture to interpret their CNT-specific manifestations. For single SWCNT--SWCNT junctions, high-transmission windows are set mainly by overlap length, doping and magnetic field. Gateway states can enhance conductance when some CNT subbands are gapped, and in some cases a magnetic field can restore transmission by lifting an interference blockade. In more complex architectures, added paths become selective: multi-junctions generate resonant filtering, while additional walls redistribute transmission instead of acting as independent channels. DWCNT junctions remain outer-wall dominated and SWCNT-like, whereas TWCNT junctions become genuinely multi-channel and more field-sensitive. This explains the lower, more field-sensitive conductance of multi-walled CNT (MWCNT) fibres, in accord with our ultrahigh-field measurements on SWCNT and MWCNT fibres. Ultimately, this work turns microscopic interference mechanisms into design principles for high-conductance, field-stable CNT conductors.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new GPU-accelerated numerical solver, PETASPIN_microelectrics, designed for large-scale, high-fidelity simulations of 3D polarization dynamics in ferroelectric materials. It overcomes the limitations of previous CPU-based and uniaxial models, enabling the study of complex topological textures like skyrmions and frequency-dependent hysteresis. This tool is essential for the predictive design of next-generation ferroelectric devices.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Existing CPU-based ferroelectric solvers are computationally expensive for large-scale 3D simulations and often rely on simplified uniaxial models that cannot capture complex 3D structures like polarization rotation or topological textures.

**Main result.** The development of PETASPIN_microelectrics, a fully GPU-acceler_rated, scalable solver that accurately reproduces temperature-driven phase transitions in BaTiO3 and demonstrates the stabilization of 3D hybrid skyrmions in PbTiO3/SrTiO3 bilayers.

**Method.** A GPU-native phase-field approach using CUDA C/C++ and the Thrust library, employing the finite-difference method and NVIDIA's CUDA FFT library to solve the time-dependent Ginzburg-Landau equations.

**Model / system.** The model uses the Time-Dependent Ginzburg-Landau (TDGL) formalism applied to perovskite oxides like BaTiO3, PbTiO3, and SrTiO3, incorporating Landau, gradient, and electrostatic energy contributions.

**Key observables.** Polarization vector field (magnitude and rotation), domain wall profiles, hysteresis loops, electrostatic fields, and topological textures (skyrmions/vortices).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Temperature (0-360 K), external electric field frequency, epitaxial strain parameter (rs), Landau and gradient coefficients, and background permittivity.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The system is modeled using the TDGL formalism; finite samples are assumed to be embedded in vacuum (P=0 outside); the Landau expansion is applied to prototypical perovskites.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the effect of Landau coefficients on 180-degree domain wall profiles; Figure 3(b) illustrates the transition from vortex tubes to stabilized skyrmions via strain tuning.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the computational bottleneck of existing solvers, presents the architecture and implementation of the new GPU solver, validates the solver against analytical domain wall profiles and phase transitions, and demonstrates advanced applications in strain-controlled skyrmion stabilization.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Ferroelectric materials can be used for the development of multiple device concepts combining non-volatility, small dimensions, low-power actuation, and electrical tunability. Such development demands efficient and precise design of simulation tools describing the polarization texture. However, most existing ferroelectric solvers are CPU-based and rely on simplified electrostatic treatments and reduced-dimensional representations of the polarization field. These approximations limit their ability to capture finite-size and boundary effects and restrict the range of domain structures and domain walls that can be realistically simulated. Here, we present a fully GPU (graphics processing units)-accelerated and scalable numerical solver, named PETASPIN_microelectrics, for computing the full polarization vector field of ferroelectric systems using the Ginzburg-Landau formalism. Our solver incorporates an optimized and validated calculation of the full electrostatic field and enables the parallel execution of multiple simulations. We systematically validated the solver with several benchmark problems, including phase transitions in BaTiO3 and ferroelectric domain wall profiles. Our simulations reproduce temperature-driven hysteretic phase transitions in BaTiO3. We also reproduce hysteresis loops and demonstrate stabilization of a three-dimensional hybrid skyrmion in a PbTiO3/SrTiO3 bilayer system. Our results show quantitative agreement with predictions from an analytical theory and prior experimental studies. The proposed solver provides an efficient, accurate platform for large-scale simulations of ferroelectric materials including stabilization of topological textures supporting predictive modeling for next-generation of ferroelectric device design.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper presents a new hybrid method to precisely measure the absolute superconducting penetration depth in thin films. By combining microwave resonator experiments with 3D electromagnetic simulations, the authors overcome the limitations of traditional model-dependent analytical methods. The technique is successfully demonstrated on both NbN and a-Re6Zr films, including very thin films using a unique flip-film geometry.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The difficulty of determining the absolute value of the superconducting magnetic penetration depth (lambda) from microwave resonator measurements due to model dependencies and complex geometric factors.

**Main result.** The development of a hybrid method combining microwave measurements with COMSOL Multiphysics simulations to accurately extract absolute penetration depth and superconducting gap values for NbN and a-Re6Zr films.

**Method.** A hybrid approach using finite-element electromagnetic simulations (COMSOL) to create look-up tables of resonant frequencies, which are then matched to experimental transmission (S12) data from microstrip resonators.

**Model / system.** Superconducting microstrip (stripline) resonators made of NbN and Nb3Sn thin films on MgO substrates, including a 'flip-film' geometry where a secondary sample is placed above the resonator using a Mylar dielectric spacer.

**Key observables.** Resonant frequency (fn), transmission coefficient (|S12|), phase shift, and loaded quality factor (QL).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Magnetic penetration depth (lambda), kinetic inductance (Lk), superconducting energy gap (Delta), film thickness (t), and temperature (T).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Gold-plated copper box surfaces are treated as perfect conductors; the thickness and penetration depth of the primary microstrip are assumed to be fixed for certain calculations; neglect of temperature variation of the resonator lambda in specific variants.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1: Device geometry and experimental setup (bare and flip-film). Fig 2: Transmission spectra, frequency vs mode number, and temperature dependence of 1/lambda^2 for NbN. Fig 3: Frequency shifts in flip-film configuration and simulated frequency vs lambda. Fig 4: Transmission and extracted lambda for 6 nm NbN. Fig 5/6: Simulated transmission and frequency dependence on conductivity and thickness.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the problem (absolute lambda measurement) -> Description of the microstrip resonator and flip-film experimental setup -> Presentation of the hybrid simulation-measurement method -> Validation using NbN films -> Extension and application to a-Re6Zr via flip-film geometry -> Discussion of thickness regimes and error analysis.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Superconducting microstrip resonators, which leverage kinetic inductance to probe electrodynamics, are sensitive tools for studying superconducting thin films at microwave frequencies. However, extracting the absolute superconducting penetration depth from these measurements remains challenging. In this work, we present a hybrid method to determine the absolute value of penetration depth over a wide temperature range by combining resonator measurements with finite-element electromagnetic simulations in COMSOL Multiphysics. We demonstrate this approach by extracting the penetration depth of NbN films by fabricating resonators from films of various thicknesses. Furthermore, we extend the technique to materials with lower critical temperatures by employing a flip-film geometry. By placing a sample above a NbN resonator, separated by a thin Mylar dielectric, we create a coupled structure where changes in the sample's penetration depth shift the resonant frequency. This non-destructive method provides a reliable, high-sensitivity platform for characterizing the penetration depth of diverse superconducting thin films.

</details>

**Summary.** This paper develops a method to use electron magnetic linear dichroism to map the direction of magnetic spins in antiferromagnets at the nanoscale. By using advanced simulations, the authors show that this technique can distinguish magnetic order from crystal anisotropy, providing a powerful tool for studying new magnetic phases like altermagnets.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of nanoscale vectorial reconstruction of magnetic order parameters, particularly in compensated magnetic phases like antiferromagnets and altermagnets where net magnetization vanishes.

**Main result.** Demonstration of Electron Magnetic Linear Dichroism (EMLD) as a quantitative, robust, and scalable probe for determining the magnetic spin axis with nanometer to atomic resolution.

**Method.** A unified vectorial simulation framework combining DFT-derived transition matrix elements, Mixed Dynamic Form Factor (MDFF) formalism, and dynamical diffraction theory.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on the metamagnetic alloy FeRh, examining both its antiferromagnetic (AF) and ferromagnetic (FM) phases using Transmission Electron Microscopy (TEM) and EELS.

**Key observables.** Electron Magnetic Linear Dichroism (EMLD), Electron Magnetic Circular Dichroism (EMCD), and Electron Natural Linear Dichroism (ENLD) signals.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Néel vector orientation (polar and azimuthal angles), momentum transfer (q), exchange-splitting energy, and probe convergence angle.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the absence of valence spin-orbit coupling for the simplified diagonal operator representation and utilizes the dipole approximation.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows scattering geometry and simulated EELS spectra for FM/AF phases; Figure 2 illustrates EMLD intensity maps and dependence on the Neel vector; Figure 3 details the reconstruction scheme and angular dependence; Figure 4 shows EMLD maps as a function of beam tilt.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the problem of magnetic reconstruction, presents a unified vectorial simulation framework, demonstrates the separation of dichroic signals, applies the method to FeRh, and evaluates the scalability and robustness of the EMLD probe.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We demonstrate electron magnetic linear dichroism as a quantitative probe of vectorial magnetic order parameters with nanometer resolution in transmission electron microscopy. Explicit inclusion of vectorial core-level exchange splitting into mixed dynamic form factor simulations accounting for dynamical diffraction enables direct reconstruction of the magnetic spin axis from momentum-resolved electron energy-loss spectra. The resulting dichroic signal is intrinsically separable from nonmagnetic anisotropy, exhibits a well-defined dependence on the Néel vector or magnetization orientation, and remains robust down to the atomic scale. Applied to the collinear antiferromagnetic and ferromagnetic phase of cubic FeRh, this approach allows quantitative real-space mapping of the magnetic vector. These results open a pathway to nanoscale spectroscopy and imaging of antiferromagnets and altermagnets where the generalized approach to electron dichroism provides direct access to different magnetic order parameters.

</details>

### [SC-1 Etching of Niobium and Titanium Nitride Thin Films](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28343v1)

**Authors:** Adrián Gutiérrez-Cruz, K. A. C. Rathnathilaka, Jani M. Taskinen, Tuomas Vaimala, Kestutis Grigoras, Harshad Mishra, Rishabh Upadhyay, Jorden Senior, Alberto Ronzani  
**Type:** experiment · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28343v1>

<img src="2605.28343_figures/2605.28343_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>

**Summary.** This paper presents a wet etching method using SC-1 solution as a damage-free alternative to dry etching for Nb and TiN thin films. While the process offers high selectivity and avoids plasma damage, the authors highlight challenges such as isotropic undercutting and morphology-dependent etch rates. This work is significant for the fabrication of high-quality superconducting and ferroelectric microelectronic components.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The challenge of patterning Niobium (Nb) and Titanium Nitride (TiN) thin films without the plasma-induced damage and limited selectivity associated with standard dry etching techniques.

**Main result.** Demonstration of a highly selective SC-1 wet etching alternative that avoids substrate damage, though it is subject to isotropic undercutting and multi-modal etch rates driven by film morphology.

**Method.** Wet etching using SC-1 solution (NH3, H2O2, and H2O) followed by characterization via SEM, AFM, and OpenCV-based image processing for surface area estimation.

**Model / system.** Sputtered Nb and TiN thin films on 150 mm Si wafers with a thermally grown SiO2 layer and a PECVD SiO2 hard mask.

**Key observables.** Etch rates, film morphology (roughness, grain size, columnar structure), selectivity, and effective surface area increase.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Etch temperatures (65C for Nb, 45C for TiN), SC-1 volume ratio (1:1:5), and lateral cavity size due to overetching.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The image processing approach assumes that SEM-derived contour measurements accurately represent the effective surface profile; AFM scanning was limited to specific regions to avoid tip damage.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the initial layer stack and the three stages of the etching process; Figure 3 displays cross-sectional SEM of films mid-etch; Figure S4 shows SEM-based contouring and AFM topography for surface area estimation; Figure S5 illustrates lateral cavity growth under the mask during overetching.

**Paper structure.** Introduction of the etching problem, description of the SC-1 experimental platform and method, analysis of Nb and TiN etch dynamics and morphology, quantification of surface area changes, and discussion of limitations like mask undercut.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Dry etching techniques, ubiquitous in microelectronics fabrication, often result in challenging levels of undesired collateral plasma-induced damage. In this work, we demonstrate a wet etching alternative for the patterning of niobium (Nb) and titanium nitride (TiN) thin films using the Standard Cleaning 1 (SC-1) solution. We characterize the etching process through its time-evolution dynamics, supported by scanning-electron and atomic force microscopy assessment of the etched film morphology. The results suggest etch dynamics that are linked to native oxides and film microstructure. Overall, the manageable etch rates, the safe operation and the high material selectivity are attractive for practical use in microelectronics fabrication.

</details>

<a id="paper-2605.27568"></a>
### [Solitonic Construction of Artificial Neural Networks from Nonlinear Field Theory](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27568v1)

**Authors:** R. A. C. Correa  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** disordered systems and neural networks · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27568v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27568_figures/2605.27568_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Constructive route from nonlinear field theory to a neural layer. The essential point is the direction of derivation: the neural architecture is not assumed and then relabeled, but obtained after projecting the solitonic sector of the field theory onto collective coordinates and readout variables.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27568_figures/2605.27568_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Solitonic activation profiles. The ϕ4 kink gives tanh z directly, while the normalized kink gives the logistic-type response [1 + tanh z]/2. The ReLU curve is shown only as a singular asymmetric limiting response, not as a smooth topological kink.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27568_figures/2605.27568_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 3. Schematic solitonic origin of effective weights. For massive fields, the tails of well-separated kinks typically generate exponentially suppressed overlaps. After projection, these overlap scales contribute to the effective coupling matrix and therefore to the neural weights.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 4</summary>

<img src="2605.27568_figures/2605.27568_fig4.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Learning as deformation of an effective solitonic potential. The bare collective-coordinate potential contains the intrinsic solitonic sector. A data-dependent term tilts or reshapes this energy, selecting the collective configuration whose readout best matches the supervised target.</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 5</summary>

<img src="2605.27568_figures/2605.27568_fig5.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 5. Illustrative two-input solitonic readout. The plotted surface represents a projected output ho = σ(2q1 −1.5q2 + 0.8q1q2) generated by linear and bilinear interaction terms in the effective energy. The figure is not a numerical simulation of a specific material platform; it visualizes how solitonic interaction coefficients become neural couplings and nonlinear features.</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper proposes a way to derive the structure of neural networks directly from nonlinear field theory. It shows that neurons, weights, and activation functions can be understood as physical properties of interacting solitons, providing a robust physical foundation for machine learning architectures.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The paper seeks to provide a formal, constructive derivation of artificial neural network (ANN) architectures from the underlying dynamics of nonlinear field theories, moving beyond mere analogy.

**Main result.** The author demonstrates that the fundamental components of a neural layer—activation functions, weights, and biases—emerge naturally from the collective-coordinate dynamics and interaction energy of solitons.

**Method.** The method involves a collective-coordinate reduction of a nonlinear scalar field theory, using a projection map to extract finite-dimensional computational states from the continuous field configurations.

**Model / system.** The primary model is a nonlinear scalar field theory, specifically the phi^4 kink model, focusing on the nonperturbative sector containing stable, localized solitonic solutions.

**Key observables.** The neural state (projected collective coordinate), activation function (solitonic response profile), weight matrix (interaction Hessian), and bias (external sources).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Mass scale, coupling constant, vacuum value, and the requirement of a separation of scales between internal soliton modes and collective coordinate evolution.

**Assumptions / limitations.** The construction assumes a separation of scales between microscopic field fluctuations and mesoscopic soliton dynamics, and is limited to architectures representable as reduced dynamics of localized modes.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the derivation flow from field action to neural layer; Figure 4 illustrates learning as the deformation of an effective potential; Figure 5 shows a 3D plot of a two-input solitonic readout.

**Paper structure.** The paper follows a logical progression from the general principle of field reduction to a specific phi^4 kink implementation, followed by the derivation of neural layer components, and concludes with implications for robustness and future architectures.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We present a field-theoretic construction of a class of artificial neural networks from solitonic degrees of freedom in nonlinear scalar field theory. The purpose is not to rename a standard neural layer in the language of solitons, but to start from a continuum action, restrict the theory to a nonperturbative sector containing localized stable solutions, perform a collective-coordinate reduction, and derive the neural layer as the finite-dimensional input-output map of the reduced solitonic dynamics. In this construction, the computational unit is a projected collective coordinate of a localized field configuration rather than an elementary point variable; the activation function is the solitonic response profile or scattering map; the weight matrix is the Hessian or overlap matrix of an effective interaction energy among solitons; the bias is induced by external sources, vacuum asymmetry, or boundary forcing; and depth is a discrete evolution parameter on the solitonic moduli space. We develop the construction explicitly for the \(φ^4\) kink, where the \(\tanh\) activation and the logistic sigmoid arise from the kink profile, and then derive the multilayer feedforward form from an operator-splitting approximation to collective-coordinate gradient flow. We emphasize the novelty criterion: the neural architecture is obtained only after specifying the field action, the solitonic ansatz, the moduli-space metric, the interaction functional, and the projection map. The result is a controlled route from nonlinear field theory to neural-network structure, with robustness tied to the energetic and topological stability of the solitonic sector.

</details>

### [Spin-Hall-Like Magnon Transport in a Synthetic Antiferromagnetic Skyrmion Lattice](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.27822v1)

**Authors:** Xingen Zheng, Xuejuan Liu, Zhenyu Wang, Jiyong Kang, Zhixiong Li, Hao Wu  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** other · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.27822v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.27822_figures/2605.27822_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 1. Bilayer SAF-SkL texture and its bulk magnon spectrum. (a) Spin configuration of the SAF-SkL</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 2</summary>

<img src="2605.27822_figures/2605.27822_fig2.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 2. Edge states and layer polarization in the SAF-SkL. (a) Strip magnon spectrum colored by the normalized</sub>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.27822_figures/2605.27822_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>FIG. 4. Schematic analogy between skyrmion-based lattices and Hall-type responses. (a), (b) Conventional</sub>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper explores magnon transport in a bilayer system of coupled skyrmion lattices with opposite textures. It demonstrates that while the system lacks the symmetry for a $Z_2$ topological phase, it successfully hosts layer-polarized, spin-Hall-like edge modes. This provides a new platform for studying bosonic Hall-type transport phenomena.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study investigates whether a synthetic antiferromagnetic skyrmion lattice (SAF-SkL) can realize a bosonic analogue of the spin-Hall effect and determines if its edge modes are $Z_2$-protected helical states or layer-polarized spin-Hall-like modes.

**Main result.** The researchers find that the SAF-SkL supports counterpropagating, layer-polarized edge modes that are not $Z_2$-protected due to the lack of pseudo-time-reversal symmetry, but instead function as 'spin-Hall-like' modes arising from the opposite Hall tendencies of the two layers.

**Method.** The study employs linear spin-wave theory using the Holstein-Primakoff transformation to derive a bosonic Bogoliubov-de Gennes Hamiltonian, supplemented by micromagnetic simulations (MuMax3) and symmetry analysis.

**Model / system.** A synthetic antiferromagnetic skyrmion lattice (SAF-SkL) consisting of two antiferromagnetically coupled skyrmion lattice layers with opposite magnetic textures, modeled via a spin Hamiltonian including exchange, interlayer coupling, DMI, and anisotropy.

**Key observables.** Bulk and strip magnon spectra, layer polarization ($P_L$), edge mode localization ($\langle y angle / L_y$), and the spin Chern number ($C_s$).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Interlayer antiferromagnetic exchange stiffness ($A_{af}$), magnetization ($M_s$), Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interaction ($D$), and an external out-of-plane magnetic field ($h_z$).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The analysis is based on the approximation of linear spin-wave theory and assumes a stabilized, relaxed bilayer texture from micromagnetic simulations.

**Figures summary.** Fig 1 shows the visual representation of the SAF-SkL; Fig 2 displays the strip magnon spectrum and edge mode localization; Fig 3 illustrates the evolution of the spectrum and transport properties as interlayer coupling varies.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the SAF-SkL system, presents the theoretical framework (BdG Hamiltonian and spin-wave theory), analyzes the bulk and edge spectra, performs symmetry analysis to classify the topological nature, and concludes with a unified framework for magnon Hall-type responses.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

We investigate spin-Hall-like magnon edge transport in a synthetic antiferromagnetic skyrmion lattice composed of two antiferromagnetically coupled skyrmion lattice layers with opposite magnetic textures. Based on a relaxed bilayer texture from micromagnetic simulations, we construct the bosonic Bogoliubov-de Gennes Hamiltonian within linear spin-wave theory and calculate the bulk and strip magnon spectrum. We find counterpropagating in-gap edge modes with opposite layer polarization, whose layer-resolved propagation is further confirmed by dynamical micromagnetic simulations. A symmetry analysis shows that the fully coupled system lacks the pseudo-time-reversal symmetry required for a genuine bosonic Z2 topological phase. Thus, the observed edge modes are not Z2-protected helical magnon edge states, but layer-polarized, spin-Hall-like modes originating from the opposite Hall tendencies of the two skyrmion lattice layers. These results establish synthetic antiferromagnetic skyrmion lattices as a platform for spin-Hall-like magnon transport beyond a strict bosonic Z2 classification.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Surface Contact Approximation for Magneto-Thermal Finite Element Analysis of No-Insulation HTS Coils](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28574v1)

**Authors:** Erik Schnaubelt, Louis Denis, Mariusz Wozniak, Julien Dular, Arjan Verweij  
**Type:** theory · **Category:** numerical methods · **PDF:** <https://arxiv.org/pdf/2605.28574v1>  
**Analysis basis:** full PDF text, analyzed in chunks

<details open><summary>📷 Fig 1</summary>

<img src="2605.28574_figures/2605.28574_fig1.jpg" width="500"><br>

The layered structure of the HTS CC is represented by different colors schematically. After homogenization, as shown in the right image, the different layers of the HTS CC are represented by a single homogeneous material. The volumetric contact layer is reduced to a surface contact layer using a dedicated weak formulation introduced in Section II-C. The cooling condition is applied to the electric ports marked in red.</sub>

</details>

</details>

<details><summary>📷 Fig 3</summary>

<img src="2605.28574_figures/2605.28574_fig3.jpg" width="500"><br>
<sub>Fig. 4. Absolute difference in central axial magnetic flux density (a), voltage between copper terminals (b), maximal winding temperature (c), and total AC losses (d) with respect to TSA results with tcl = 1 µm. The four figures (a) - (d) share the same legend shown in (b).</sub>

</details>

</details>

**Summary.** This paper introduces a new numerical method called Surface Contact Approximation (SCA) to simplify the simulation of no-insulation superconducting coils. By replacing a volumetric layer with a surface-based model, the method reduces computational complexity and improves numerical stability. This allows for more efficient and accurate modeling of the thermal and electromagnetic behavior in HTS applications.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** Simulating the magneto-thermal behavior of no-insulation (NI) high-temperature superconducting (HTS) coils is computationally expensive and numerically unstable when using standard volumetric thin-shell approximations.

**Main result.** The proposed Surface Contact Approximation (SCA) accurately reproduces the electromagnetic and thermal behavior of the coils while being more numerically robust, computationally efficient, and easier to implement than the existing Thin Shell Approximation (TSA).

**Method.** The authors derive a surface weak formulation (SCA) from the more general Thin Shell Approximation (TSA) by assuming negligible tangential surface currents and eddy currents within the contact layer.

**Model / system.** The study focuses on no-insulation (NI) HTS pancake coils wound from coated conductors, specifically modeling the turn-to-turn contact layers (T2TCL) using a finite element analysis (FEA) framework.

**Key observables.** Central axial magnetic flux density, voltage between electric ports, maximal winding temperature, and total AC losses.

**Important parameters / regimes.** Electric contact resistance (Rcl), thermal contact conductance (Kcl), critical current density (Ic), and virtual thickness (tcl).

**Assumptions / limitations.** Negligible tangential surface currents and eddy-current effects within the contact layer, negligible thermal mass of the contact layer, and negligible magnetic field variations within the T2TCL thickness.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the cross-section of an NI coil before and after simplification; Figure 2 displays the finite element mesh; Figure 3 compares magnetic flux, voltage, temperature, and AC loss curves between the TSA and SCA models.

**Paper structure.** The paper identifies the stability/accuracy trade-off in existing TSA methods, proposes the SCA as an alternative, derives the SCA formulation from the TSA, implements it in the FiQuS software, and validates it through numerical comparison and transient simulations.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

High-temperature superconducting (HTS) coated conductors (CCs) can be wound into no-insulation (NI) coils, in which electrical current can partially bypass local normal zones via turn-to-turn contact layers (T2TCLs). Accurate magneto-thermal simulation of such coils, therefore, requires an efficient representation of the electrical and thermal behavior of the T2TCLs. This paper introduces a magneto-thermal surface contact approximation (SCA) for finite element analysis of NI HTS coils. The formulation is derived as a special case of the more general thin shell approximation (TSA) by introducing suitable approximations such as negligible tangential surface currents and eddy-current effects inside the T2TCL. The resulting SCA formulation replaces the thin volumetric contact layer with a dedicated surface weak formulation based on the electric contact resistance and thermal contact conductance. In contrast, the TSA formulation requires the definition of electric resistivities and thermal conductivities as well as the thickness of the T2TCL. The SCA is implemented in the Pancake3D module of the free and open-source Finite Element Quench Simulator. It is verified through transient magneto-thermal simulations of a model NI pancake coil. Numerical results are compared against the established TSA formulation. The results show that the SCA accurately reproduces the relevant electromagnetic and thermal behavior. For the TSA, there is a trade-off between choosing large (potentially unphysical) thicknesses with low resistivities leading to inaccurate results, or small thicknesses with large resistivities making the linear system harder to solve, increasing the computational effort. In contrast, the SCA, thanks to using contact resistances and conductances directly without the necessity to define a thickness, is easy to use and robust.

</details>

<sub>[↑ back to top](#top)</sub>

### [Three-component superconductivity: the effect of second-order Josephson couplings](http://arxiv.org/abs/2605.28221v1)

**Summary.** This paper provides a comprehensive theoretical framework for three-component superconductivity, specifically addressing the physics of kagome superconductors. It maps out a complex phase diagram containing frustrated and phase-locked states and predicts a unique hybridized Higgs-Leggett mode as a dynamical signature of the frustrated phase.

**Why it may be interesting.** not directly relevant

<details><summary>Detailed structure</summary>

**Main problem.** The study aims to understand the stability, phase structure, and collective excitations of a three-component superconducting system driven by second-order Josephson couplings.

**Main result.** The authors identified five distinct ground states, including an 8-fold degenerate frustrated state and four 4-fold degenerate phase-locked states, and discovered a unique Higgs-Leggett mode in the frustrated regime.

**Method.** The work uses a Ginzburg-Landau theoretical framework, employing analytical derivations of ground-state solutions, Sylvester's Criterion for stability, and linear response theory for collective mode analysis.

**Model / system.** A three-component Ginzburg-Landau model representing the 3Q pair-density-wave state in vanadium-based kagome superconductors, featuring second-order Josephson-type couplings.

**Key observables.** Collective modes (Higgs-Leggett, Higgs, Leggett, and Goldstone modes), superfluid densities, and fractional magnetic flux quantization (phi_0/3).

**Important parameters / regimes.** Second-order Josephson coupling constants (eta_jk), Ginzburg-Landau coefficients (a_j, b_j), and relative phases (theta_jk).

**Assumptions / limitations.** The model assumes the physics is dominated by second-order Josephson couplings due to momentum conservation suppressing first-order coupling.

**Figures summary.** Figure 1 shows the stability domain in parameter space; Figure 2 plots relative phase solutions; Figure 3 illustrates the topological partition of the frustrated state; Figure 7 shows the evolution of collective mode eigenvalues.

**Paper structure.** The paper introduces the 3Q PDW model, derives ground-state solutions and phase boundaries using GL theory, classifies the resulting phase diagram, and concludes with a numerical analysis of collective excitation spectra.

</details>

<details><summary>Abstract</summary>

Recently, a three-component Ginzburg-Landau (GL) model compatible with the 3Q pair-density-wave state has been proposed to explain the fractional quantum magnetic resistance oscillations of period $φ_0/3 = hc/6e$ observed in vanadium-based kagome superconductors. The physics of this model is governed by second-order Josephson-type couplings, which break both time-reversal symmetry and discrete $π$-phase flip symmetry. In this work, we theoretically derive the complete set of ground-state solutions and construct a comprehensive phase diagram in the GL parameter space, characterized by analytically determined phase boundaries. We identify five distinct ground states: an 8-fold degenerate frustrated state and four 4-fold degenerate non-frustrated phase-locked states. Four of these states spontaneously break time-reversal symmetry. Numerical analysis of the collective modes reveals the emergence of a Higgs-Leggett mode unique to the frustrated region, accompanied by mode softening near the phase boundaries. Our findings provide a comprehensive theoretical framework for understanding the multifaceted physics of multicomponent superconductivity.

</details>
